Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Definition of Sawm and Obligation of Ramadan Fasting
Defines sawm and states its obligation with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of sawm, identify the ruling of Ramadan fasting, and apply the condition of knowledge of obligation in Dar al-Harb.

Core Idea
Fasting is not just hunger; it is worshipful abstention with intention from dawn to sunset.

This lesson opens the Book of Fasting in the Hanafi text 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. We begin with the precise legal definition of sawm and establish the obligation of fasting Ramadan. A critical nuance emerges for new Muslims in non-Muslim territories—understanding when ignorance excuses missed fasts.

Guided Path
1
Extract the legal definition
Identify the four components of the legal definition: abstaining from eating, drinking, and marital relations, from dawn to sunset, with intention.
Linguistic definition is broader (any abstention), but legal is specific.
2
Identify the ruling
State the ruling: fasting Ramadan is fard 'ayn (individual obligation) on every Muslim who is mukallaf.
Fard 'ayn means every accountable person must perform it.
3
List the conditions of obligation
The text mentions conditions: being Muslim, being mukallaf (sane, mature), and having knowledge of the obligation (or residing in Dar al-Islam). Women must be free of menstruation/postpartum bleeding.
Intention is a condition for validity, not obligation.
4
Analyze the Dar al-Harb exception
Understand that a person who accepts Islam in Dar al-Harb without knowing about fasting is not required to make up missed days, unlike one in Dar al-Islam.
Ignorance is an excuse in Dar al-Harb due to lack of Islamic environment.
5
Synthesize practical implications
Apply the rule: if a new Muslim in a non-Muslim country misses fasts before learning, no qada is needed. If living in a Muslim country, qada is required even if ignorant.
The key factor is residence and availability of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
الصوم : هو تركُ الأكل والشرب والوطء من الصبح إلى المغرب مع النية. وصومُ رمضان فرض على كل مسلم مكلف أداء وقضاء
[1] قوله : كتاب الصوم ؛ هو ثالث أركان الإسلام، وكان ينبغي أن يذكر تلو بحث الصلاة، لكن لما كانت الزكاة مقرونة بها في مواضع من القرآن قدم ذكرها، وقدم الصوم على الحج ؛ لكونه أهم بالنسبة إليه، حيث يفرضُ في كل سنة، والحج في العمر مرة.
[2] قوله : هو ... الخ ؛ هذا التفسير المعتبر شرعاً، ولغة : هو عبارة عن الإمساك مطلقاً).
[3] قوله : مع النية ؛ أي المعتبرة شرعاً من رجلٍ أو امرأة خالية عن حيض ونفاس.
[4] قوله : رَمَضان ؛ بفتحات اسم للشهرِ الذي بين شعبان وشوال ؛ سمي به ؛ لأنه ترمض فيه الذنوب ؛ أي تُحْرَقُ وتُحَطّ ، وقيل : إن علمه شهر رمضان.
[5] قوله : مسلم ؛ بشرط أن يكون عالماً بالوجوب، أو مقيماً بدار الإسلام، فإن أسلم الحربي ولم يهاجر إلينا، ولم يعلم بالوجوب فلا فرضيّة عليه، حتى إذا عَلِمَ لا يجب عليه قضاء ما سبق ؛ لأنّ الجهل عذر في دار الحرب لا في دار الإسلام". كذا ذكره الشرنبلالي.
(1) أي سواء أكان ما تمسك عنه . كلاماً أو فعلاً، وسواء أكان الفعل أكلاً أم شرباً أم غيرهما. ينظر : سبيل الفلاح»(ص۱۸۹). (۲) فلا بد من العلم بوجوب الصوم لمن أسلم بدار الحرب ; أما من كان بدار الإسلام فإنه يجب عليه ; إذ الكون بدار الإسلام موجب للصوم ، وإن لم يعلم بوجوبه ; إذ لا يعذر بالجهل في دار الإسلام، بخلاف من أسلم في دار الحرب ولم يعلم به ، فإنه لا يجب عليه ما لم يعلم، فإذا
The Book of Fasting. Fasting: it is refraining from eating, drinking, and marital relations from dawn to sunset with intention. And fasting of Ramadan is an obligation upon every Muslim who is accountable, both performance and make-up. [1] His statement: 'The Book of Fasting' – it is the third pillar of Islam, and it should have been mentioned following the discussion of prayer, but since zakat is paired with it in places in the Quran, he mentioned it first, and he advanced fasting over Hajj because it is more important relative to it, as it is obligatory every year, while Hajj is once in a lifetime. [2] His statement: 'it is... etc.' – this is the legally considered interpretation, and linguistically it is an expression of absolute abstention. [3] His statement: 'with intention' – meaning the legally valid intention from a man or a woman free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. [4] His statement: 'Ramadan' – with fathah (vowel marks) is the name of the month between Sha'ban and Shawwal; it was named thus because sins are burned in it, i.e., they are burnt and removed, and it is said: it is known as the month of Ramadan. [5] His statement: 'Muslim' – with the condition that he be aware of the obligation, or residing in the Abode of Islam. For if a non-Muslim in the Abode of War embraces Islam and does not migrate to us, and does not know of the obligation, then no obligation is upon him, and when he learns, he is not required to make up what preceded, because ignorance is an excuse in the Abode of War, not in the Abode of Islam. Thus mentioned by al-Sharnablali. (1) I.e., whether what he refrains from is speech or action, and whether the action is eating, drinking, or other. See: Sabil al-Falah (p. 189). (2) Knowledge of the obligation of fasting is necessary for one who embraces Islam in the Abode of War. As for one in the Abode of Islam, it is obligatory upon him, because being in the Abode of Islam necessitates fasting even if he does not know of its obligation, for ignorance is not excused in the Abode of Islam, contrary to one who embraces Islam in the Abode of War and does not know of it, for it is not obligatory upon him until he knows.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on al-Wiqayah, opening the Book of Fasting. The author first defines sawm (fasting) legally as abstaining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn (true dawn) to sunset, accompanied by intention (niyyah). This definition excludes mere dieting or hunger; it must be worshipful abstention with intent. Then he states the ruling: fasting Ramadan is fard (obligatory) upon every Muslim who is mukallaf (accountable, i.e., sane and mature), both the current year's fast (ada') and making up missed days (qada'). The commentator explains why the author placed zakat before fasting in the book: because zakat is often paired with prayer in the Quran, so it was mentioned next, and fasting is placed before Hajj because it recurs annually while Hajj is once a lifetime. He clarifies that the definition given is the valid legal one; linguistically, sawm means any abstention. The condition of intention requires it to be valid, done by a person free from ritual impurity like hayd and nifas. The month name 'Ramadan' is explained etymologically: it burns sins. A crucial fiqh point is made about the obligation on a person who accepts Islam in Dar al-Harb (non-Muslim territory). If he embraces Islam there without knowing the obligation of fasting, the missed days before his knowledge are not required to be made up, because ignorance is excused in Dar al-Harb. However, if he is in Dar al-Islam (Muslim territory), ignorance is not an excuse; he must observe and make up any missed fasts even if he was unaware. This distinction is attributed to al-Sharnablali, a later Hanafi authority.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): Legally: abstaining from eating, drinking, and marital relations from dawn to sunset with intention. Linguistically: any abstention.
النية (al-niyyah): The intention to fast, which must be present from the night (before dawn) for obligatory fasts. It is required for validity.
رمضان (Ramadan): The ninth lunar month between Sha'ban and Shawwal; named because it burns sins.
مكلف (mukallaf): One who is accountable for religious obligations: a sane, mature Muslim.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule where Islamic law is not enforced; ignorance of obligations can be an excuse for a new Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who embraces Islam in Dar al-Harb and later learns about the obligation of fasting Ramadan?
A) He must make up all previous days of Ramadan missed.
B) He does not have to make up the days prior to his knowledge.
C) He must make up only the current year's Ramadan.
D) He must fast from the day he learns onward but no qada is needed.

Key Takeaway
Sawm legally requires abstention with intention from dawn to sunset; Ramadan fasting is fard 'ayn on every mukallaf Muslim, but ignorance of the obligation is excused in Dar al-Harb.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Definition and Obligation of Fasting in Ramadan
Defines sawm and states obligation of Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of sawm and identify the conditions for its obligation, including the exception for new Muslims in dar al-harb.

Core Idea
Fasting is not just abstaining; it requires intention and is binding on every accountable Muslim, with a special exemption for those ignorant in dar al-harb.

This passage opens the Book of Fasting by defining sawm and establishing its obligation. The commentary expands on the definition, emphasizing the necessity of intention (niyyah) and explaining the exceptional case of a Muslim who embraces Islam in non-Muslim territory without knowledge of the obligation. We will unpack these elements to understand what makes a fast valid and who is required to observe it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of sawm
Note the three acts to be avoided (eating, drinking, sexual intercourse) and the time boundaries (from dawn to sunset). Also note that intention is part of the definition, separating legal fasting from mere abstention.
The definition is operative for validity.
2
Recognize the condition of intention
Understand why intention is necessary for the fast to be valid. In Hanafi, for fard fasts, intention must be made at night; for nafl, it can be made after dawn. The passage stresses 'with intention' to exclude linguistically valid but legally void abstention.
Intention differentiates ritual fasting from dieting or other abstinence.
3
Understand the obligation of Ramadan fasting
The passage states that Ramadan fasting is fard upon every Muslim who is mukallaf (adult and sane). This establishes the general rule of obligation.
Mukallaf = accountable, i.e., mentally sound and reached puberty.
4
Examine the exception for new Muslims in dar al-harb
Identify that a person who converts in non-Muslim territory and is unaware of the obligation is not required to fast or make up past days. Ignorance is an excuse in dar al-harb, unlike in dar al-Islam.
This exception is based on inability to know the obligation.
5
Synthesize the practical consequence
Combine the elements: for most Muslims, fasting Ramadan is obligatory; for a new convert in dar al-harb, obligation begins only upon learning, and no qada is required for past ignorance.
Thus, the rule is knowledge-based, not automatic upon conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
الصوم : هو تركُ الأكل والشرب والوطء من الصبح إلى المغرب مع النية. وصومُ رمضان فرض على كل مسلم مكلف أداء وقضاء
[١]قوله : كتاب الصوم ؛ هو ثالث أركان الإسلام، وكان ينبغي أن يذكر تلو بحث الصلاة، لكن لما كانت الزكاة مقرونة بها في مواضع من القرآن قدم ذكرها، وقدم الصوم على الحج ؛ لكونه أهم بالنسبة إليه، حيث يفرضُ في كل سنة، والحج في العمر مرة.
[۲] قوله : هو ... الخ ؛ هذا التفسير المعتبر شرعاً، ولغة : هو عبارة عن الإمساك مطلقا).
(۳) قوله : مع النية ؛ أي المعتبرة شرعاً من رجلٍ أو امرأة خالية عن حيض ونفاس.
[٤] قوله : رَمَضان ؛ بفتحات اسم للشهرِ الذي بين شعبان وشوال ؛ سمي به ؛ لأنه ترمض فيه الذنوب ؛ أي تُحْرَقُ وتُحَطّ ، وقيل : إن علمه شهر رمضان.
(٥) قوله : مسلم ؛ بشرط أن يكون عالماً بالوجوب، أو مقيماً بدار الإسلام، فإن أسلم الحربي ولم يهاجر إلينا، ولم يعلم بالوجوب فلا فرضيّة عليه، حتى إذا عَلِمَ لا يجب عليه قضاء ما سبق ؛ لأنّ الجهل عذر في دار الحرب لا في دار الإسلام. كذا ذكره الشرنبلالي.
(1) أي سواء أكان ما تمسك عنه . كلاماً أو فعلاً، وسواء أكان الفعل أكلاً أم شرباً أم غيرهما. ينظر : سبيل الفلاح»(ص۱۸۹).
(۲) فلا بد من العلم بوجوب الصوم لمن أسلم بدار الحرب ؛ أما من كان بدار الإسلام فإنه يجب عليه ؛ إذ الكون بدار الإسلام موجب للصوم ، وإن لم يعلم بوجوبه ؛ إذ لا يعذر بالجهل في دار الإسلام، بخلاف من أسلم في دار الحرب ولم يعلم به ، فإنه لا يجب عليه ما لم يعلم، فإذا علم ليس عليه قضاء ما مضى ؛ إذ لا تكليف بدون العلم ثمة للعذر بالجهل، وإنما يحصل له العلم الموجب بإخبار رجلين أو رجل وامرأتين مستورين، أو واحد عدل. ينظر: «فتح القدير» (۲ : ۳۰۲)، و«الدر المختار ورد المحتار (۲) : ۸۰ - (۸۱) ، و«الهدية العلائية»(ص 151 - ١٥٢)، وغيرها.
The Book of Fasting. Fasting is: refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn until sunset with intention. And the fast of Ramadan is fard upon every Muslim who is mukallaf, both performance and makeup. [1] He said: The Book of Fasting; it is the third pillar of Islam, and it should have been mentioned after the discussion of prayer, but because zakat is coupled with it in several places in the Quran, its mention was brought forward, and fasting was placed before Hajj because it is more important relative to it, as it is obligated every year while Hajj is once in a lifetime. [2] He said: 'it is'... etc. This is the legally valid interpretation. Linguistically, it is absolute abstention. [3] He said: 'with intention', i.e., the legally valid intention from a man or a woman free from menstruation and post-natal bleeding. [4] He said: 'Ramadan', with fatha, is the name of the month between Sha'ban and Shawwal; it is so named because it burns sins, i.e., incinerates and removes them. It is said that it is a proper noun for the month of Ramadan. [5] He said: 'Muslim' with the condition that he be aware of the obligation, or resident in Dar al-Islam. If a Harbi (non-Muslim from dar al-harb) becomes Muslim and does not migrate to us, and is not aware of the obligation, then no obligation is upon him. Even when he learns, he is not required to make up the past, because ignorance is an excuse in dar al-harb but not in dar al-Islam. This is as mentioned by al-Sharnablali. (1) i.e., whether what he refrains from is speech or action, and whether the action is eating, drinking, or otherwise. Refer to Sabil al-Falah p. 189. (2) Knowledge of the obligation of fasting is necessary for one who becomes Muslim in dar al-harb. As for one in dar al-Islam, it is obligatory upon him, because being in dar al-Islam necessitates fasting even if he is not aware of its obligation, as ignorance is not excused in dar al-Islam. Unlike one who becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and is unaware, it is not obligatory upon him until he knows. When he knows, he is not required to make up what passed, because there is no obligation without knowledge there due to the excuse of ignorance. And the knowledge that obligates is attained by the information of two men, or a man and two women who are trustworthy, or one just person. Refer to Fath al-Qadir (2:302), al-Durr al-Mukhtar wa Radd al-Muhtar (2:80-81), al-Hadiyya al-Ala'iyya (pp. 151-152), and others.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Fasting in the commentary. It first defines sawm (fasting) legally: refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from true dawn (subh sadiq) to sunset (maghrib) with intention. The definition excludes mere abstention without intention, which is linguistically called imsak but not legally considered fasting. The author then states the ruling: fasting Ramadan is fard (obligatory) upon every Muslim who is mukallaf (accountable, i.e., adult and sane), both for performing it on time (ada') and for makeup (qada') if missed. The commentary explains the placement of the book after zakat and before hajj due to practical priorities. The condition of intention (niyyah) is emphasized: it is required from the start, and a woman's intention is valid only when free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. The name 'Ramadan' is explained as burning sins. A key point is the condition of being Muslim with awareness of obligation: a person who embraces Islam in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory) without knowledge of the obligation is not required to fast or make up past days, because ignorance is an excuse there, unlike in dar al-Islam where the obligation applies regardless of knowledge. The commentary cites authorities on how knowledge is established: by testimony of two men, or one man and two women of reliable character, or one upright person.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): Legal definition: refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention, a condition for the validity of fasting, must be made before dawn for fard fasts.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory not under Islamic rule where Muslims are not the ruling authority and Islamic law is not enforced.
مكلف (mukallaf): Legally accountable person: a sane adult Muslim who has reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a person who becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb and does not know about the obligation of fasting Ramadan?
A) He must fast the entire month of Ramadan and make up any days that passed before his knowledge.
B) He is not required to fast until he learns of the obligation, and when he learns he does not need to make up past days.
C) He must fast the days after conversion but must make up all previous days back to his conversion.
D) He is not required to fast at all because he lives in Dar al-Harb.

Key Takeaway
Fasting Ramadan is fard 'ayn on every mukallaf Muslim, but a new convert in dar al-harb is exempt from makeup until after learning, because ignorance is an excuse there.

Sawm — Fasting

The Hanafi Distinction Between Fard and Wajib as Applied to Nadhr and Kaffarah Fasts
States ruling of vow and expiation fasts and distinguishes fard from wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib in terms of evidentiary strength and consequence of denial, and identify the two opinions within the Hanafi school regarding the fasts of nadhr and kaffarah.

Core Idea
Fard is proven by definitive evidence and its denial is disbelief; wajib is proven by speculative evidence and its denial is not.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: are the fasts of vows (nadhr) and expiations (kaffarah) properly classified as fard or wajib? While both categories are binding in practice, the Hanafi school distinguishes them based on the strength of their evidence and the consequence of denial. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Hanafi epistemological hierarchy and its impact on daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling in the Matn
The base text (matn) states: 'Wa sawm al-nadhr wa al-kaffarah wajib.' This is the starting point. Note that the author of the matn clearly classifies these fasts as wajib, not fard.
The matn is from Al-Wiqayah, a foundational Hanafi text.
2
Understand the Fard/Wajib Distinction
The commentary explains the Hanafi definition: fard is established by qat'i (definitive) evidence (e.g., mutawatir hadith, clear Quranic text), while wajib is established by zanni (speculative) evidence (e.g., ahad hadith, interpreted verses, or general texts that have been specified).
Both are equally binding in practice, but denying fard constitutes disbelief, whereas denying wajib does not.
3
Locate the Disagreement
The passage lists two opinions: (1) nadhr and kaffarah fasts are wajib (chosen by the matn author, al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, etc.) and (2) they are fard (preferred by the commentator, al-Sharnablali, etc.).
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute, not a cross-madhhab one.
4
Analyze the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
Determine what difference it makes whether these fasts are considered fard or wajib. The key practical difference is the consequence of denial: a person who denies fard becomes a disbeliever (kafir), while one who denies wajib is sinful but remains a Muslim.
The actual obligation to fast is the same in either case.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi (Relied-Upon) Position
The passage lists more authorities for the wajib view, and the matn itself states wajib. Therefore, the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that these fasts are wajib, not fard.
This is important for fatwa and practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصوم النذر والكفارة واجب
[1]قوله : وصوم النذر ؛ مطلقاً كان أو معيناً، وهو إيجاب ما ليس بواجب شرعاً، كما إذا قال : الله علي صومٌ أو صوم يوم الاثنين، أو إن قضى الله حاجتي فعلي صوم كذا.
[٢] قوله : والكفارة ؛ ككفارة الظهار، وكفارة اليمين وكفارة القتل ونحو ذلك.
[٣]قوله : واجب؛ الفرقُ بينه وبين الفرض أنّ ما ثبت لزومه بدليل قطعي لا شبهة فيه فهو فرض، وما ثبت لزومه بدليل ظني كأخبار الآحاد والآيات المؤولة، والعام المخصوص منه البعض فهو واجب وهما متساويان عملاً، حيث يعاقب تاركهما، ويكفر منكر الأول لا الثاني، وتفصيل ذلك ليطلب من كتب الأصول.
(۱) اختلف في صوم النذر على قولين : الأول: واجب، وهو ما اختاره المصنف، وصاحب الهداية) (۱): ۱۱۸)، و«الكنز»(ص)، و«المختار» (١: ١٦١)، و«الفتح» (٢: ۲۳۵)، و(الايضاح»(ق۳۰/ب)، و«الملتقى (ص) (۳۵) ، والتنوير) (۲ : ۸۲)، غيرهم. والثاني : فرض، وهو ما رجحه الشارح، و«المواهب (ق١/٥٦)، والشرنبلالي في «غنية ذوي الأحكام»(۱ : ۱۹۷)، وغيرهم.
(۲) اختلف في صوم الكفارات على قولين : الأول : واجب، وهو اختيار المصنف، وصاحب الهداية) (۱) : ۱۱۸)، و«المختار»(۱ : ١٦١)، و((الايضاح (ق) ۳۰ /ب) ، و (الملتقى» (ص ۳۵) ، و (رد المحتار»(۲ : ۸۲)، وغيرهم. والثاني : فرض، وهو اختيار الشارح، وصاحب الفتح (۲): ٢٣٥)، و(الغرر»(۱ : ۱۹۷)، و«المواهب ( ق ٥٦ /أ) ، و التنوير ) (۲) (۸۲) ، و(الدر المختار» (۲ : ۸۲)، وغيرهم. وأدلة كل طرف مبسوطة في الكتب، وسيأتي من صدر الشريعة ذكر دليل فرضية ذلك، ويوجد غيره من الأدلة ليس المقام مقام بسطها.
And the fast of nadhr (vow) and kaffarah (expiation) is wajib. [1] He said: And the fast of nadhr, whether absolute or specific. It is making obligatory something that is not legally obligatory, such as when one says: 'Upon me is a fast' or 'fast on Monday' or 'if Allah fulfills my need, then upon me is such-and-such fast.' [2] He said: And kaffarah, such as the kaffarah of zihar, the kaffarah of an oath, and the kaffarah of killing, and the like. [3] He said: 'wajib'; the difference between it and fard is that what is established as binding by definitive evidence with no doubt is fard, and what is established as binding by speculative evidence, such as ahadith (single-narrator reports), interpreted verses, and general texts that are partially specified, is wajib. And they are equal in practice: the one who abandons either is punished, but the denier of the first (fard) becomes a disbeliever, not the second. Detail is to be sought from books of usul. (1) There are two opinions concerning the fast of nadhr: the first is that it is wajib, which is the choice of the author, the author of al-Hidayah (1:118), al-Kanz, al-Mukhtar (1:161), al-Fath (2:235), al-Idah (fol. 30b), al-Multaqa (p. 35), al-Tanwir (2:82), and others. The second is that it is fard, which is preferred by the commentator, al-Mawahib (fol. 1/56), al-Sharnablali in Ghunyat Dhawi al-Ahkam (1:197), and others. (2) There are two opinions concerning the fast of kaffarat: the first is that it is wajib, which is the choice of the author, the author of al-Hidayah (1:118), al-Mukhtar (1:161), al-Idah (fol. 30b), al-Multaqa (p. 35), Radd al-Muhtar (2:82), and others. The second is that it is fard, which is the choice of the commentator, the author of al-Fath (2:235), al-Ghurar (1:197), al-Mawahib (fol. 56a), al-Tanwir (2:82), al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:82), and others. The proofs of each side are detailed in the books, and it will come from Sadr al-Shari'a the mention of the proof of its fardiyya, and other proofs exist; this is not the place to elaborate.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of fasting that is not Ramadan, specifically fasts from vows (nadhr) and expiations (kaffarah). It states that these are wajib. The commentary then explains the nature of nadhr (making obligatory something not originally obligatory) and gives examples. For kaffarah, it mentions common expiations. The key point is the distinction between fard and wajib: fard is proven by qat'i (definitive) evidence such as mutawatir or Quran, while wajib is proven by zanni (speculative) evidence like ahad hadith. Practically both are binding (abandonment is sin, punishable), but denying fard is kufr, while denying wajib is not. The passage then records an intra-Hanafi disagreement: some Hanafi authorities consider nadhr and kaffarah fasts as fard (like the commentator al-Sharnablali and others), while the text (matn) and many others consider them wajib. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to the commentary is that they are wajib, as indicated by the author's choice.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow: making obligatory upon oneself an act that is not originally obligatory, such as fasting a specific day.
واجب (wajib): Obligatory, but established by speculative evidence; its denial does not constitute disbelief, but abandoning it is sinful.
فرض (fard): Obligatory, established by definitive evidence (qat'i); its denial is disbelief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in the passage, what is the key difference between fard and wajib?
A) Fard is proven by definitive evidence and its denial is disbelief; wajib is proven by speculative evidence and its denial is not.
B) Fard is for Ramadan only, wajib for other fasts.
C) Fard is more rewardable than wajib.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the fasts of nadhr and kaffarah are classified as wajib, not fard, meaning their obligation is based on speculative evidence, and denying them does not amount to disbelief.

The Vowed Fast: Fard or Wajib?
Clarifying the distinction between fard and wajib in different types of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib in the context of vowed fasting and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute regarding its classification.

Core Idea
A vowed fast is wajib, not fard, because its proof lacks the tawatur required to make its denial kufr.

Fasting in Islam has different levels of obligation. While Ramadan is unanimously fard—denial of which is disbelief—other obligatory fasts, such as vowing or expiation, raise a nuance: are they fard or wajib? This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi debate from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, where the commentator challenges the mainstream view that the vowed fast is wajib, arguing instead that ijma should make it fard. We will trace the proof, the objection, and the decisive response that settled the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the mainstream Hanafi ruling on the vowed fast (nadhr) as found in al-Hidayah and later relied-upon works. Note the ruling for Ramadan for contrast.
Ramadan is fard; nadhr is wajib. The difference hinges on proof strength.
2
Identify the Opponent and Their View
Locate the objection raised by the commentator of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah (the 'I say' section). He argues that since ijma establishes the binding of nadhr, and ijma is qat'i, the ruling should be fard.
The commentator uses the same principle—consensus—to claim the opposite conclusion.
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Trace the opponent's dalil: the Qur'anic verse on vows (general and specified) plus ijma. Explain why they think ijma elevates the ruling to fard.
They acknowledge the verse is zanni, but argue ijma is definitive, so the combination yields fard.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Response
Examine the response from the author of al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurur. He distinguishes between ijma transmitted by tawatur (yields fard) and ijma transmitted by shuhra/ahad (yields wajib). Since the ijma on nadhr is not tawatur, it remains wajib.
This refines the concept of ijma: not all ijma is equally binding in creedal matters.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
State the practical difference: denying the obligation of a vowed fast does not make one a disbeliever, but it is still sinful to reject it.
The dispute does not affect the obligation to fast when vowed, only the theological weight.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explaining the levels of obligation in fasting. It begins by stating that Ramadan is fard (obligation) based on a definitive Qur'anic text and consensus. Denial of this is kufr. The vowed fast (nadhr) is classified as wajib, not fard, because the verse commanding fulfillment of vows (Surah al-Hajj, 29) is general and has been specified by exceptions (e.g., vows to sin, or non-obligatory acts like wudu). This specification renders the transmission (thubut) of the obligation speculative (zanni), so the ruling is wajib rather than fard. The commentator then objects: if consensus (ijma) establishes the binding nature of a vow, and consensus is a definitive proof, then the vowed fast should be fard. However, the response from the author of al-Durar clarifies that for a ruling to be fard in the sense that its denier becomes a kafir, the ijma must be transmitted via tawatur (mass transmission), like Ramadan. Since the ijma on the vowed fast is only known via shuhra (well-known) or ahad (isolated) transmission, it remains wajib. This intra-Hanafi dispute highlights the delicate relationship between textual proof and consensus in determining the strength of an obligation.

Key Terms
فريضة (fard): An obligation established by a definitive proof (qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalala); denial of which constitutes disbelief.
وجوب (wujub): An obligation established by a speculative proof (zanni al-thubut or al-dalala); denial is sinful but not disbelief.
المنذور (al-mundhoor): The vowed act; here specifically the vowed fast, which is a type of fasting that becomes obligatory upon making a vow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the Prophet (ﷺ) discouraged vowing
D) Because there is no consensus on vowed fasting

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies the vowed fast as wajib because its proof is zanni, and only tawatur-grade consensus would elevate it to fard.

The Binding Nature of Vows: Fard or Wajib?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether vowed acts are fard or wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vowed acts, distinguish between fard and wajib based on evidence transmission, and apply this distinction to real cases.

Core Idea
A vowed fast is wajib, not fard, because the evidence is zanni—only tawatur consensus establishes fard.

The passage before you addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: whether vowed and expiatory fasts are fard (requiring tawatur evidence) or wajib (based on zanni evidence). Some scholars argued that consensus on their obligation makes them fard. The majority, however, maintained that because the consensus transmission is only ahad or shuhra, the obligation remains at the level of wujub. Understanding this distinction is key to mastering the Hanafi hierarchy of certainty in legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act under dispute
Note the specific acts: vowed fasts (sawm al-nadhr) and expiatory fasts (sawm al-kaffarah). These are the acts being classified as either fard or wajib.
2
Recognize the two possible categories
Recall that in Hanafi usul, fard is an obligation established by qat'i evidence (denial = kufr), while wajib is established by zanni evidence (denial = sin, but not kufr).
fard: tawatur evidence; wajib: ahad/shuhra evidence.
3
Examine the primary evidence: the verse
The verse 'and let them fulfill their vows' (22:29) is general (amm) but has been specified in some contexts (takhsis). This specification makes the meaning (dalala) conjectural (zanni), even though the transmission (thubut) is certain as Quran.
Amm makhsus becomes zanni in Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the transmission of consensus
The consensus (ijma') on the obligation of these fasts has been transmitted through widespread (shuhra) or solitary (ahad) chains, not through mass transmission (tawatur). In the Hanafi school, only tawatur consensus yields qat'i evidence and thus establishes fard.
Ijma' must be mutawatir for fard; otherwise, it gives zanni evidence.
5
Apply the Hanafi principle
Since both the verse's meaning and the consensus transmission are zanni, the resulting obligation is at the level of wujub, not fard. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that vowed fasts and expiatory fasts are wajib.
The thamarah al-khilaf: denial of wajib is sinful but not kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : المنذور " إذا كان من العبادات المقصودة كالصلاة والصوم والحج، ونحو ذلك، فلزومه ثابت بالإجماع فيكون قطعي الثبوت، وإن كان سند الإجماع ظنياً، وهو العام" المخصوص البعض، فينبغي أن يكون فرضاً، وكذا صومُ الكفارات ؛ لأنَّ ثبوته بنص قطعي مؤيد بالإجماع.
ومنها : النذرُ بعبادات غير مقصودة كالوضوء والغسل.
ومنها : النذرُ بما ليس من جنسه واجب بإيجاب الله الله كعيادة المريض وصلاة الجنازة، ونحو ذلك.
فإنّ أمثال هذه النذور لا يجب الوفاء بها، عُلم ذلك بدليل آخر، وإنما اللازم إيفاء نذر طاعة مقصودة من جنسها واجب، وفي المقام تفصيل ليس هذا موضعه، وليطلب من رسالتي : «السعي المشكور في ردّ المذهب المأثور» التي ألفتها رداً على من أنكر شرعيّة زيارة قبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، ولما ثبت أنه عام مخصوص البعض، صار ظنياً، فما ثبت لزومه به يكون واجبا لا فرض

I say: The vowed act, if it is among the intended acts of worship like prayer, fasting, Hajj, and the like, its binding nature is established by consensus, so it is definitively established, even though the basis of consensus is conjectural, and it is a general text that is specified in part, so it should be fard. Similarly, the fast of expiation; because its establishment is by a definitive text supported by consensus. And among them: vows for non-intended acts of worship like wudu and ghusl. And among them: vows for that which is not obligatory by Allah's command, like visiting the sick and funeral prayer, and the like. Indeed, such vows are not obligatory to fulfill, known by another evidence. Rather, what is necessary is the fulfillment of a vow of an intended act of worship that is of the type that is obligatory. There is detail in this matter which is not its place; let it be sought from my treatise: 'al-Sa'y al-Mashkur fi Radd al-Mahdhab al-Ma'thur' which I authored in refutation of those who deny the legality of visiting the grave of the Prophet (peace be upon him). And since it is established that it is a general text specified in part, it became conjectural, so what is established as binding by it is wajib, not fard.

[1] His saying: 'I say' is an objection to what was chosen in al-Hidayah and followed by the author.
[2] His saying: 'the vowed act... etc.'; its summary: that the verse, although it became conjectural, consensus has been established on the binding nature of the vowed fast and the expiatory fast, and what is established by consensus is fard. And the author of Durar Sharh al-Ghurar responded to him: that what is meant by fard here is doctrinal fard whose denier becomes a disbeliever, and fard in this sense is not established by absolute consensus, but by consensus on fard transmitted by tawatur, as in the fast of Ramadan. Since the transmission of consensus on its being fard by tawatur is not established here, it remains in the rank of wujub; for consensus transmitted by widespread (shuhra) or ahad (solitary) routes establishes wujub, not fard.
(3) His saying: 'the general'; it is His saying: (And let them fulfill their vows).
[4] His saying: 'by a text'; it is the text that occurred in the chapter on expiations.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the legal classification of vowed acts of worship and expiatory fasts—whether they are fard or wajib. The commentator begins by asserting that vows for prescribed acts are binding by consensus, and since the consensus is based on a general verse (Surah al-Hajj, 29) that has been specified in some parts, the binding is definitive, so the vowed act should be fard. He then distinguishes between vows for intended acts (like prayer, fasting) and non-intended acts (like wudu, ghusl) or acts not originally obligatory (like visiting the sick). The latter are not binding. He concludes that the general text being specific makes it conjectural, so the ruling is wajib, not fard.

The footnotes clarify: The first note identifies this as an objection to al-Hidayah. The second summarizes the argument that despite the verse's conjectural nature, consensus establishes the binding nature of vowed and expiatory fasts, making them fard. The response from the author of Durar Sharh al-Ghurar distinguishes doctrinal fard (denial entails disbelief) from general obligation; fard requires consensus transmitted by tawatur (massively recurrent), which is absent here, so it remains wajib. The third footnote identifies the general text (the verse). The fourth identifies the textual basis for expiations.

The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that vowed and expiatory fasts are wajib, not fard, as held by the majority of Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow: a pledge to perform an act of worship for Allah's sake.
فرض (fard): An obligation established by definitive (qat'i) evidence; denial entails kufr.
واجب (wajib): An obligation established by conjectural (zanni) evidence; denial is sinful but not kufr.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahid scholars of the ummah on a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a vowed fast (sawm al-nadhr) is classified as:
A) Fard, because it is established by consensus.
B) Wajib, because the consensus is not transmitted by tawatur.
C) Sunnah, because it is not obligatory.
D) Mubah, because it is optional.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, vowed fasts are wajib, not fard, because the evidence does not reach the level of tawatur required to establish fard.

Time for Intention for Ramadan and Specified Vowed Fasts
Ruling on the validity of fasting with intention from night until mid-morning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the valid time for intention for Ramadan and specified vowed fasts according to the Hanafi school and identify the exception near al-dahwa al-kubra.

Core Idea
For Ramadan and specified vowed fasts, intention is valid from night until just before midday, but not at the exact midpoint.

This lesson addresses a key Hanafi ruling that provides ease in fasting. Unlike the Shafi'i school which requires intention before Fajr, the Hanafi school allows an extended window until the greater forenoon. We will examine the precise time frame and the exception near the zenith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is fasting (sawm) for two specific types: Ramadan and specified vowed fasts (nadhr mu'ayyan). The ruling: these fasts are valid (sahih) if intention is made from night until al-dahwa al-kubra.
Specified vowed fasts are those where a particular day is vowed, e.g., 'I vow to fast the coming Thursday'.
2
Define the time window
Night begins after Maghrib (or specifically after the previous fast ends). The end time is 'al-dahwa al-kubra'—the greater forenoon, approximately the midpoint between dawn and sunset (around local noon). It is actually a bit before true noon, when the sun is at its zenith but just before it begins to decline.
The exact calculation of al-dahwa al-kubra depends on the length of the shar'i day.
3
Note the exception near the deadline
The most correct view (al-asahh) is that intention is valid up to but not at the exact moment of al-dahwa al-kubra. If someone intends exactly at the midpoint, the fast is not valid. Some earlier Hanafi scholars allowed it even at that moment, but the relied-upon opinion is the restrictive one.
This reflects a cautious approach to ensure the fast is clearly in effect after the intention.
4
Understand the rationale for leniency
This leniency (taysir) is specific to Ramadan and specified vowed fasts because of their special status. It is not extended to voluntary fasts or unspecified vowed fasts, which require intention from the night.
Hanafi school distinguishes 'mu'ayyan' (specified) from 'ghayr mu'ayyan' (unspecified) vows.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school requires intention for any fast (including Ramadan) to be made before Fajr. This shows the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the schools: a person who wakes up after Fajr without intention would have a valid fast in the Hanafi school but invalid in the Shafi'i.
This difference highlights the Hanafi focus on necessity and ease.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the time for making the intention (niyyah) for fasting Ramadan and for a specified vowed fast (e.g., fasting on a specific day like Thursday). The ruling states that the intention is valid if made from the night (i.e., after maghrib of the previous day) until the 'greater forenoon' (al-dahwa al-kubra), which is approximately the midpoint of the day according to the shar'i day. However, the most correct opinion (al-asahh) is that it is not valid at the exact moment of the dahwa itself.

The footnotes then comment on the terminology used by the author of al-Hidayah, who called the vowed fast 'wajib' instead of 'fard'. Some interpret this as meaning 'fard' by metaphorical extension, but the commentator strongly criticizes this interpretation, pointing out that in al-Hidayah's own wording, 'wajib' is contrasted with 'faridah' (prescribed obligation), so it cannot be equated with fard in that context. The final footnote (incomplete) begins to summarize that for Ramadan and specified vowed fasts, the condition of intention is not required throughout the whole day? It seems cut off.

The relied-upon ruling for fasting Ramadan and specified vowed fasts is that intention can be made from night until just before the dahwa al-kubra. The Hanafi school allows this leniency, contrary to other schools that require intention before Fajr.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention: the resolve in the heart to perform an act of worship; for fasting, it is required to distinguish the type of fast.
ضحوة كبرى (dahwa al-kubra): The greater forenoon: approximately the midpoint of the shar'i day (from true dawn to sunset). It is the time when the sun is at its zenith, a bit before noon.
نذر معين (nadhr mu'ayyan): A specified vow: a vow to fast on a particular day or days (e.g., 'I vow to fast on the first Thursday of this month').

The Validity of Intention for Ramadan and Specified Vows
Ruling with proof on intention timing for obligatory fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the timing of intention for Ramadan and specific vows, explain the condition of 'most of the day', and justify the ruling using the Ashura hadith.

Core Idea
Intention for Ramadan and specified vows is valid from night until legal midday, not at it—a unique Hanafi position.

The Hanafi school holds a distinctive view that the intention for obligatory fasts can be made during the day, up to al-dahwah al-kubra (legal midday). This lesson unpacks the ruling, its conditions, and the key evidence from the hadith of Ashura that supports it. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the definition of the legal day and the precise cutoff time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the passage: 'ويصح صوم رمضان ، والنذر المعين بنية من الليل إلى الضحوة الكبرى ، لا عندها في الأصح'. Identify that intention is valid from night until al-dahwah al-kubra, but not at that time.
2
Define the cutoff time
The passage defines al-dahwah al-kubra as the midpoint of the legal day (from true dawn to sunset). Understand that this is not the same as the customary day (sunrise to sunset).
The legal day starts at fajr sadiq, not at sunrise.
3
Assess the condition of 'most of the day'
The intention must be present in most of the day, meaning the remaining time after intention should be more than the time already passed. This ensures the fast is predominantly intended.
The principle: 'most has the ruling of the whole' (للأكثر حكم الكل).
4
Examine the proof
The Ashura hadith shows the Prophet allowed fasting the remainder of the day for obligatory fasts. Since Ashura was obligatory before Ramadan, this ruling extends to Ramadan by analogy.
Hadith: 'Whoever has eaten, let him fast the remainder of his day...' (Bukhari and Muslim).

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the time limit for the intention (niyyah) for fasting in Ramadan and for specific vows (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan). The main ruling is that the intention can be made from the night (i.e., after sunset of the previous day) until al-dahwah al-kubra, which is defined as the midpoint of the legal day (from true dawn to sunset). However, the intention is not valid at al-dahwah al-kubra itself. The author of al-Hidayah used the term 'wajib' for the vowed fast, and the commentator clarifies that this could mean 'fard' (obligatory) because the vowed fast is obligatory by consensus, but the term 'wajib' is used due to the speculative nature of the consensus evidence. The text then explains the legal definition of day and confirms that the intention must be present for most of the day, specifically before the midpoint. It cites a hadith about the Day of Ashura, which was originally obligatory before Ramadan was made obligatory, to support the permissibility of making intention during the day for Ramadan. The hadith shows that those who had eaten were told to fast the rest of the day, implying a valid intention after the day had started. This establishes the precedent that intention after dawn is valid for obligatory fasts, by analogy from Ashura to Ramadan. The commentator also notes that the term 'shar'i' in the text means legally defined, as opposed to the customary day which starts at sunrise. Finally, it states that the 'greater forenoon' is exactly the legal midday, so the texts from al-Jami' al-Saghir and the main text are consistent.

Key Terms
الضحوة الكبرى (al-dahwah al-kubra): The midpoint of the legal day, i.e., half of the time from true dawn (fajr sadiq) to sunset.
النذر المعين (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan): A vow to fast on a specific day (e.g., 'I vow to fast on Thursday').
نية (niyyah): The legal intention to perform the fast, which must be made in the heart; it is a condition for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits intention for Ramadan and specified vows from night until legal midday (al-dahwah al-kubra), based on the Ashura hadith and the principle that most of the day has the ruling of the whole.

Validity of Ramadan Fast with Various Intentions
Explains validity of Ramadan fast with various intentions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of the Ramadan fast with absolute, supererogatory, and other obligatory intentions, and explain the reasoning based on the divine designation of Ramadan.

Core Idea
Ramadan's divine designation overrides the servant's specific intention, making absolute or supererogatory intention sufficient for validity.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, we explore a distinctive Hanafi ruling: the Ramadan fast is valid even if the fasting person did not explicitly intend Ramadan. The author presents the ruling from the matn, addresses an apparent conflict with the famous hadith 'Actions are by intentions', and resolves it using the principle that Ramadan is divinely designated for obligation. Understanding this ruling reveals how the Hanafi school balances textual evidence with its own legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of intention discussed
The passage examines three cases: 1) absolute intention (e.g., 'I intend to fast'), 2) intention of a supererogatory (nafl) fast, and 3) intention of another obligatory fast (e.g., kaffarah or nadhr). For each, state the ruling.
The ruling for absolute and supererogatory intention is straightforward: the Ramadan fast is valid. For another obligatory intention, there is an exception for travelers and sick persons.
2
Understand the objection from the hadith
The text raises a discussion: the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' seems to say that one gets what one intends. If someone intends a nafl fast, how can that day count as Ramadan? Recognize this as a conflict between explicit textual evidence and the school's ruling.
The author notes that absolute intention is clearly valid; the difficulty is with supererogatory intention.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: divine determination
The Hanafi resolution is that Ramadan is divinely determined (ta'ayyan min Allah) for the obligatory fast. This divine determination is superior to any determination by the servant. Therefore, if the servant intends something else, the extra specification is nullified, and the absolute fast remains, which fulfills the Ramadan obligation.
The response uses the principle that the divine designation overrides human intention unless the human has a valid excuse that removes the obligation (e.g., travel or sickness).
4
Apply the exception for traveler or sick person
The passage states that if a traveler or sick person intends another obligatory fast, it counts for that other intention, not for Ramadan. This is because such persons are not obligated to fast Ramadan, so Ramadan in their case is like an ordinary day. The time is occupied with the more pressing obligation.
There is an internal Hanafi difference for whether a traveler/sick person intending a supererogatory fast results in a supererogatory or Ramadan fast. The relied-upon view (from al-Siraj) is that it counts as supererogatory.
5
Note the ruling for specified vows
If one has vowed to fast a specific day (nadhr mu'ayyan) and then intends another obligatory fast on that same day, the other obligation takes precedence. This is because the determination of the day came from the servant (through the vow), so he can nullify it.
This contrasts with Ramadan, where the determination is from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تاب الصوم / تعريفه ونيته وأنواعه وهلاله
وبنية مطلقة ، أو بنية نفل
وفي «مختصر القدوري» : إلى الزّوال. والأوّل أصح

قوله : وفي «مختصر القدوري» ؛ عبارته هكذا : «إذا لم ينو حتى أصبح أجزأته النية ما بينه وبين الزوال. انتهى
قوله : الزوال ؛ أي زوال الشمس، وهو نصف النهار العرفي.
قوله : والأوّل ؛ أي ما ذكر في المتن، و«الجامع الصغير».
قوله : أصح ؛ لما مر من أنه لا بد لتحقق النية في أكثر النهار.

قوله : وبنية مطلقة ؛ يعني يصح صوم رمضان بنيّة مطلقة ؛ كنويت الصوم، من غير أن يقيده بالفرض أو النفل، وكذا لو نوى صوم نفل أو صوم واجب آخر كصوم الكفارة. ووجهوا ذلك : بأنَّ رمضانَ متعيّن لصومِ الفرضِ من جانب الله ، وهو فوق تعيين العبد، فيصح بمطلق النية ، وإذا نوى نفلاً أو واجباً آخر، فقد نوى مطلقه مع قيدٍ زائد، فيلغو ما زاد لتعين رمضان للفرض، ويبقى المطلق فيصح الفرض، وزيادة تفصيله في كتب الأصول.
وفيه بحث، وهو أن تعيين الله له وإن كان فوق تعيين العبد، لكن في العبادات الاختيارية لا مخلص من التعيين منه، فـ«إنّما الأعمال بالنيات، وإنما لكل امرئ ما نوى»، على ما ورد مرفوعاً في الصحاح الستة وغيرها، فإذا عين الصائم غير صومِ رمضان فكيف يصح منه صوم رمضان، فمن فعل فعلاً خاصاً ونوى خاصاً يعتبر هو ا غيره ، نعم ؛ صحته بنية مطلقة مما لا شبهة فيه.

وأداء رمضان بنية واجب آخر إلا في مرض أو سفر، بل عما نوى، والنذر المعين عن واجب آخر نواه
أي أداء رمضان يصح بنية عن واجب آخر إلا في المرض أو السفر، فإنَّه يقع عن ذلك الواجب، وإذا نذر صوم يومٍ معين فنوي في ذلك اليوم واجباً آخر، يقع عن ذلك الواجب، سواء كان مسافراً أو مقيما ، صحيحاً أو مريضاً . وعبارة «المختصر» هذا : ويصح أداء رمضانَ بنية قبل نصف النَّهارِ الشَّرعي، وبنية نفل وبنية مطلقة، وبنية واجب آخر، إلا في سفر، أو مرض
وأما النذر المعين فصحته بمطلق النية أيضاً لا شبهة فيه ؛ لأنه لَمَّا عَيْنَ ذلك اليوم لصوم بنذره السابق لم يبق محتملاً لغيره، فيحمل المطلق عليه.
وأما صحته بنية نفل فوجهوه : بأنه لما تَعيَّنَ ذلك اليوم بتعينه لصوم النذر، لغت نية التنفل، وبقى مطلق الصوم، بخلاف ما إذا نوى في ذلك اليوم واجباً آخر، فإنّه يقعُ متساويان، بخلافِ النفل ، فإنَّ النذر لواجب فوقه، وفيه أيضاً ما عنه ؛ لأنَّ كلاً من هم .

قوله : إلا في مرض أو سفر ؛ يعنى إذا صام المريض أو المسافر في رمضان عن واجب آخر يقع عنه ؛ لأنه شغلَ الوقت بالأهم ؛ لتحتّمه في الحال، وتخيره في صوم رمضان، فرمضان في حقه كشعبان.
قوله : عن ذلك الواجب ؛ وأما إذا نوى المسافرُ أو المريض النفل فيه، ففي رواية : يقعُ عمّا نوى ؛ لكون رمضان في حقه كشعبان، وفي رواية : عن رمضان ؛ لأنَّ النفل ليس بأهم، فتلغونية التنفل، وصحح الأوّل في «السراج» وغيره.
قوله : يقع ... الخ ؛ لأن تعين ذلك اليوم كان من جانبه، فله أن يبطله.
قوله : وبنية مطلقة ؛ قال البرْجَنْدي : بالإضافة ؛ أي مطلق الصوم، وفي بعض النسخ : بنيّة مطلقة بالوصف، فقوله : «وواجب آخر على الأول، عطف على قوله :
Chapter of Fasting / Its definition, intention, types, and crescent.
And with an absolute intention, or intention of supererogatory.
And in al-Quduri's Mukhtasar: 'until noon'. And the first is more correct.

His saying: And in al-Quduri's Mukhtasar; its expression is: 'If he did not intend until dawn, the intention between him and noon suffices him.' End.
His saying: Noon; meaning the sun's zenith, which is the customary midday.
His saying: And the first; meaning what is mentioned in the text and al-Jami' al-Saghir.
His saying: More correct; because of what passed that verification of intention is necessary for most of the day.

His saying: And with an absolute intention; meaning the fast of Ramadan is valid with an absolute intention, like 'I intended fasting', without restricting it as obligatory or supererogatory, and similarly if he intended supererogatory fast or another obligatory fast like expiation fast. They explain that: because Ramadan is determined for the obligatory fast from Allah's side, and it is above the servant's determination, so it is valid with absolute intention. If he intended supererogatory or another obligatory fast, he intended its absolute with an extra restriction, so what was added is nullified due to Ramadan's determination for obligation, and the absolute remains, so the obligation becomes valid. Further detail is in books of usul.
And there is discussion, namely that Allah's determination of it, even though it is above the servant's determination, but in voluntary acts of worship, there is no escape from determination by him, so 'Actions are by intentions, and each person gets what he intended', as reported marfu'an in the six sahihs and others. So if the fasting person determined other than Ramadan fast, how can Ramadan fast be valid from him? For whoever performs a specific act and intends something specific, it is considered his intention, not another. Yes, its validity with an absolute intention is without doubt.

And performing Ramadan with intention of another obligatory fast except in sickness or travel, rather it falls upon what he intended. And the specified vow over another obligatory fast intended.
That is, performing Ramadan is valid with intention of another obligatory fast except in sickness or travel, for then it falls upon that obligation. And if he vowed to fast a specific day, then he intended another obligatory fast on that day, it falls upon that obligation, whether he is traveling or resident, healthy or sick. And the expression of al-Mukhtasar is: And performing Ramadan is valid with intention before the legal noon, and with intention of supererogatory, and with absolute intention, and with intention of another obligatory fast, except in travel or sickness.

As for the specified vow, its validity with absolute intention is also without doubt; because when he determined that day for fasting by his prior vow, it did not remain possible for other than it, so the absolute is carried upon it.
As for its validity with intention of supererogatory, they explain: because when that day was determined by its determination for the vow fast, the intention of supererogatory is nullified, and the absolute fast remains, unlike if he intended another obligatory fast on that day, for then they are equal, unlike supererogatory, because the vow is above it. And there is also what is against it; because each of them [?].

His saying: Except in sickness or travel; meaning if a sick or traveling person fasts in Ramadan for another obligation, it falls upon that; because he occupied the time with the more important due to its necessity immediately, and his choice in Ramadan fast, so Ramadan in his case is like Sha'ban.
His saying: Upon that obligation; and if the traveler or sick person intended supererogatory in it, in one narration: it falls upon what he intended; because Ramadan in his case is like Sha'ban. In another narration: upon Ramadan; because supererogatory is not more important, so the intention of supererogatory is nullified. The first was corrected in al-Siraj and others.
His saying: It falls... etc.; because the determination of that day was from his side, so he may nullify it.
His saying: And with an absolute intention; al-Barjandi said: as an addition; meaning absolute fast. In some copies: 'with an absolute intention' as an adjective, so his saying 'and another obligatory fast' is a conjunction upon his saying:
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains rulings on intentions for the Ramadan fast, building on the sharh of al-Wiqayah. The passage type is a ruling with proof. The author first presents the ruling from the matn: the Ramadan fast is valid with an absolute intention (e.g., 'I intend fasting' without specifying) or with intention of supererogatory fast. He then discusses the difference with al-Quduri's view that intention must be before noon, and states the correct view is the first (intention before dawn? Actually the text says 'the first is more correct' referring to the text's ruling that absolute intention suffices). The commentary explains that because Ramadan is divinely designated as obligatory, the servant's absolute intention or even a supererogatory intention is overridden and the Ramadan obligation is fulfilled. However, a discussion is raised: the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' seems to conflict—if one intends supererogatory, how can it become Ramadan? The response is that absolute intention (without qualification) clearly works; the case of supererogatory intention is debated but the school accepts validity. Then the passage addresses the ruling for Ramadan fast with intention of another obligatory fast (e.g., expiation): it is valid unless the person is traveling or sick, in which case the fast counts for the other obligation. The reason: a traveler or sick person is not obliged to fast Ramadan, so Ramadan in his case is like a non-Ramadan day. For a specified vow (nadhr mu'ayyan), the same rule applies: intention of another obligatory fast overrides the vow because the vow's determination is from the servant, so he can nullify it. The author provides commentary on each phrase, clarifying that for a traveler/sick person, intending supererogatory yields two narrations, with the correct one being that it counts for what he intended (the supererogatory). The passage concludes with a note on the phrase 'absolute intention' from al-Barjandi. The heavily relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that Ramadan fast is valid with absolute intention or intention of supererogatory, but intention of another obligatory fast only works for those with a legal excuse (travel/sickness).

Key Terms
نية مطلقة (niyya mutlaqa): An absolute intention to fast without specifying whether it is obligatory or supererogatory, e.g., 'I intend to fast'.
نفل (nafl): A supererogatory or voluntary act of worship.
واجب آخر (wajib akhar): Another obligatory fast, such as expiation (kaffara) or a fulfilled vow.
نذر معين (nadhr mu'ayyan): A vow to fast on a specific day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a healthy resident person who intends an expiation fast on a day of Ramadan?
A) The fast counts as the expiation.
B) The fast counts as Ramadan.
C) The fast is invalid because the intention is contradictory.
D) The fast counts as supererogatory.

Key Takeaway
The Ramadan fast is valid with an absolute intention or even a supererogatory intention; only a traveler or sick person can redirect the fast to another obligation by intending it.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
The Ruling on Fasting Ramadan with Intention of Another Obligatory Fast
Commentary on matn: validity of Ramadan fast with other intentions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting Ramadan with intention of another wajib fast, identify the exception for traveler and sick, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
A Ramadan fast with another wajib intention is valid; for traveler/sick it counts as that wajib.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya examines a nuanced rule: what happens when a person fasting Ramadan intends a different obligatory fast? The Hanafi school holds the fast is valid, but if the person is traveling or sick, it counts as the other obligation. We will explore the proof and the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being discussed? Fasting in Ramadan. What is the ruling? It is valid (sahih) if one intends another wajib fast, except for traveler/sick. For them, the fast counts as the other wajib.
The ruling is about validity (sihha), not about reward.
2
Note the exception
The exception is for a traveler or sick person. For them, the Ramadan fast with intention of another wajib actually falls upon that wajib, not Ramadan. The rationale is that their Ramadan obligation is deferred (they can make it up later), so the immediate wajib takes precedence.
This transforms the ruling from validity to substitution.
3
Understand the rationale
Why does the exception exist? Because the traveler/sick has a choice to fast or defer Ramadan, so they are prioritizing a more pressing wajib. For the healthy resident, Ramadan is obligatory immediately, so it overrides the other intention.
The principle: the obligation that is more immediate (mutahammam) prevails.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a difference among Hanafi scholars regarding when a traveler or sick person intends nafl (voluntary) fast in Ramadan. One riwaya says it counts as nafl, another says it counts as Ramadan. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that it counts as nafl.
This shows that even within the school, there are variant narrations.
5
Apply to examples
Practice with examples: a healthy resident intending make-up → counts as Ramadan. A traveler intending make-up → counts as make-up. A sick person intending nafl → preferred view: counts as nafl.

And performing Ramadan [fast] with the intention of another obligatory [fast] is [valid] except in illness or travel, rather it falls upon what he intended, and a specific vow [falls] upon another obligatory [fast] that he intended. That is: performing Ramadan fast is valid with the intention of another obligatory [fast] except in illness or travel, then it falls upon that obligatory [fast]. And if one vowed to fast a specific day and then intended on that day another obligatory [fast], it falls upon that obligatory [fast], whether he is a traveler or resident, healthy or sick. The expression of 'Al-Mukhtasar' is: And performing Ramadan fast is valid with intention before the middle of the legal day, and with intention of supererogatory [fast] and absolute intention, and with intention of another obligatory [fast], except in travel or illness. As for a specific vow, its validity with absolute intention is also unquestionable; because when he specified that day for fasting by his prior vow, it no longer admits of anything else, so the absolute [intention] is applied to it. As for its validity with intention of supererogatory [fast], they explained it: because when that day is specified by its specification for the vowed fast, the intention of supererogatory is voided, and the absolute fast remains; contrary to when he intends on that day another obligatory [fast], then they are equal, unlike supererogatory, for the vow is [for] an obligatory [fast] above it. And if the traveler or sick intends supererogatory in it, then in one narration: it falls upon what he intended, because Ramadan in his case is like Sha'ban; and in another narration: upon Ramadan, because supererogatory is not more important, so the intention of supererogatory is voided. The first was deemed correct in 'Al-Siraj' and others. [3] His statement: it falls... etc., because the specification of that day was from his side, so he can nullify it. So his statement: 'and another obligatory [fast]' in the first [reading] is a conjunct to his statement: and supererogatory with its intention, and with absolute intention before noon, not after it. And a condition for make-up, expiation, and absolute vow is the night intention and specification. (YK1) And similarly supererogatory and specific vow except in the last [i.e. another obligatory]. That is: the ruling of supererogatory and specific vow is the ruling of performing Ramadan except in the last, which is the other obligatory. And supererogatory [is valid] with its intention and with absolute intention before noon, not after. And a condition for make-up, expiation, and absolute vow is the night intention and specification. On the second [reading] it is a conjunct to intention, and the addition of the modifier is needed to make the meaning correct, i.e. intention of another obligatory [fast], like make-up, expiation, and vow. This is on the version which does not have: 'and with intention of another obligatory [fast]'. But on it, as indicated by the commentator's expression in this book, there is no need for the addition. (2) His statement: except in the last; exception from his statement: and similarly supererogatory and specific vow. [3] His statement: before noon; the preferable [interpretation] is before the greater duha [i.e. forenoon] or before the half of the day, and perhaps that is what is meant, for 'zawal' may be interpreted as the legal middle of the day, as in 'Al-Nihaya'. [4] His statement: and specification; as for the condition of specification, because the day on which he fasts for make-up, expiation, or absolute vow is not specified for it, neither by Allah's side nor by the slave's side, so it must be specified due to the existence of competition and because that day is capable of any fast. Unlike the fast of Ramadan and specific vow, because there is specification from his side or from his Lord, so absolute intention suffices, indeed the intention of supererogatory is also voided. As for the condition of night intention, it is also due to lack of its specification, and it is supported by the hadith: 'Whoever does not intend the fast before dawn, there is no fast for him' reported by the four Sunan and others.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Al-Wiqaya regarding the validity of fasting Ramadan with various intentions. The author begins by stating the rule: performing the Ramadan fast with the intention of another obligatory fast (such as make-up, expiation, or a vow) is valid except for a traveler or a sick person. For them, the fast actually counts as that other obligatory fast, not Ramadan. The reasoning is that their obligation for Ramadan is deferred (they can make it up later), so the immediate obligation takes precedence. For a specific vowed fast (nadhr mu'ayyan), if one intends another obligatory fast on that day, it always counts as the other obligation, regardless of travel or illness, because the specification came from the person himself, so he can nullify it. The commentary then discusses the wording of Al-Mukhtasar, adding that intention before noon is a condition for the validity of the Ramadan fast. It also explains that for a specific vowed fast, an absolute intention (intending simply 'fast') suffices because the day is already designated for that vow. The passage then addresses the case of intending supererogatory (nafl) fast on a day of an obligatory fast: for Ramadan, if one is a traveler or sick, there is a difference of narration; the preferred view is that it counts as the nafl. For a specific vow, intending nafl is voided and it counts as the vow. The commentary also clarifies the conditions for make-up (qada), expiation (kaffarah), and absolute vow (nadhr mutlaq): they require night intention (tabyit) and specification of the fast (ta'yin), unlike Ramadan and specific vow which only require an absolute intention before noon. The authority for night intention is the hadith 'Whoever does not intend the fast before dawn, no fast for him' (narrated by the four Sunan). The commentator notes that 'zawal' in the text may refer to the middle of the day (nisful nihar). This passage reflects the Hanafi position that intention before noon (for voluntary or Ramadan fasts) is valid, but for obligatory make-up and expiation, intention must be made before dawn.

Key Terms
التبييت (al-tabyit): Making intention for the fast during the night before the day of fasting, i.e. before dawn.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specifying which fast one is intending (e.g. make-up for a particular missed day, expiation for a particular reason).
النذر المعين (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan): A vow to fast a specific day or days (e.g., 'I will fast on Friday').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person who is not traveling or sick fasts on a day of Ramadan intending to make up a missed fast from the previous year. What is the ruling?
A) The fast is invalid because you cannot combine intentions.
B) The fast counts as the Ramadan fast, not the make-up.
C) The fast counts as the make-up, and he must still fast Ramadan later.
D) The fast is valid and counts as both simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a Ramadan fast with intention of another wajib is valid; for the traveler/sick it fulfills that other wajib, but for the resident healthy it remains a Ramadan fast.

Specific Vowed Fast and Conditions for Intention in Obligatory Fasts
Commentary on matn: conditions of intention for different obligatory fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the intention requirements for different types of obligatory fasts (Ramadan, specific vow, make-up, expiation, absolute vow) and apply the rule that tabyit and ta'yin are required for unspecified wajib fasts.

Core Idea
For unspecified wajib fasts, night intention and specification are needed; for Ramadan and specific vow, absolute intention before noon suffices.

This lesson continues the commentary on Al-Wiqaya, turning to the conditions of intention for various obligatory fasts. The key distinction is between fasts that are specified by themselves (Ramadan and specific vows) and those that are not (make-up, expiation, absolute vows). The hadith 'Whoever does not intend the fast before dawn, no fast for him' is applied to the latter category.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the types of wajib fasts
Identify the four categories: Ramadan, specific vow (nadhr mu'ayyan), make-up (qada), expiation (kaffarah), and absolute vow (nadhr mutlaq). The first two are 'specified' by their nature; the rest are not.
Specified means the day itself is determined by divine command (Ramadan) or by personal vow (specific vow).
2
Rule for specific vow (nadhr mu'ayyan)
A specific vow is valid with absolute intention (just 'fast') because the day is already designated. If another wajib is intended, it counts as that wajib, and the vow is nullified.
The person can undo his own specification.
3
Rule for unspecified wajib fasts
Make-up, expiation, and absolute vow require both tabyit (intention before dawn) and ta'yin (specification of which fast). The day is not predetermined, so explicit intention is needed.
This is based on the hadith and the principle of avoiding uncertainty.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith 'Whoever does not intend the fast before dawn, no fast for him' is cited as evidence for tabyit. Hanafis apply it only to unspecified wajib fasts.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its application is through takhsis (specification) of the general command.
5
Understand the rationale
Why are Ramadan and specific vow exempt? Because the day is already specified, so a general intention suffices. For unspecified fasts, without explicit intention, the fast could be confused with other obligations.
This is a practical application of the usul principle: 'ma utiqa min al-bayan' (what is known from context needs no explicit statement).
