And performing Ramadan [fast] with the intention of another obligatory [fast] is [valid] except in illness or travel, rather it falls upon what he intended, and a specific vow [falls] upon another obligatory [fast] that he intended. That is: performing Ramadan fast is valid with the intention of another obligatory [fast] except in illness or travel, then it falls upon that obligatory [fast]. And if one vowed to fast a specific day and then intended on that day another obligatory [fast], it falls upon that obligatory [fast], whether he is a traveler or resident, healthy or sick. The expression of 'Al-Mukhtasar' is: And performing Ramadan fast is valid with intention before the middle of the legal day, and with intention of supererogatory [fast] and absolute intention, and with intention of another obligatory [fast], except in travel or illness. As for a specific vow, its validity with absolute intention is also unquestionable; because when he specified that day for fasting by his prior vow, it no longer admits of anything else, so the absolute [intention] is applied to it. As for its validity with intention of supererogatory [fast], they explained it: because when that day is specified by its specification for the vowed fast, the intention of supererogatory is voided, and the absolute fast remains; contrary to when he intends on that day another obligatory [fast], then they are equal, unlike supererogatory, for the vow is [for] an obligatory [fast] above it. And in it also is what about them, because each of them. [1] His statement: except in illness or travel; meaning if a sick or traveler fasts in Ramadan for another obligatory [fast], it falls upon it; because he occupied the time with the more important, due to its immediate necessity and his option in fasting Ramadan, so Ramadan in his case is like Sha'ban. [2] His statement: upon that obligatory [fast]. And if the traveler or sick intends supererogatory in it, then in one narration: it falls upon what he intended, because Ramadan in his case is like Sha'ban; and in another narration: upon Ramadan, because supererogatory is not more important, so the intention of supererogatory is voided. The first was deemed correct in 'Al-Siraj' and others. [3] His statement: it falls... etc., because the specification of that day was from his side, so he can nullify it. [4] His statement: and with absolute intention. Al-Birjandi said: by addition, meaning absolute fast, and in some copies: with absolute intention as description. So his statement: 'and another obligatory [fast]' in the first [reading] is a conjunct to his statement: and supererogatory with its intention, and with absolute intention before noon, not after it. And a condition for make-up, expiation, and absolute vow is the night intention and specification. (YK1) And similarly supererogatory and specific vow except in the last [i.e. another obligatory]. That is: the ruling of supererogatory and specific vow is the ruling of performing Ramadan except in the last, which is the other obligatory. And supererogatory [is valid] with its intention and with absolute intention before noon, not after. And a condition for make-up, expiation, and absolute vow is the night intention and specification. On the second [reading] it is a conjunct to intention, and the addition of the modifier is needed to make the meaning correct, i.e. intention of another obligatory [fast], like make-up, expiation, and vow. This is on the version which does not have: 'and with intention of another obligatory [fast]'. But on it, as indicated by the commentator's expression in this book, there is no need for the addition. [1] His statement: and similarly supererogatory; meaning supererogatory [fast] is valid with the intention of absolute fast and intention of supererogatory, not with intention of another obligatory [fast]; and similarly specific vow. (2) His statement: except in the last; exception from his statement: and similarly supererogatory and specific vow. [3] His statement: before noon; the preferable [interpretation] is before the greater duha [i.e. forenoon] or before the half of the day, and perhaps that is what is meant, for 'zawal' may be interpreted as the legal middle of the day, as in 'Al-Nihaya'. [4] His statement: and specification; as for the condition of specification, because the day on which he fasts for make-up, expiation, or absolute vow is not specified for it, neither by Allah's side nor by the slave's side, so it must be specified due to the existence of competition and because that day is capable of any fast. Unlike the fast of Ramadan and specific vow, because there is specification from his side or from his Lord, so absolute intention suffices, indeed the intention of supererogatory is also voided. As for the condition of night intention, it is also due to lack of its specification, and it is supported by the hadith: 'Whoever does not intend the fast before dawn, there is no fast for him' reported by the four Sunan and others.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Al-Wiqaya regarding the validity of fasting Ramadan with various intentions. The author begins by stating the rule: performing the Ramadan fast with the intention of another obligatory fast (such as make-up, expiation, or a vow) is valid except for a traveler or a sick person. For them, the fast actually counts as that other obligatory fast, not Ramadan. The reasoning is that their obligation for Ramadan is deferred (they can make it up later), so the immediate obligation takes precedence. For a specific vowed fast (nadhr mu'ayyan), if one intends another obligatory fast on that day, it always counts as the other obligation, regardless of travel or illness, because the specification came from the person himself, so he can nullify it. The commentary then discusses the wording of Al-Mukhtasar, adding that intention before noon is a condition for the validity of the Ramadan fast. It also explains that for a specific vowed fast, an absolute intention (intending simply 'fast') suffices because the day is already designated for that vow. The passage then addresses the case of intending supererogatory (nafl) fast on a day of an obligatory fast: for Ramadan, if one is a traveler or sick, there is a difference of narration; the preferred view is that it counts as the nafl. For a specific vow, intending nafl is voided and it counts as the vow. The commentary also clarifies the conditions for make-up (qada), expiation (kaffarah), and absolute vow (nadhr mutlaq): they require night intention (tabyit) and specification of the fast (ta'yin), unlike Ramadan and specific vow which only require an absolute intention before noon. The authority for night intention is the hadith 'Whoever does not intend the fast before dawn, no fast for him' (narrated by the four Sunan). The commentator notes that 'zawal' in the text may refer to the middle of the day (nisful nihar). This passage reflects the Hanafi position that intention before noon (for voluntary or Ramadan fasts) is valid, but for obligatory make-up and expiation, intention must be made before dawn.

Key Terms
التبييت (al-tabyit): Making intention for the fast during the night before the day of fasting, i.e. before dawn.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specifying which fast one is intending (e.g. make-up for a particular missed day, expiation for a particular reason).
النذر المعين (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan): A vow to fast a specific day or days (e.g., 'I will fast on Friday').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following requires intention before dawn (tabyit) and specification (ta'yin) according to the Hanafi school?
A) Ramadan fast
B) Specific vow (nadhr mu'ayyan)
C) Make-up fast (qada)
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, intention requirements for fasting depend on whether the day is already specified; unspecified wajib fasts need night intention and specification, while specified ones need only absolute intention before noon.

Intention for Supererogatory and Obligatory Fasting in the Hanafi School
Defines intention rules for supererogatory and obligatory fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the intention requirements between supererogatory and obligatory fasting and apply the conditions of tabyit and ta'yin correctly.

Core Idea
For voluntary fasts, intention before midday suffices; for obligatory fasts, intention must be made at night with specification.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the intention conditions for different fasting types. It explains when a simple intention is enough and when tabyit (night intention) and ta'yin (specification) are required. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for the validity of your fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of fasting discussed
The passage lists nafl (supererogatory), qada (make-up), kaffara (expiation), nadhr mutlaq (absolute vow), and nadhr mu'ayyan (specified vow).
2
State the Hanafi ruling on intention per type
For nafl: absolute intention before zawal suffices. For qada, kaffara, and mutlaq nadhr: tabyit and ta'yin are required. For nadhr mu'ayyan and Ramadan: same as nafl.
Zawal is interpreted as the Shari'i midday (half of the day).
3
Examine the rationale for ta'yin
The day for make-up, expiation, or absolute vow is not inherently specified for that fast, so specification is necessary to distinguish it from other potential fasts. In contrast, Ramadan and specified vows are already determined by Allah or the vow maker.
4
Analyze the dalil for tabyit
The hadith 'Whoever does not resolve to fast before dawn, there is no fast for him' is cited. The Hanafi school applies it to obligatory fasts that lack inherent specification, not to nafl or Ramadan.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah.
5
Note the Shafi'i counter-position
The Shafi'i school requires tabyit for all fasts including nafl, applying the same hadith universally. The practical difference: Hanafis allow spontaneous nafl during the day; Shafi'is require planning the night before.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (scholarly difference).

And supererogatory (fasting) is with its intention, and with an absolute intention before zawal (declination of the sun), not after it. And for make-up (qada), expiation (kaffara), and absolute vow (nadhr mutlaq), the conditions are tabyit (night intention) and ta'yin (specification). (YK1) Likewise supererogatory and the specified vow, except in the last (21): that is, the ruling of supererogatory and the specified vow is the ruling of performing Ramadan except in the last, which is the other obligatory. And supererogatory is with its intention, and with an absolute intention before zawal, not after it. And the condition for make-up, expiation, and absolute vow is tabyit and ta'yin. 'Absolute' and the second is a conjunction on intention, and it requires estimating the genitive to make the meaning correct: that is, intention of another obligatory, like make-up, expiation, and vow. End. And this is according to the version in which there is not 'and intention of another obligatory.' (2) His statement: 'Except in the last' is an exception from his statement: 'Likewise supererogatory and the specified vow.' [3] His statement: 'Before zawal' is better interpreted as before the greater forenoon (al-dahwa al-kubra) or before half of the day, and perhaps the intended meaning is this, as zawal may be interpreted as the middle of the day according to the Shari'ah, as in al-Nihaya. And as for the condition of tabyit, it is due to its lack of specification as well, supported by the hadith: 'Whoever does not resolve to fast before dawn, there is no fast for him,' narrated by the authors of the four Sunans and others. (2)
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, expounding the conditions of intention (niyyah) for different categories of fasting. The matn states that for supererogatory (nafl) fasting, an absolute intention (simply intending to fast) made before zawal (declination of the sun from the zenith) is valid. Zawal here is interpreted as midday (the Shari'i midday). For obligatory fasts like make-up (qada), expiation (kaffara), and absolute vows (nadhr mutlaq), two conditions are required: tabyit (making the intention at night, before Fajr) and ta'yin (specifying which fast one intends). The commentator clarifies that the rule for nafl and for a specified vow (nadhr mu'ayyan) is similar to that of Ramadan: absolute intention suffices, because the day is inherently specified for that fast. However, in the case of a specified vow, the 'last' (i.e., the obligation) is different because it is a wajib that has been specified by the vow. The condition of tabyit is derived from the hadith: 'Whoever does not resolve to fast before dawn, there is no fast for him' (narrated by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, etc.). The condition of ta'yin is needed because the day for a make-up fast is not inherently linked to that fast; it could be used for any fast, so one must specify to distinguish it from other potential fasts. In the case of Ramadan and specified vows, the day is already determined by Allah or by the vow, so specification is not needed; absolute intention suffices.

Key Terms
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory fasting – any voluntary fast not obligatory.
قضاء (qada): Make-up fast for a missed obligatory fast (e.g., Ramadan).
كفارة (kaffara): Expiation fast for breaking a Ramadan fast intentionally or for certain sins.
نذر (nadhr): A vow to fast; if absolute (mutlaq), it requires tabyit and ta'yin.
تبييت (tabyit): Making the intention for fasting during the night before Fajr.
تعيين (ta'yin): Specifying the type of fast (e.g., qada, kaffara) in one's intention.
زوال (zawal): The time when the sun passes the meridian (beginning of Dhuhr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person intends to perform a nafl fast at 11:00 AM (before zawal) without specifying any particular fast. According to the Hanafi school, this intention is:
A) Invalid because ta'yin is required.
B) Valid because absolute intention before zawal suffices for nafl.
C) Invalid because intention must be at night.
D) Valid only if the fast is for Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
The intention conditions for fasting vary by type: supererogatory allows daytime intention until midday, while obligatory non-Ramadan fasts require night intention and specification.

Fasting on the Day of Doubt: Ruling and Validity
Ruling on fasting when the moon is hidden on the night of doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting on yawm al-shakk, distinguish between makruh tahrimi and tanzihi, and explain the preferred (al-ashahh) position on validity with its proof.

Core Idea
Fasting on the day of doubt is disliked but counts for an intended wajib in the preferred Hanafi view.

The day of doubt (yawm al-shakk) arises when the new moon of Ramadan is not sighted on the night of the 30th of Sha'ban due to clouds. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies the ruling for a Muslim who wishes to fast on that day—whether for Ramadan, a vow, make-up, or expiation. It also reveals an intra-Hanafi debate about whether such a fast fulfills the obligation, and settles on the most sound opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Day of Doubt
Recognise that yawm al-shakk is the morning after the 30th night of Sha'ban when the moon is hidden. The ruling only applies when the moon is not seen due to clouds or dust.
If the moon is absent due to genuine impossibility (e.g. early birth), the day is still yawm al-shakk.
2
Distinguish the Two Intentions
The passage distinguishes between intending Ramadan (or any wajib) on that day. Intending Ramadan is makruh tahrimi; intending another wajib (qada, nadhr, kaffarah) is makruh tanzihi.
The dislikedness is attached to the act of fasting, not to the intention of the fast.
3
Understand the Dislikedness Levels
Makruh tahrimi is closer to haram and is based on a clear prohibition (the hadith). Makruh tanzihi is slightly disliked and is due to avoiding resemblance to Christians who anticipated their fast.
The Prophet's command 'do not fast before Ramadan' underlies the tahrimi level.
4
Analyse the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Validity
Some Hanafis argue that because the fast is prohibited (nafl only), it cannot fulfill a wajib obligation. The preferred view (al-ashahh) holds that the prohibition is on the timing, not the fast itself, so the fast is valid and counts for the wajib.
The counter-argument is that 'the obligatory is complete, and a deficient fast cannot replace it'; but the majority responds that the deficiency is in the time, not the essence.
5
Apply the Proof
The hadith 'Do not fast before Ramadan...' proves prohibition, but the wording 'لا تصوموا' is general. The Hanafi response: the prohibition is specific to anticipating Ramadan; it does not render the fast invalid if done for another reason.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its dalala is zanni, allowing room for interpretation.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, discussing the legal status of fasting on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk), which is the 30th of Sha'ban when the new moon of Ramadan is not sighted due to clouds or dust. The matn states that on such a day, fasting is only permissible as a supererogatory (nafl) fast. If one fasts intending an obligatory fast (like Ramadan, make-up, expiation, or a vow), it is disliked (makruh). However, according to the most sound opinion (al-ashahh), the fast counts for that obligation. The commentator explains that the reason for prohibition is the hadith: 'Do not fast before Ramadan...' and the principle of avoiding resemblance to Christians who anticipated their fast. There is a distinction: intending Ramadan is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) due to the specific prohibition, while intending another wajib (like make-up) is slightly disliked (makruh tanzihi) because the prohibition is only about anticipating Ramadan. The disagreement is whether the fast fulfills the obligation. Some say it only counts as nafl, but the preferred view is that it counts for the intended wajib, because the prohibition is about the time, not the essence of the fast itself.

Key Terms
غم (ghamma): The moon being hidden from sight due to clouds or dust.
ليلة الشك (laylat al-shakk): The night of the 30th of Sha'ban when the new moon of Ramadan is uncertain.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked; here divided into tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and tanzihi (slightly disliked).
الأصح (al-ashahh): The most sound opinion; the preferred view among Hanafi scholars.

Sawm — Fasting

Fasting on the Day of Doubt: Ruling and Intentions
Explains ruling and intent for fasting on day of doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting the day of doubt, distinguish between the two levels of dislike (makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi), and explain the asahh opinion that the fast counts for other obligatory fasts.

Core Idea
Fasting the day of doubt is permissible only as nafl; intending Ramadan is prohibitively disliked, but other obligatory intentions are lightly disliked and valid.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the Hanafi position on fasting the 30th of Sha'ban when the crescent is not sighted (yawm al-shakk). It explains which intentions are valid, which are disliked, and which are prohibitively disliked, grounding the rulings in the hadith prohibiting anticipation of Ramadan. The commentary also resolves an intra-school dispute on whether such a fast counts for an intended obligation, using the analogy of prayer on usurped land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fasting on the day of doubt (30th Sha'ban). The basic ruling: only supererogatory (nafl) fasting is permissible. Fasting for Ramadan is prohibited; for other obligations it is disliked but valid in the more correct view.
The day of doubt occurs when the crescent is not seen due to clouds.
2
Distinguish between intentions
Three categories of intention are discussed: (1) intending Ramadan – prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi); (2) intending another obligatory fast – lightly disliked (makruh tanzihi) but valid; (3) intending nafl – permissible and not disliked.
The hadith 'Do not fast before Ramadan' is the primary proof.
3
Analyze the dalil and its application
The hadith prohibits fasting before Ramadan. The Hanafis interpret this as a prohibition of intending Ramadan specifically, not of all fasting. The wisdom cited is avoiding resemblance to the Christians who anticipated their fast.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so it yields a ruling of karahah, not haram.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and tarjih
There is a dispute: some say fasting for another obligation only counts as supererogatory (tatawwu'); the more correct (asahh) opinion says it counts for the intended obligation. The proof for the asahh is the analogy to prayer in usurped land – the prohibition of the place does not invalidate the prayer.
The tarjih is made by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and is the mufta bihi.
5
Apply the ruling to edge cases
The commentary also covers: if the day later appears to be Ramadan, it counts for Ramadan; if it remains unknown, it does not fulfill the obligation. Also, fasting on the day of doubt is recommended if it coincides with a habitual fast or if one fasts three days at the end of Sha'ban.
The definition of 'elite' (khawas) includes anyone who knows this ruling, not only scholars.

[۱] قوله : إن لم يظهر ؛ أي إن لم يعلم أنَّ ذلك اليوم من رمضان، والأولى أن يقول : إن ظهر أنه من شعبان، فإنّه لو لم يظهر الحالُ لا يكفي عمّا نوى، ولا يسقط عنه ذلك الواجب الجواز أن يكون من رمضان، فلا يكون قضاء في الشك. كذا في «السراج الوهاج». [۲] قوله : وإلا ؛ أي إن ظهر أنه من رمضان بأن تثبت رؤية هلاله في تلك الليلة. [۳]قوله : إن وافق ؛ كما لو كان يعتاد صوم يوم الاثنين أو يوم الخميس فوافق ذلك يومَ الشَّكَ، أو صام من آخر شعبان ثلاثة أو أكثر لا أقل، فإن التقديم بيوم أو يومين منهي عنه ، لا أكثر.

And if one fasts it for another obligatory [fast], it is disliked, and it counts [for that obligatory fast] in the more correct [opinion]. The meaning of 'tabyit' is to intend from the night. (And if it is overcast on the night of doubt): i.e., the night of the thirtieth of Sha'ban, one should not fast except as supererogatory. And if one fasts it for another obligatory [fast], it is disliked, and it counts [for that obligatory fast] in the more correct [opinion]: i.e., it counts for the other obligatory [fast] in the more correct [opinion]. [1] His statement: 'overcast' in the passive voice; i.e., the crescent is hidden and not seen due to cloud, meaning cloud or dust preventing its sight, so doubt and hesitation occur as to whether it is the night of Ramadan or the night of the thirtieth of Sha'ban; therefore its morning is called the day of doubt. [2] His statement: 'except as supererogatory'; not for Ramadan nor for another obligatory [fast], like vow, make-up, and expiation. For the first [i.e., fasting for Ramadan] is prohibitively disliked; due to the hadith: 'Do not fast before Ramadan; fast when you see it and break your fast when you see it; and if clouds obscure it for you, complete the count of thirty, and do not anticipate the month anticipatorily.' Narrated by al-Tirmidhi and others. The fiqh of this is that it resembles the Christians: fasting was prescribed for them in Ramadan, so they anticipated it and increased their fast until they made fifty days obligatory. The second [i.e., fasting for another obligatory] is lightly disliked. Thus in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah and others. [3] His statement: 'and it counts' etc.; i.e., if he fasts it for another obligatory, it suffices for him according to him. [4] His statement: 'so the obligatory is not fulfilled'; because the obligatory is complete, and fasting if prohibited is deficient, and the complete is not fulfilled by the deficient. The face of the more correct [opinion] is that the prohibited matter is only the anticipation of Ramadan by fasting for Ramadan, not by every fast. And we only said it is disliked for another obligatory because it is like the fast of Ramadan in obligation, so that does not cause deficiency in the fast itself; so it is suitable for dropping what is obligatory and what he intended, like prayer in usurped land, for its dislike does not affect the dropping of prayers. Thus in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah. [1] His statement: 'if it does not become apparent'; i.e., if it is not known that that day is from Ramadan, and it is better to say: if it becomes apparent that it is from Sha'ban; for if the situation does not become apparent, it does not suffice for what he intended, and that obligatory does not fall from him since it is possible that it is from Ramadan, so it cannot be a make-up in doubt. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. [2] His statement: 'otherwise'; i.e., if it becomes apparent that it is from Ramadan by the sighting of its crescent being established that night. [3] His statement: 'if it coincides'; as if he was accustomed to fast Monday or Thursday and that coincides with the day of doubt, or he fasts three or more from the end of Sha'ban, not less, for anticipating by one or two days is prohibited, not more. [4] His statement: 'otherwise'; i.e., if it does not coincide with a fast he was accustomed to, and he did not fast three or more from the end of Sha'ban. [0] His statement: 'the elite'; the meaning of 'elite' in this context is not only the mufti and judge, nor only the scholar as many laymen think, rather every person who knows the modality of fasting on the day of doubt is among the elite, otherwise he is among the commoners.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn regarding fasting on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk), which is the 30th of Sha'ban when the crescent for Ramadan is not seen due to cloud cover. The ruling states that on such a day, one may only fast as a supererogatory (nafl) act. Fasting with the intention of Ramadan or another obligatory fast (like a vow, makeup of a missed fast, or expiation) is disliked. However, if someone does fast for another obligatory fast, according to the more correct (asahh) opinion, it counts for that obligation, not as a supererogatory fast. The opposing view (qila) holds it counts only as supererogatory because the fast is prohibited and thus deficient. The Hanafi proof is that the prohibition (karahah) is specifically for fasting with the intention of Ramadan, not for any other intention. The hadith 'Do not fast before Ramadan...' is cited, and the wisdom is avoiding resemblance to the Christians who anticipated their fast. The commentary distinguishes between two types of disliked: for fasting for Ramadan it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), and for another obligatory fast it is makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked). The proof for counting the obligatory fast is analogous to prayer in usurped land: the prayer is valid despite the sin of usurpation. The passage further explains conditions: if later it appears that the day was indeed from Ramadan, then that day counts for Ramadan (if intended appropriately). The elite (khawas) are defined as those who know the ruling of fasting on this day, not just judges or scholars. The commentary concludes with recommendations: it is better to fast on the day of doubt if it coincides with a habitual fast (e.g., Monday/Thursday) or if one already fasts three days at the end of Sha'ban, because the prohibition is only for one or two days ahead.

Key Terms
يوم الشك (yawm al-shakk): The 30th of Sha'ban when the crescent for Ramadan is not sighted due to clouds, causing doubt about whether it is Ramadan or still Sha'ban.
التبييت (al-tabyit): Making the intention for a fast during the night before dawn. Derived from 'bayt' (night).
الخواص (al-khawas): The elite; defined here as those who know the ruling and modality of fasting on the day of doubt, not merely judges or scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The fast is valid and counts for the make-up obligation.
B) The fast is invalid and must be repeated.
C) The fast is valid only as a supererogatory (nafl) fast.
D) The fast is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) and does not count.

Key Takeaway
The day of doubt is only fasted as nafl; other intentions are disliked but valid in the more correct Hanafi opinion, due to the specific prohibition being limited to anticipating Ramadan.

Fasting on the Day of Doubt: Intention and Categories
States the ruling on fasting the day of doubt and defines 'khawass' and 'awamm'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting the day of doubt, explain the distinction between khawass and awamm, and apply the conditions for a valid fast on this day.

Core Idea
Fasting the day of doubt is valid but may be disliked; only those who understand intention rules (khawass) should fast without a habit.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on fasting the 30th of Sha'ban when Ramadan's sighting is uncertain. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies who may fast, what intention they should make, and why consensus recommends it only for those with a habit or special knowledge. By parsing the commentary, you will learn to distinguish between commoners (awamm) and specialists (khawass) in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The matn states that if the Ramadan-ness of the day is unknown, one may fast with the intention of Ramadan or another wajib fast. Note that this intention must be definitive (jazm).
This establishes permissibility.
2
Distinguish supererogatory fasting
The text then addresses supererogatory fasting (tatawwu'). It is recommended by consensus only if it coincides with a habit (e.g., Mondays). Otherwise, only the khawass (knowledgeable) should fast.
Habit makes it recommended; without habit, it is disliked for awamm.
3
Define khawass and awamm
The commentary clarifies that khawass are not only scholars or judges but anyone who understands the proper intention for this day. Awamm are those who lack this knowledge.
This is a knowledge-based distinction, not status-based.
4
Analyze the analogy
The sharh compares the disliked fast to prayer in usurped land: the act is valid despite the dislike. Therefore, a disliked fast on the day of doubt is still valid and fulfills the obligation if the intention was correct.
Dislike does not affect validity.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a person with no habit but knowledge of intention rules (khawass), they may fast. Given a person with a habit but no knowledge, they may fast as well because the habit makes it recommended.
Practice distinguishing cases.

If its Ramadan-ness does not appear, then fast it with intention of Ramadan or another wajib; and supererogatory fasting on it is liked by consensus if it coincides with a habit, otherwise only the khawass fast it. [1] (If its Ramadan-ness does not appear, then from it): meaning from Ramadan, for Ramadan fast is fulfilled by the intention of another obligatory fast. (And supererogatory fasting on it): meaning on the day of doubt, is liked by consensus if it coincides with a habit, otherwise only the khawass fast it, like the mufti and judge. ... The meaning of khawass here is not only the mufti and judge, nor only the scholar as many common people think, but everyone who knows the manner of fasting the day of doubt is from the khawass, otherwise from the awamm.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-statement from the matn on fasting the day of doubt, expanded by the sharh. The matn establishes that if one does not know whether the day is from Ramadan or not, one may fast with the intention of Ramadan or another wajib fast, and the fast will count as Ramadan if it indeed is. Supererogatory fasting on this day is recommended (ahabba) by consensus only if it coincides with a habit the person observes (e.g., fasting Mondays or Thursdays). If it does not match a habit, only the khawass—those who understand the legal nuance of intention—should fast. The commentary clarifies that 'khawass' includes any person who knows the proper manner of intention for the day of doubt, not only scholars or judges. It further explains that it is disliked (makruh) for the general public to fast without a habit, but the fast is valid and suffices even if intention was for another wajib. An analogy is drawn to prayer in usurped land: the dislike does not invalidate the act.

Key Terms
يوم الشك (yawm al-shakk): The day of doubt: the 30th of Sha'ban when the sighting of Ramadan's crescent is uncertain.
الخواص (al-khawāṣṣ): Special people: muftis, judges, or anyone who knows the proper manner of intention for the day of doubt.
نية الجزم (niyyat al-jazm): Definitive intention: a clear, unambiguous intention not subject to condition or doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is invalid because it is makruh.
B) The fast is valid and recommended.
C) The fast is valid but disliked (makruh tanzihan).
D) The fast is haram.

Key Takeaway
Fasting the day of doubt is permissible with proper intention, and its dislike does not invalidate the fast; only knowledgeable persons (khawass) should fast without a habitual pattern.

Conditional Intentions and the Day of Doubt
Rulings on conditional intentions and timing for commoners on the day of doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a commoner on the day of doubt and classify three types of conditional intentions, distinguishing between invalid and disliked.

Core Idea
Hesitation in the essence of intention invalidates fasting; hesitation in description only makes it disliked.

On the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk), when the start of Ramadan is uncertain, special rulings apply. For commoners (awamm), the Hanafi school prescribes waiting until zawal before breaking the fast. For intention, the key principle is that intention must be definite (jazm). This lesson explains the timing for awamm and three types of conditional intentions and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of people
The text distinguishes between 'khawass' (those who can determine the month) and 'awamm' (commoners). Recognize that different rulings apply to each group regarding the day of doubt.
2
Understand the ruling for awamm
The text says 'and others break their fast after zawal.' This means the commoner should wait until after noon for news of the crescent, then eat. It does not nullify a valid nafl fast, but means they hold off eating until that time.
3
Examine the three types of intentions
The passage presents three scenarios: (1) 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan, I am fasting for it; otherwise not' – pure conditional. (2) 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan, then for it; otherwise for another wajib; otherwise as nafl' – conditional with alternative. (3) hesitation in description (e.g., uncertain whether the fast is fard or nafl while being definite about fasting).
The third type is implied in the commentary about hesitation in description.
4
Apply the principle of jazm
The pillar of intention is 'al-'azm bi al-jazm' (determination with definiteness). The first type lacks jazm in the essence, so it is invalid. The second type has jazm in the essence but hesitation in description, so it is disliked. The third type is also disliked but valid.
5
Determine the practical outcome
For an invalid intention, the fast must be repeated if it was obligatory. For a disliked intention, the fast is valid but contrary to the recommended practice; it is better to avoid such intentions.

And others (non-khawass) break their fast after zawal. And no fast (is valid) if he intends: 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan, then I am fasting for it; otherwise not.' And it is disliked if he intends: 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan, then I am fasting for it; otherwise for another wajib; otherwise as nafl.' [0] His saying: 'and they break fast': it does not mean he nullifies his fast if he fasted nafl, rather it means the common person waits until zawal for news of the crescent, then eats and drinks. Is it disliked for the awamm to fast nafl? The correct view is it is not disliked, rather it is better not to fast... [2] His saying: 'no fast': meaning his fast is absolutely invalid if he hesitates in the essence of intention due to lack of definiteness, for the pillar of intention—which is determination with definiteness—is missed. If he hesitates in the description while being definite in the essence of the fast, it is disliked (tanzihan). (3) His saying: 'otherwise no': meaning if it is not from Ramadan then I do not fast at all. [4] His saying: 'it is disliked': due to hesitating between disliked and not disliked, or between two disliked things. [5] His saying: 'if he intends': indicates that the phrase 'otherwise as nafl' stands in place of 'otherwise for another wajib'.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage presents additional rulings on the day of doubt concerning intention and the timing of breaking fast. First, for the common person (aammi) who is not khawass, the ruling is that he should wait until after zawal (noon) for news of the crescent; if no news arrives, he breaks his fast. This does not mean he nullifies a valid nafl fast, but rather that he holds off eating until noon. Second, three types of intention are discussed: (1) Conditional intention: 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan, I am fasting for it; if not, I am not fasting at all.' This intention is invalid (la sawma) because it lacks definiteness (jazm) in the essence of the intention—a necessary condition for validity. (2) Reservation with alternative: 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan, then for it; otherwise for another wajib; otherwise as nafl.' This is disliked (makruh) because it involves hesitation between categories. (3) The commentary clarifies that hesitation in the description (such as whether it is wajib or nafl) while being definite about fasting itself leads to dislike (tanzihi), not invalidity. The key principle is that intention must be definite (jazm) in its essence; hesitation invalidates. The text also notes that the structure 'otherwise as nafl' is equivalent to 'otherwise for another wajib', indicating the same ruling.

Key Terms
نية التردد (niyyat al-taraddud): Hesitant intention: an intention that is conditional or uncertain about the nature of the act.
العزم بالجزم (al-'azm bi al-jazm): Determination with definiteness: the necessary condition for a valid intention, meaning a firm, unambiguous resolve.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Valid
B) Invalid
C) Disliked
D) Makruh tahrimi

Key Takeaway
Conditional intention that lacks definiteness in essence invalidates the fast; hesitation in description is disliked but the fast is valid.

Intentions and Fasting on the Day of Doubt
Commentary on rulings of intention in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rulings on intention for fasting on the day of doubt and distinguish between conditional intention and alternative intention.

Core Idea
Conditional intention invalidates the fast; only firm resolve (jazm) makes the intention valid.

This lesson examines the rulings on fasting on the day of doubt (30th Sha'ban). The Hanafi school distinguishes between conditional intentions that void the fast and intentions with alternatives that are merely disliked. We also learn the ruling for common people who may confuse decisive and hesitant intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is fasting on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk), which is the 30th of Sha'ban when there is uncertainty about the start of Ramadan.
2
State the three rulings
From the passage: (1) Common people break fast after midday; it is not disliked for them to fast voluntarily but better not to. (2) Conditional intention (ta'liq) invalidates the fast. (3) Intention with an alternative (tardid in description) is disliked.
The commentary clarifies the basis for each ruling.
3
Understand the principle of firm resolve (jazm)
The pillar of intention (rukn al-niyyah) is firm resolve. Conditional intention lacks jazm, so it invalidates. Hesitation in description (e.g., whether it is Ramadan or voluntary) while firm in the act of fasting results in dislike.
The commentary distinguishes between hesitation in asl (essence) and wasf (description).
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a specific intention, determine if it is valid, invalid, or disliked based on the presence or absence of jazm and the type of hesitation.
Practice with examples from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويَفْطُرُ غيرُهم بعد الزوال ، ولا صومَ لو نَوَى : إن كان الغد من رمضان فأنا صائم عنه ، وإلا فلا ، وكُرِهَ لو نَوى إن كان الغد من رمضان، فأنا صائم عنه وإلا فعن واجب آخر، وإلا فعن نفل
( ويفطر غيرُهم بعد الزوال. [1] قوله : ويفطر؛ ليس معناه أن ينقض صومه إن صام نفلاً ، بل معناه أن العامي يتلوّم وينتظر إلى الزوال لوصول خبر الهلال ثمّ يأكل ويشرب، وهل يكره للعوام الصوم نفلاً؟ الأصح أنه لا يكره ، نعم ؛ الأفضل له عدم الصوم، وإنما فرق بين العامي والخاصي ؛ لأنَّ العامي لا يفرِّقُ بين نية الجزم وبين نية التردد.
[2] قوله : ولا صوم؛ أي لا يصح صومه مطلقاً إن ردَّدَ في أصل النية لعدم الجزم فيفوت ركن النية وهو العزم بالجزم، ولو ردَّدَ في الوصف مع الجزم بأصل الصوم: أي تنزيها.

والنية المعتبرة هاهنا : أن ينوي التطوع من لا يعتاد صوم ذلك اليوم على سبيل الجزم، ولا يرد في النية بين كونه نفلاً إن كان من شعبان، وفرضاً إن كان من رمضان، بل يجزم بنيته نفلاً محضاً، ولا يضرُّه خطور احتمال كونه من رمضان بعد جزمه بنية النفل؛ لأنه يصوم احتياطاً لذلك الاحتمال. كذا في الدر المختار و رد المحتار.
And others break their fast after midday, and there is no fasting if he intends: 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan then I am fasting for it, and if not then no fast.' And it is disliked if he intends: 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan then I am fasting for it, and if not then for another obligatory fast, and if not then for a voluntary fast.' (So he breaks his fast after midday.) And it is not the meaning that he nullifies his fast if he fasts voluntarily, but rather the meaning is that the common person waits and delays until midday for the arrival of the news of the crescent, then eats and drinks. And is it disliked for the common people to fast voluntarily? The correct view is that it is not disliked, but the better for him is not to fast. And the differentiation between the common person and the scholar is because the common person does not differentiate between a decisive intention and a hesitant intention. And as for his statement: 'And there is no fasting', meaning his fast is absolutely not valid if he hesitated in the essence of the intention due to lack of decisiveness, thus the pillar of intention is missed, which is firm resolve. And if he hesitated in the description while being decisive in the essence of the fast, then it is only disliked (tanzihan). And as for his statement: 'And if not then no fast', meaning if tomorrow is not from Ramadan then I do not fast at all. And as for his statement: 'And it is disliked', due to hesitation between disliked and not disliked, or between two disliked things. And as for his statement: 'That is, if he intends', it indicates that his statement 'and if not then for a voluntary fast' stands in place of 'and if not then for another obligatory fast'. And the valid intention here is that one who is not accustomed to fasting on that day intends supererogatory fasting with decisiveness, and does not fluctuate in intention between it being voluntary if it is from Sha'ban and obligatory if it is from Ramadan, but rather firmly resolves his intention as purely voluntary. And the occurrence of the possibility that it might be from Ramadan does not harm him after his firm resolve of voluntary intention, because he fasts as a precaution for that possibility. This is as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses rulings related to intention and fasting on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk), which is the 30th of Sha'ban when there is uncertainty whether the crescent for Ramadan has been sighted. The text first states that non-scholars (common people) break their fast after midday, meaning they wait until noon for news of the crescent, and then eat and drink. The commentator clarifies that this does not mean they nullify a voluntary fast; rather, it is about delaying to break the fast. The ruling is that it is not disliked for commoners to fast voluntarily on that day, but it is better for them not to fast because they may confuse decisive intention with hesitant intention. The second ruling states that a conditional intention (e.g., 'if tomorrow is Ramadan, I am fasting; if not, no fast') invalidates the fast entirely because it lacks the required firm resolve (al-jazm) in the intention. The third ruling says that if the intention is conditional but includes an alternative (e.g., if not Ramadan, then for another obligation or voluntary), it is disliked. The commentator explains that hesitation in the essence of intention nullifies the fast, but hesitation in the description (e.g., whether it is Ramadan or Sha'ban) while being decisive in the act of fasting only renders it dislikable (makruh tanzihan). The valid intention is to firmly resolve to fast voluntarily, ignoring the possibility that it might be Ramadan, as this is precautionary.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): The intention of the heart required for validity of fast; must be firm (jazm) and decisive, without hesitation.
جزم (jazm): Firm resolve and decisiveness in intention; the opposite of hesitation (taraddud). It is a condition for valid intention.
تردد (taraddud): Fluctuation or hesitation in intention; if in the essence of the act, it invalidates the intention; if in the description, it only makes it disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is disliked but valid.
D) It is recommended as a precaution.

Key Takeaway
Conditional intention invalidates the fast; only unconditional firm resolve ensures validity.

Ruling on Hesitant Intention Between Ramadan and Other Fasts
Commentary on rulings for fasting with hesitant intention between Ramadan and other fasts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on fasting with hesitant intention to determine whether the fast counts for Ramadan, another wajib, or nafl, depending on the actual day.

Core Idea
If you fast with hesitation between Ramadan and another obligation, it counts for Ramadan if it is Ramadan; otherwise it counts as voluntary.

This lesson explains a common scenario: you are unsure whether a day is Ramadan or a makeup fast, so you formulate a conditional intention. The Hanafi school provides specific rules to determine the validity of such a fast. Understanding this helps avoid breaking Ramadan unintentionally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of hesitation
Determine between which two types the person hesitates: Ramadan vs another wajib, or Ramadan vs nafl.
The ruling differs based on the second intention.
2
Determine the actual day
Is it ultimately Ramadan or not? This is known later through confirmation or calendar.
The actual day affects the outcome.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on specification
For wajib fasts, both specification (ta'yin) and firm resolve (jazm) are conditions. For nafl, absolute intention suffices. If the actual day is Ramadan, the hesitation is overridden by determination.
The extra description is cancelled when the actual day matches.
4
Derive the ruling
If actual is Ramadan, the fast counts for Ramadan in both scenarios. If not, in the first scenario it counts as nafl (not the other wajib), and in the second it counts as nafl.
Absolute intention remains, so the fast is valid as nafl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ظَهَرَ رمضانيّته كان عنه ، وإلا فنفل فيهما
(فإن ظهر رمضانيته كان عنه؛ لوجود مطلق النية، (وإلا فنفل فيهما): أي فيما قال: وإلا فعن واجب آخر، وفيما قال، وإلا فعن نفل. أما في الصورة الأولى؛ فلأنه متردد في الواجب الآخر، فلا يقع عنه؛ لأنه يشترط فيه التعيين مع الجزم، وإذا فقد ذلك بقي مطلق نية الصوم، وبه يتأدى النفل لا الواجب. وفي الثانية؛ لوجود مطلق النية أيضاً.
[1] قوله: فإن ظهر؛ أي فيما إذا صام متردّداً بين صوم رمضان وبين واجب آخر، أو بين صوم رمضان وبين نفل إن ظهر أنه من رمضان يقع صومه عنه؛ لوجود مطلق النية وإلغاء الوصف الزائد بسبب تعينه.
[2] قوله: فلا يقع عنه؛ لأنه يشترط فيه التعيين مع الجزم، وإذا فقد ذلك بقي مطلق نية الصوم، وبه يتأدى النفل لا الواجب.
[3] قوله: لوجود مطلق النية أيضاً؛ فإنّه إذا تردد بين الفرض والنفل لم يتعين أحدهما، فلغا التعيين وبقي الإطلاق.
ومن رأى هلال صوم أو فطر وحده يصوم، وإن رد قوله
(ومن رأى هلال صوم أو فطر وحده يصوم، وإن رد قوله): يعني إذا رأى مكلّف؛ أي عاقل بالغ مسلم هلال رمضان وحده، وشهد به عند الإمام، فردّ شهادته بدليل شرعي، وهو فسقه إن كانت السماء متغيمة، وغلطه، وعُدم تكامل العد. وإن كانت مصحية يصوم هو - أي وجوباً -؛ لقوله: فَمَن شَهِدَ مِنكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْهُ (البقرة 185)، وقد تحقق عنده وجوده برؤيته، وإن لم يقبل قوله لأمر ما. وكذا إذا رأى هلال فطر فلم تقبل شهادته لا يفطر هو، بل يصوم احتياطاً، فإنَّ ترك صوم من رمضان أشدّ من صوم يوم العيد، وكونه عيد، أو إن ثبت عنده برؤيته، لكن لما لم يقبله الإمام أورث ذلك شبهة، فينبغي الاحتياط.
Then if its Ramadan-ness becomes apparent, it is for it, and otherwise voluntary in both. So if its Ramadan-ness becomes apparent, it is for it, due to the existence of absolute intention. And otherwise voluntary in both, meaning in what he said: 'and if not then for another obligatory fast' and in what he said 'and if not then for voluntary.' As for the first case, because he is hesitant regarding the other obligatory fast, so it does not occur for it, because specification with firm resolve is required, and when that is lost, absolute intention of fasting remains, and by it the voluntary is performed, not the obligatory. And in the second, due to the existence of absolute intention as well. His statement: 'Then if it appears' meaning regarding when he fasts hesitant between Ramadan and another obligatory fast, or between Ramadan and voluntary, if it appears that it is Ramadan, his fast occurs for it, due to existence of absolute intention and cancellation of the extra description due to its determination. His statement: 'So it does not occur for it' because specification with firm resolve is required, and when that is lost, absolute intention of fasting remains, and by it the voluntary is performed, not the obligatory. His statement: 'Due to existence of absolute intention as well' because when he hesitates between obligatory and voluntary, neither is specified, so the specification is void and the absolute remains. And whoever sees the crescent of fasting or breaking fast alone, he fasts even if his statement is rejected. Meaning, if a responsible person (i.e., sane, adult, Muslim) sees the crescent of Ramadan alone and testifies before the imam, and the imam rejects his testimony due to a legal proof, such as his fisq (sinfulness) if the sky is cloudy, or his error, or lack of complete number of witnesses, and if the sky is clear, he fasts (obligatorily) due to His saying: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (Quran 2:185), since he is certain of its existence by his sight, even if his statement was not accepted for some reason. Similarly, if he sees the crescent of breaking fast (Eid) and his testimony is not accepted, he does not break fast, but rather fasts as a precaution, because abandoning a fast of Ramadan is more severe than fasting on the day of Eid, and its being Eid is not certain due to the rejection, so precaution is recommended.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the rulings of intention and crescent sighting. It first addresses the case of a person who fasts with hesitant intention between Ramadan and another obligation or voluntary fast. If it later becomes clear that the day was Ramadan, the fast counts for Ramadan because the absolute intention to fast exists, and the hesitation in description is overridden by the actual determination. However, if the hesitation is between Ramadan and another obligation, and it turns out not to be Ramadan, the fast does not count for that other obligation because specification (ta'yin) with firm resolve is required for obligatory fasts; instead, it counts as voluntary. If the hesitation was between Ramadan and voluntary, then it counts as voluntary in either case because absolute intention is present. The second major ruling concerns a person who sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal alone, i.e., without corroborating witnesses. Such a person is obligated to fast if the sky is clear, based on the Quranic verse 'Fa-man shahida minkum al-shahra falyasumhu' (2:185). His personal certainty through sighting imposes the obligation on him, even if the imam rejects his testimony due to legal reasons (like his own sinfulness if cloudy, or lack of completeness). For the crescent of Eid, if his testimony is rejected, he should not break his fast but continue fasting as a precaution, because the harm of breaking a Ramadan fast is greater than the harm of fasting on the prohibited day of Eid when uncertain.

Key Terms
هلال (hilal): The new crescent moon that marks the beginning of a lunar month, particularly for Ramadan and Shawwal.
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony given before a judge (qadi) regarding the sighting of the crescent moon.
نية (niyyah): Intention, a necessary condition for the validity of fasting. For wajib fasts, it must be specific and firm; for nafl, absolute intention suffices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person fasts with intention: 'If this is Ramadan, I am fasting Ramadan; if not, I am fasting a voluntary fast.' The day turns out not to be Ramadan. What is the ruling on his fast?
A) It counts as voluntary.
B) It counts as a missed Ramadan.
C) It is invalid.
D) It counts as voluntary only if he intended voluntary from the start.

Key Takeaway
A hesitant intention does not invalidate the fast; the actual day determines whether it counts as Ramadan or voluntary as long as absolute intention is present.

Ruling on One Who Sees the Crescent Alone
Commentary on the ruling for a solitary crescent seer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a person who sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal alone and explain why personal certainty imposes obligation despite rejection of testimony.

Core Idea
Personal certainty of the crescent sighting obligates fasting; rejection of testimony does not remove that obligation.

This lesson addresses a situation where a person sees the crescent moon of Ramadan or Shawwal but his testimony is rejected by the judge. Does he still have to fast? The Hanafi school holds that personal certainty through sighting makes fasting obligatory for Ramadan and prohibits breaking fast for Eid if testimony is rejected. The proof is the Quranic verse 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
The person sees the crescent alone (Ramadan or Shawwal) and testifies but is rejected.
Rejection may be due to lack of witnesses, fisq, or error.
2
Determine the legal proof
The Quranic verse 2:185 commands: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.'
The word 'shahida' includes personal sighting.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
Personal sighting creates certainty (yaqin) for the individual, which overrides rejection by the imam. The command is personal.
Yaqin is not removed by rejection.
4
Differentiate between Ramadan and Shawwal
For Ramadan, he must fast (wajib). For Shawwal, he does not break fast but continues as precaution (ihtiyat), because the harm of breaking a Ramadan fast is greater than fasting on Eid.
Precaution applies when there is doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ظَهَرَ رمضانيّته كان عنه ، وإلا فنفل فيهما
(فإن ظهر رمضانيته كان عنه؛ لوجود مطلق النية، (وإلا فنفل فيهما): أي فيما قال: وإلا فعن واجب آخر، وفيما قال، وإلا فعن نفل. أما في الصورة الأولى؛ فلأنه متردد في الواجب الآخر، فلا يقع عنه؛ لأنه يشترط فيه التعيين مع الجزم، وإذا فقد ذلك بقي مطلق نية الصوم، وبه يتأدى النفل لا الواجب. وفي الثانية؛ لوجود مطلق النية أيضاً.

No Kaffarah for Breaking the Fast: The Hanafi Position
Explaining the ruling that only qada' is required, not kaffarah, with the Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that only qada' is required when a single witness breaks his fast after sighting the Ramadan crescent, and explain why kaffarah is not due by applying the principle that hudud are repelled by doubts.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is dropped by doubt: the imam's rejection of a single witness creates shubhah.

This lesson examines a key point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: the obligation of kaffarah when one who has sighted the crescent alone breaks his fast. The Hanafi school holds that only qada' is required, not kaffarah, because the testimony of a single witness, though accepted for establishing the beginning of Ramadan, creates a legal doubt that repels the severe penalty of kaffarah. Understanding this ruling illustrates a fundamental Hanafi principle: penalties are averted by uncertainties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
The act is breaking the fast on a day that the individual believes to be Ramadan based on his own sighting of the crescent. The person is a single witness whose testimony might not be accepted by the imam for the general public.
This is not about breaking the fast after the end of Ramadan, but during it.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to the Hanafi school, if such a person breaks his fast, he must only make up that day (qada') and does not owe kaffarah (expiation).
3
Identify the Shafi'i opposition
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that since the sighting is confirmed for the individual, breaking the fast deliberately by intercourse incurs kaffarah just as on any confirmed Ramadan day.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle
The evidence for the Hanafi position is that the imam's rejection of the witness's testimony transforms the confirmed sighting into a doubtful matter (shubhah). The principle 'hudud and kaffarat are repelled by doubts' is applied.
This principle is derived from the hadith: 'Avert the hudud by doubts as much as you can'.

And if he breaks the fast, he must make it up, and it is accepted without a lawsuit, and the wording 'I bear witness' for fasting with cloud is the news of an individual, and if he breaks the fast, he must make it up) – mention of qada' only to show that there is no expiation upon him, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Then his saying: 'he broke the fast' means he invalidated it after having started it, and similarly if he did not start it. His saying: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' because he says: upon him is expiation if he invalidates it by intercourse, but he broke the fast in Ramadan due to its being confirmed in his view by his sighting. Our evidence: that the imam, when he rejected his testimony, caused doubt in his sighting, and such expiation is repelled by doubts. This is in the case of Ramadan; as for breaking the fast, there is no expiation because it is a day of Eid. His saying: 'without a lawsuit' means that it is not a condition that someone make a claim or that the seer say 'I bear witness to my sighting' because it is a religious matter similar to narration of hadith, and it is not among the rights of servants that require claim and testimony; therefore the statement of one whose testimony is not accepted is accepted here, such as a single woman, a slave, and one who has been flogged for slander, i.e. one who slandered someone of adultery and did not prove it, so he was flogged the flogging of slander, which is eighty lashes; indeed his testimony is never accepted in our view, even if he repents. His saying: 'with cloud' with fatha, meaning clouds, and similarly smoke, dust, and the like, which prevent complete sight. His saying: 'an individual' with fatha, meaning alone, for it is established that the Prophet ﷺ permitted the testimony of one person regarding Ramadan, reported by the authors of Sunan, and in al-Daraqutni with a weak chain: 'The Prophet ﷺ would not permit for breaking the fast except the testimony of two men.' (References). Then on the next page: on condition that he be upright, even if a slave, or a woman, or one flogged for slander who has repented, and for breaking the fast, two men or one man and two women are a condition, and the wording 'I bear witness' not the lawsuit, and without cloud, a large multitude is required in both. Then his saying: 'upright' means not openly sinful, and if his state is unknown, his statement is accepted, as in al-Hidayah. His saying: 'who has repented' for if he did not repent, he is sinful and his statement is not accepted in religious matters because of the verse: 'If a wicked person comes to you, verify.' His saying: 'and the wording I bear witness' – the requisite number of witnesses is stipulated, and the wording 'I bear witness' because it contains an element of glorification. There is disagreement on the number of witnesses when there is no obstruction in the sky: first view: a multitude that reason deems incapable of collusion on a lie, transmitted from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and they should be from every direction; this is indicated by the commentator's words, and chosen by the author of al-Fath. Second: a multitude that gives predominant probability; chosen by the author of al-Idah. Third: two suffice, a narration from Abu Hanifah due to people's laziness, chosen by the author of al-Bahr. Fourth: fifty men like the oath of qasamah, transmitted from Abu Yusuf. Fifth: the inhabitants of a locality. Sixth: not limited to a number, left to the imam's opinion due to variation in people's truthfulness, transmitted from Muhammad, deemed correct by the author of al-Ikhtiyar. Seventh: five hundred in Balkh, few, transmitted from Khalaf ibn Ayyub. Eighth: a thousand, transmitted from Abu Hafs al-Kabir.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Quduri regarding the rulings of testimony concerning the crescent of Ramadan. The author explains that if a person sees the crescent and thus begins the fast, but later breaks it, he must make up that day (qada') but there is no expiation (kaffarah). This contradicts al-Shafi'i, who holds that if one breaks the fast by intercourse after a confirmed sighting, kaffarah is due. The Hanafi argument is that since the imam rejected the witness's testimony (due to being a single witness), that creates doubt (shubhah) about the sighting, and kaffarah is dropped by doubts. Then the commentary discusses that for the start of Ramadan, the testimony of one upright (ʿadl) witness is accepted without any lawsuit (daʿwa) being required, because it is a religious matter akin to hadith narration, not a legal claim between people. Thus even a woman, a slave, or a person who had been flogged for qadhf (false accusation of zina) — if he repents — can have their testimony accepted for sighting the moon. The wording "ashhadu" (I bear witness) is required for the formal testimony. When the sky is cloudy (ghaym), one witness suffices; when the sky is clear, a large number of witnesses (jamʿun ʿaẓīm) is required, though there is ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars on the exact number. For the end of Ramadan (the sighting of Shawwal), two men or one man and two women are required, and the wording "ashhadu" is also needed. The commentary also notes that the required number for clear skies is a matter of multiple views within the madhhab, ranging from two to a thousand, with the preferred view being to leave it to the imam's discretion or to require a multitude that precludes collusion.

Key Terms
عدل (ʿadl): A person who is upright, not openly sinful; his testimony is accepted for religious matters.
غيم (ghaym): Cloud cover that obstructs clear vision of the sky.
فرد (fard): A single individual; here refers to one witness.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina (adultery/fornication).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is kaffarah not due when a single witness breaks his fast after sighting the Ramadan crescent and the imam rejects his testimony?
A) Because the sighting was not confirmed by a judge
B) Because the imam's rejection creates doubt, and penalties are repelled by doubts
C) Because the fast was invalid from the start
D) Because kaffarah is only due for breaking a confirmed Ramadan day with certainty

Key Takeaway
When a rejected single witness breaks his fast, only qada' is due because kaffarah is repelled by the doubt created by the imam's rejection.

Conditions for Accepting Testimony for Ramadan Crescent
Detailing the conditions for accepting witness testimony for Ramadan crescent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions for accepting testimony for the beginning and end of Ramadan, including the requirements of ʿadalah, wording, and number of witnesses for cloudy vs clear skies.

Core Idea
One ʿadl witness suffices for Ramadan start when cloudy; for end, two men or one man two women.

This lesson focuses on the detailed rules for accepting witness testimony regarding the sighting of the Ramadan crescent. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the beginning and end of Ramadan, and between cloudy and clear skies. Additionally, special allowances are made for witnesses who are normally disqualified in court cases, such as women, slaves, and those punished for qadhf after repentance. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly implementing the collective obligation of witnessing the moon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between testimony for the start of Ramadan (al-sawm) and for the end (al-fitr). Different numbers of witnesses are required.
Also note the distinction between cloudy (ghaym) and clear skies (bila ghaym).
2
List the conditions for start with cloud
For the start of Ramadan when the sky is cloudy, one upright (ʿadl) witness suffices. The witness must use the wording 'ashhadu' (I bear witness). No lawsuit (daʿwa) is required.
The witness can be a woman, a slave, or one flogged for qadhf after repentance.
3
List the conditions for end of Ramadan
For the end of Ramadan (sighting of Shawwal), two men or one man and two women are required, also with the wording 'ashhadu'.
Cloudy vs clear sky also affects the number: without cloud, a large multitude is required.
4
Explain the special acceptance of disqualified witnesses
In religious matters like moon sighting, the statement of one whose testimony is not accepted in court (woman, slave, repentant qadhf) is accepted because it is akin to hadith narration, not a legal claim.
The condition of ʿadalah is required, but a woman and slave can be ʿadl.

Then his saying: 'he broke the fast' means he invalidated it after having started it, and similarly if he did not start it. His saying: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' because he says: upon him is expiation if he invalidates it by intercourse, but he broke the fast in Ramadan due to its being confirmed in his view by his sighting. Our evidence: that the imam, when he rejected his testimony, caused doubt in his sighting, and such expiation is repelled by doubts. This is in the case of Ramadan; as for breaking the fast, there is no expiation because it is a day of Eid. His saying: 'without a lawsuit' means that it is not a condition that someone make a claim or that the seer say 'I bear witness to my sighting' because it is a religious matter similar to narration of hadith, and it is not among the rights of servants that require claim and testimony; therefore the statement of one whose testimony is not accepted is accepted here, such as a single woman, a slave, and one who has been flogged for slander, i.e. one who slandered someone of adultery and did not prove it, so he was flogged the flogging of slander, which is eighty lashes; indeed his testimony is never accepted in our view, even if he repents. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Quduri regarding the rulings of testimony concerning the crescent of Ramadan. The author explains that if a person sees the crescent and thus begins the fast, but later breaks it, he must make up that day (qada') but there is no expiation (kaffarah). غيم (ghaym): Cloud cover that obstructs clear vision of the sky.
فرد (fard): A single individual; here refers to one witness.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Witness Numbers for Clear Sky Sighting
Presenting multiple Hanafi views on witness count for moon sighting under clear skies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list at least four opinions on the required number of witnesses for moon sighting on a clear night, and state the mufta bihi view preferred by later Hanafi scholars.

Core Idea
For a clear sky, witness numbers range from two to a thousand; the preferred view leaves it to the imam's discretion.

When the sky is clear and there is no obstruction, the Hanafi school has multiple opinions on how many witnesses are needed to confirm the crescent. The passage records eight different views, ranging from two to a thousand, with some scholars leaving the number to the imam's judgment. This lesson unpacks these opinions, their bases, and which view is considered the most sound (asahh) in later scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario is when the sky is clear (bila ghaym), meaning no cloud, smoke, or dust obstructs the view of the crescent.
This applies to both the start and end of Ramadan.
2
List the eight opinions
The passage lists eight views: (1) a multitude that reason deems incapable of collusion, from each direction; (2) a multitude that gives predominant probability; (3) two witnesses, from Abu Hanifa; (4) fifty men like qasamah; (5) inhabitants of a locality; (6) not limited, left to imam's discretion; (7) five hundred; (8) a thousand.
Some views are attributed to specific scholars like Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Abu Hanifa.
3
Identify the mufta bihi view
The text mentions that the view of leaving it to the imam's discretion (view 6) is deemed correct (asahh) by the author of al-Ikhtiyar and preferred by others. This is the relied-upon position.
Other later sources like al-Durr al-Muntaqa also consider this the most sound.

اختلف في عدد الشهود إن لم يكن في السماء علة على أقوال: الأول : جمع يحكم العقل بعدم تواطئهم على الكذب، وهو مروي عن أبي يوسف ومحمد وأن يكونوا من كل جانب. وإليه يشير كلام الشارح، واختاره صاحب «الفتح»(٢ : ٢٥٢)، و درر الحکام»(۱ : ۲۰۰). والثاني : جمع يحصل بهم غلبة الظن. وهو اختيار صاحب (الايضاح (ق۱/۳۱). والثالث : يكفي اثنان وهي رواية عن أبي حنيفة ؛ لتكاسل الناس، وهو اختيار صاحب «البحر»(ص۲۸۹)، و(رد المحتار» (۲ : ۹۳). والرابع : خمسون رجلا كالقسامة ، وهو مروي عن أبي يوسف . والخامس : أهل محلة. والسادس : غير مقدر بعدد، وهو مفوّض إلى رأي الإمام ؛ لتفاوت الناس صدقاً، مروي عن محمد له، وصححه صاحب الاختيار » (۱): ١٦٧). وفي المواهب» (ق٥٦/ب)، والدر المنتقى» (۱) : (٢٣٦) : هو الأصح ، واختاره صاحب التنوير»(۲ : ۹۲). والسابع : خمسمئة ببلخ قليل وهو مروي عن خلف بن أيوب. والثامن: ألف، وهو مروي عن أبي حفص الكبير.
And if he breaks the fast, he must make it up, and it is accepted without a lawsuit, and the wording 'I bear witness' for fasting with cloud is the news of an individual, and if he breaks the fast, he must make it up) – mention of qada' only to show that there is no expiation upon him, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And it is accepted without a lawsuit, and the wording 'I bear witness' for fasting with cloud is the news of an individual. Then his saying: 'he broke the fast' means he invalidated it after having started it, and similarly if he did not start it. His saying: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' because he says: upon him is expiation if he invalidates it by intercourse, but he broke the fast in Ramadan due to its being confirmed in his view by his sighting. Our evidence: that the imam, when he rejected his testimony, caused doubt in his sighting, and such expiation is repelled by doubts. This is in the case of Ramadan; as for breaking the fast, there is no expiation because it is a day of Eid. His saying: 'without a lawsuit' means that it is not a condition that someone make a claim or that the seer say 'I bear witness to my sighting' because it is a religious matter similar to narration of hadith, and it is not among the rights of servants that require claim and testimony; therefore the statement of one whose testimony is not accepted is accepted here, such as a single woman, a slave, and one who has been flogged for slander, i.e. one who slandered someone of adultery and did not prove it, so he was flogged the flogging of slander, which is eighty lashes; indeed his testimony is never accepted in our view, even if he repents. His saying: 'with cloud' with fatha, meaning clouds, and similarly smoke, dust, and the like, which prevent complete sight. His saying: 'an individual' with fatha, meaning alone, for it is established that the Prophet ﷺ permitted the testimony of one person regarding Ramadan, reported by the authors of Sunan, and in al-Daraqutni with a weak chain: 'The Prophet ﷺ would not permit for breaking the fast except the testimony of two men.' (References). Then on the next page: on condition that he be upright, even if a slave, or a woman, or one flogged for slander who has repented, and for breaking the fast, two men or one man and two women are a condition, and the wording 'I bear witness' not the lawsuit, and without cloud, a large multitude is required in both. Then his saying: 'upright' means not openly sinful, and if his state is unknown, his statement is accepted, as in al-Hidayah. His saying: 'who has repented' for if he did not repent, he is sinful and his statement is not accepted in religious matters because of the verse: 'If a wicked person comes to you, verify.' His saying: 'and the wording I bear witness' – the requisite number of witnesses is stipulated, and the wording 'I bear witness' because it contains an element of glorification. There is disagreement on the number of witnesses when there is no obstruction in the sky: first view: a multitude that reason deems incapable of collusion on a lie, transmitted from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and they should be from every direction; this is indicated by the commentator's words, and chosen by the author of al-Fath. Second: a multitude that gives predominant probability; chosen by the author of al-Idah. Third: two suffice, a narration from Abu Hanifah due to people's laziness, chosen by the author of al-Bahr. Fourth: fifty men like the oath of qasamah, transmitted from Abu Yusuf. Fifth: the inhabitants of a locality. Sixth: not limited to a number, left to the imam's opinion due to variation in people's truthfulness, transmitted from Muhammad, deemed correct by the author of al-Ikhtiyar. Seventh: five hundred in Balkh, few, transmitted from Khalaf ibn Ayyub. Eighth: a thousand, transmitted from Abu Hafs al-Kabir.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Quduri regarding the rulings of testimony concerning the crescent of Ramadan. The author explains that if a person sees the crescent and thus begins the fast, but later breaks it, he must make up that day (qada') but there is no expiation (kaffarah). This contradicts al-Shafi'i, who holds that if one breaks the fast by intercourse after a confirmed sighting, kaffarah is due. The Hanafi argument is that since the imam rejected the witness's testimony (due to being a single witness), that creates doubt (shubhah) about the sighting, and kaffarah is dropped by doubts. Then the commentary discusses that for the start of Ramadan, the testimony of one upright (ʿadl) witness is accepted without any lawsuit (daʿwa) being required, because it is a religious matter akin to hadith narration, not a legal claim between people. Thus even a woman, a slave, or a person who had been flogged for qadhf (false accusation of zina) — if he repents — can have their testimony accepted for sighting the moon. The wording "ashhadu" (I bear witness) is required for the formal testimony. When the sky is cloudy (ghaym), one witness suffices; when the sky is clear, a large number of witnesses (jamʿun ʿaẓīm) is required, though there is ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars on the exact number. For the end of Ramadan (the sighting of Shawwal), two men or one man and two women are required, and the wording "ashhadu" is also needed. The commentary also notes that the required number for clear skies is a matter of multiple views within the madhhab, ranging from two to a thousand, with the preferred view being to leave it to the imam's discretion or to require a multitude that precludes collusion.

Key Terms
عدل (ʿadl): A person who is upright, not openly sinful; his testimony is accepted for religious matters.
غيم (ghaym): Cloud cover that obstructs clear vision of the sky.
جمع عظيم (jamʿ ʿaẓīm): A large multitude; a number that precludes collusion on a lie.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina (adultery/fornication).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which view on the number of witnesses for a clear sky is considered the most sound (al-asahh)?
A) A multitude that reason deems incapable of collusion
B) Two witnesses
C) Fifty men like qasamah
D) Not limited, left to the imam's discretion

Key Takeaway
For clear sky moon sighting, the Hanafi school has multiple opinions; the preferred view leaves the number of witnesses to the imam's discretion.
