Injections Under the Skin Do Not Break the Fast
Fatwa on subcutaneous injections not breaking fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a substance breaks the fast according to the Hanafi school and apply it to determine that subcutaneous injections do not break the fast.

Core Idea
A fast is broken only when something reaches the stomach through a normal body opening.

Modern medical procedures like injections raise questions about fasting. This fatwa by the Hanafi scholar Muhammad Bakheet clarifies the principle: the fast is only invalidated if a substance reaches the internal cavity (jawf) through the usual passages. Since an injection under the skin does not use the mouth, nose, or other typical openings, it does not break the fast — even if the injected material is nutritious.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for breaking the fast
From the fatwa, locate the key condition: a substance must reach the stomach (jawf) through normal openings (manafidh mu'tada). Write this as a conditional statement.
The condition is two-part: reaching the jawf AND entering through a usual passage.
2
Analyze the pathway of a subcutaneous injection
Consider how a needle is inserted under the skin. Does it go through the mouth, nose, or any other typical opening? No. It penetrates pores, which are not considered normal passages. Therefore, the condition is not met.
The fatwa explicitly states that pores are not customarily used for reaching the stomach.
3
Conclude the ruling
Since the injection does not meet the condition, it does not break the fast. The ruling applies to any injection under the skin (intramuscular, subcutaneous) regardless of location on the body.
The fatwa covers upper arms, thighs, buttocks, and any outer body part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما حكم الاحتقان في العضدين أو غيره ، فقد أفتى الشيخ العلامة محمد بخيت : إن شرط المفطر أن يصل إلى الجوف وان يستقر فيه والمراد بذلك أن يدخل إلى الجوف ولا يكون طرفه خارج الجوف ولا متصلا بشيء خارج عن الجوف وأن يكون الوصول إلى الجوف من المنافذ المعتادة ; لأن المسام ونحوها من المنافذ التي لم تجر العادة بأن يصل منها شيء إلى الجوف، ومن ذلك يعلم أن الاحتقان بالحقن المعروف الآن عملها تحت الجلد سواء كان ذلك في العضدين أو الفخذين أو رأس الإليتين أو في أي موضع من ظاهر البدن غير مفسد للصوم ; لأن مثل هذه الحقنة لا يصل منها شيء إلى الجوف من المنافذ المعتادة أصلاً وعلى فرض الوصول، فإنما تصل من المسام فقط وما تصل إليه ليس جوفاً ولا في حكم الجوف. والله أعلم . ينظر : (الفتاوى الإسلامية»(۱ : ۹۰). منحة السلوك (٢ : ١٧٥).
As for the ruling of injection in the upper arms or elsewhere, the eminent scholar Muhammad Bakheet issued a fatwa: The condition for something to break the fast is that it reaches the stomach and settles there. What is meant by that is that it enters the stomach, its end is not outside the stomach, and it is not connected to anything outside the stomach, and the reaching to the stomach must be through the usual openings. Because pores and similar openings are not customarily used for something to reach the stomach. From this, it is known that injections under the skin, whether in the upper arms, thighs, buttocks, or any part of the outer body, do not invalidate the fast. Because such an injection does not reach the stomach through the usual openings at all. And even if it did reach, it would only reach through pores, and what it reaches is not the stomach nor is it considered like the stomach. And Allah knows best. See: al-Fatawa al-Islamiyyah (1:90); Minhat al-Suluk (2:175).
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This is a modern fatwa appended to the sharh, addressing injections during fasting. The fatwa is by the scholar Muhammad Bakheet. It defines the condition for something to break the fast: it must reach the stomach (jawf) through the usual openings (manafidh mu'tada). Since an injection under the skin is given into the muscle or tissue, it does not go through the mouth, nose, or other normal passages. It only enters through pores, which are not considered usual pathways. Therefore, such injections do not break the fast. The fatwa covers all types of injections (intramuscular, subcutaneous) in any part of the body (upper arms, thighs, buttocks). This is a practical ruling for medical needs.

Key Terms
احتقان (ihtiqaan): Injection; specifically, the administration of a substance under the skin or into a muscle.
جوف (jawf): The stomach or internal cavity; the place where food and drink normally settle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa, why does an injection under the skin not break the fast?
A) Because it does not reach the stomach through the usual openings
B) Because it is not food or drink
C) Because the amount is small
D) Because it is done for medical reasons

Key Takeaway
A subcutaneous injection does not break the fast because it does not reach the stomach via normal body openings; the Hanafi condition focuses on pathway, not nutrition.

Rain and Snow Entering the Throat During Fasting
States the ruling that rain/snow invalidates fasting and presents the ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rain/snow entering the throat during fasting and explain the intra-madhhab dispute and the rationale for the majority view.

Core Idea
Rain/snow invalidates fasting because it is avoidable — closing the mouth prevents entry, unlike dust.

Fasting requires guarding the stomach from anything that reaches through the mouth. But what about elements like rain or snow that may enter involuntarily? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether such entry breaks the fast, and why the majority rule that it does.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
The passage begins by stating the relied-upon ruling: rain and snow entering the throat invalidate the fast. This is the view of the majority of Hanafi scholars.
The phrase 'في الأصح' indicates this is the most correct opinion.
2
Identify the minority view
Some Hanafi scholars held that rain/snow does not invalidate the fast. Their reasoning is not detailed but likely based on it not being food or drink.
The minority is unnamed but represents a valid ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Understand the key distinction: avoidability
The majority's proof is that one can guard against rain/snow by closing the mouth. This distinguishes it from dust or smoke, which are unavoidable and do not invalidate.
The term 'التحرز' (guarding) is critical: if avoidance is possible, failure to do so is negligence.
4
Compare with similar cases
Think of other things that enter the mouth: dust (unavoidable) does not invalidate; water during rinsing (avoidable if one is careful) would invalidate if swallowed intentionally. Rain/snow is like water in that it can be prevented.
This analogy reinforces the majority's logic.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The author confirms that the majority view is the most correct (al-asahh) and the one relied upon for fatwa, as cited in later Hanafi works.
Thus, the operative ruling is that rain/snow invalidates and requires qada (make-up fast).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمطر والثلج يفسد في الأصح. اختلفوا في المطر والثلج لو دخلا في الحلق: فقال بعضهم: لا يفسد. وقال عامتهم بإفسادهما؛ لإمكان التحرز عنهما بضم الفم، وهو الأصح كما في «الملتقى» وشرحه «مجمع الأنهر» (١: ٢٤٥)، و«غنية ذوي الأحكام» (١: ٢٠٤)، وغيرها.
And rain and snow invalidate, according to the most correct opinion. They differed concerning rain and snow if they enter the throat: some said it does not invalidate. The majority said they invalidate, because it is possible to guard against them by closing the mouth, and this is the most correct, as in 'Al-Multaqa' and its commentary 'Majma' al-Anhur' (1:245) and 'Ghunyat Dhawi al-Ahkam' (1:204) and others.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether rain or snow entering the throat invalidates fasting. The base ruling states that rain and snow do invalidate, which is the relied-upon position. The author then reports an ikhtilaf: some scholars held it does not invalidate, but the majority (including the author's preferred view) rule that it does invalidate. The rationale is that unlike dust or a fly, one can easily avoid rain or snow by closing the mouth. Since protection is possible, failure to do so results in invalidation. This reasoning distinguishes it from unavoidable things like dust or smoke, which do not invalidate because they cannot be avoided. The correct view (al-sahih) is that of the majority, as confirmed by later Hanafi works.

Key Terms
الأصح (al-asahh): The most correct opinion among the Hanafi scholars on this issue.
التحرز (al-taharrur): Guarding against something; here, the ability to prevent rain/snow from entering by closing the mouth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does rain or snow invalidate fasting according to the majority of Hanafis?
A) Because it is food or drink
B) Because it enters through the mouth and one can guard against it
C) Because it is impure
D) Because it breaks the intention

Key Takeaway
Rain and snow entering the throat invalidate the fast because they are avoidable by closing the mouth.

Seven Actions That Do Not Invalidate Fasting
Explains why each of seven actions does not invalidate fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state each of the seven actions that do not invalidate fasting and explain the Hanafi principle that no substance reaches the internal cavity through an open passage.

Core Idea
No entry through a natural passage means no invalidation—the Hanafi definition of muftir.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lists seven actions or states that do not break the fast. Each is followed by a brief legal reason (ta'lil). The rulings are not based on mere tradition but on a consistent principle: invalidation requires the arrival of a substance into the internal cavity (al-batin) through a natural open passage (al-manfadh). Understanding this principle helps distinguish between actions that invalidate and those that do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven items
List each action or state mentioned: ejaculation by looking/thinking, oiling the body, applying kohl, backbiting, vomiting, remaining junub, and pouring oil into urethra.
These are all cases where the fast remains valid.
2
For each, extract the reason
Pay attention to the reason given: for ejaculation, lack of intercourse; for oil/kohl, absence of substance reaching interior via a passage; for backbiting, it is a tongue-sin unrelated to physical intake; for vomiting, the hadith distinction; for janabah, no new invalidator; for urethral oil, no passage to the cavity.
Some reasons are based on analogical reasoning (qiyas) and some on direct textual evidence.
3
Identify the underlying principle
Combine the reasons into one principle: an invalidator must involve a substance reaching the internal cavity through a natural open passage (manfadh). Pores do not count.
This principle is unique to the Hanafi school and explains why smelling perfume does not invalidate.
4
Apply the principle to a similar case
Consider if inhaling smoke from incense breaks the fast. According to the principle, it does not, because smoke reaches the body through pores or inhalation through the mouth (which is a passage) but the passage is open; however, the principle requires a substantial substance. This is a debated issue; the Hanafi position is that it does not invalidate unless the smoke is thick enough to collect in the stomach.
This step trains you to use the principle in novel scenarios.
5
Contrast with actions that do invalidate
Recall that eating, drinking, and intentional ejaculation through direct contact invalidate because they involve either substance entry or 'the meaning of intercourse'.
The exception for kissing/touching shows that the principle is not purely physical; it also considers the act's meaning (ma'na).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الإنزال بالنظر والفكر؛ فلعدم وجود المفطر وهو الجماع لا حقيقةً ولا معنى، بخلاف ما إذا قَبَّلَ ولَمَسَ بشهوة فأنزلَ فإنه يفطر؛ لوجود معنى الجماع. وأما في الادهان والاكتحال؛ فلأنه لا يصل فيه شيء إلى الباطن إلا الأثر، وإن وصل شيء من الكحل إلى الحلق فمن المسامات، فإنه ليس بين العين وبينه منفذ، والمفطر إنما هو وصول شيءٍ بعينه من منفذ إلى الباطن؛ ولذا لا يفطر شم العطر ونحوه. وأما في الغيبة؛ فلأنه إثم متعلّق باللسان، ولا تعلق لمثل هذه الآثام بإفساد الصوم. وأما في القيء فلحديث: «من قاء فلا قضاء عليه، ومن استقاء عمداً فعليه القضاء»، أخرجه أصحاب السنن. وأما في بقاء الجنابة السابقة حال الصوم فلعدم وجود مفطر فيه، وإن بقي جنباً كل اليوم نعم، يكون آثماً. وأما في صب الدهن في الإحليل؛ فلأنه ليس بين المثانة وبين الجوف منفذ يصل به إليه حتى يوجد المفطر.
As for ejaculation by looking and thinking: because there is no invalidating action, that is, intercourse neither in reality nor in meaning, unlike if he kissed and touched with desire and ejaculated, then he breaks fast because the meaning of intercourse exists. As for oiling and applying kohl: because nothing reaches the interior except the trace, and if something of kohl reaches the throat it is from pores, for there is no passage between the eye and it, and the invalidator is only the arrival of something specific through a passage to the interior; hence smelling perfume and the like does not break fast. As for backbiting: because it is a sin related to the tongue, and such sins have no connection to invalidating fasting. As for vomiting: due to the hadith: 'Whoever vomits, there is no qada upon him, and whoever induces vomiting intentionally, then qada is upon him,' reported by the authors of the Sunan. As for remaining in a state of janabah during fasting: because there is no invalidating action in it, even if he remains junub the whole day; yes, he is sinful. As for pouring oil into the urethra: because there is no passage between the bladder and the internal cavity through which it could reach so that an invalidator would exist.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage lists seven actions or states that do NOT invalidate fasting, each with its proof. The rulings are: (1) Ejaculation by mere looking or thinking does not invalidate because it lacks both the reality and the meaning of intercourse – unlike kissing or touching with desire which do invalidate because they include the meaning of intercourse. (2) Applying oil to the body or kohl to the eyes does not invalidate because nothing substantial reaches the internal cavity; any trace that reaches the throat does so through pores, not through an open passage. The general principle is that an invalidator must reach the interior through a natural open passage. (3) Backbiting (ghiba) is a sin but does not invalidate the fast; it is an offense of the tongue that does not break the fast. (4) Vomiting does not invalidate if unintentional; the hadith distinguishes between vomiting involuntarily (no qada) and inducing vomiting intentionally (qada required). (5) Remaining in a state of janabah (major ritual impurity) does not invalidate the fast, though it is sinful to delay ghusl. (6) Pouring oil into the urethra does not invalidate because there is no direct passage from the bladder to the internal cavity. These rulings are all based on the core principle that invalidation occurs only when a substance enters the internal cavity through a natural open passage.

Key Terms
المفطر (al-muftir): That which invalidates fasting; defined as the arrival of a specific substance through a natural passage to the internal cavity.
المنفذ (al-manfadh): A natural open passage into the body, such as the mouth, nose, ears, or urethra; pores are not considered manfadh.
الباطن (al-batin): The internal cavity of the body (jawf), such as the stomach or intestines; reaching it via a passage invalidates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does applying kohl to the eyes not invalidate fasting according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because kohl is not food or drink
B) Because it does not reach the internal cavity through an open passage
C) Because the amount is negligible
D) Because it is applied only externally

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: an action invalidates fasting only when a substance enters the internal cavity through a natural open passage, explaining why these seven actions do not break the fast.

Non-Vaginal Intimate Contact and Fasting
States the ruling for non-vaginal intimate contact during fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a fasting person engages in non-vaginal intimate contact with a corpse, animal, or other body part, and determine whether qada is required based on ejaculation.

Core Idea
Non-vaginal intimate contact invalidates fasting only if ejaculation occurs.

This lesson focuses on the first ruling in the passage: if a fasting person has intercourse with a corpse or an animal, or engages in other non-vaginal acts like kissing or touching, the fast is only invalidated if ejaculation takes place. Without ejaculation, the fast remains valid and no qada is required. The reasoning is that actual intercourse involves insertion, while these acts are incomplete unless accompanied by ejaculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts
List the specific acts mentioned: intercourse with a corpse, with an animal, non-vaginal intercourse, kissing, and touching.
2
Note the condition
The ruling hinges on whether ejaculation occurs. If it does, qada is required; if not, no qada.
3
Understand the reasoning
The commentary explains that these acts lack the reality of intercourse (insertion into a desired vagina). Ejaculation provides the meaning of intercourse, thus invalidating the fast. Without it, no invalidation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وطئ ميتة، أو بهيمة، أو غير فرج أو قبل، أو لمس، إن أنزل قضى، وإلا فلا. وإن أكل لحماً بين أسنانه مثل حمصة قضى فقط، وفي أقل منها لا إلا إذا أخرجه وأخذه بيده، ثم أكل، ولو بدأ بأكل سمسمة فسد إلا إذا مضغ، وقيء كثير عاد، أو أعيد يفسد، لا القليل في الحالتين، وعند محمد له يفسد بإعادة القليل لا عود الكثير. [1] قوله: إن أنزل قضى؛ لأن هذه الصور ليس فيها الجماع حقيقة، فإنه عبارة عن إيلاج ذكر في فرج مشتهى، فإن أنزل وجد معناه فيفسد، وإلا فلا. [2] قوله: وفي أقل منها لا؛ الأصل فيه أن المانع من الإفطار هو ما لا يسهل الاحتراز عنه، وذلك هو القليل بين الأسنان والمتلاشي من السمسمة في الريق، وما ليس كذلك فهو مفسد. [3] قوله: اتفاقاً؛ أي ليس باحترازي، فإن المقصود وإن أكله بعد إخراجه مفسد أخذه باليد أو بالعود أو بغير ذلك. [4] قوله: يتلاشى؛ أي يفنى ويفقد باختلاطه بالريق. [5] قوله: يعتبر الصنع، بالضم: أي الفعل، فإن صورة الفطر وهو الابتلاع إنما تتحقق إذا تحقق فعله، ولأبي يوسف: أنه خارج، فبعوده يوجد الدخول من خارج.
And if he has intercourse with a corpse, or an animal, or other than the vagina (i.e., between the thighs), or kisses, or touches, if he ejaculates he makes qada, otherwise not. And if he eats meat between his teeth equivalent to a chickpea, he only makes qada, and for less than that, no qada unless he takes it out and takes it in his hand, then eats. And if he begins to eat a sesame seed it invalidates unless he chews it (so it disintegrates). And vomit that returns or is re-swallowed: if much, it invalidates; if little, it does not invalidate in both cases. According to Muhammad, re-swallowing little invalidates, but return of much does not. [Commentary: ...]
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage contains several rulings about what invalidates fasting, followed by a commentary (sharh) that explains each point. The matn (base text) states: (1) If a fasting person has intercourse with a corpse, an animal, or in a non-vaginal location (e.g., between thighs), or kisses or touches with desire, then if ejaculation occurs, qada is required; otherwise no qada. This is because actual intercourse (insertion into a desired vagina) is missing in reality, but if ejaculation occurs, the meaning (effect) of intercourse is present. (2) Eating meat stuck between teeth: if the piece is the size of a chickpea (hummusa) or more, qada is required; if smaller, no qada unless he takes it out with his hand (or similar) and then eats it. The principle is that what is difficult to avoid (small bits) does not invalidate; what is easy to avoid (larger or taken out) invalidates. (3) Starting to eat a sesame seed: if he puts it in his mouth and eats it as is, it invalidates; but if he chews it first, it disintegrates and mixes with saliva, thus not invalidating. (4) Vomit: if a mouthful of vomit returns to the stomach involuntarily, or if he deliberately re-swallows it, it invalidates (if much). Little vomit does not invalidate in either case. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Muhammad holds that re-swallowing little vomit invalidates (because it is an act of swallowing), while the return of much vomit does not invalidate (because it was not an act of swallowing). The commentary clarifies that 'al-san' (action) is the criterion for Muhammad, whereas Abu Yusuf focuses on the substance entering from outside.

Key Terms
قضى (qada): To make up the fast (qada) after it has been invalidated; here used for cases requiring compensation.
حمصة (hummusa): A chickpea; used as a measure for the threshold of what invalidates when eating food residue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fasting person touches his wife with desire but does not ejaculate, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is invalid and qada is required.
B) The fast remains valid and no qada is required.
C) The fast is invalid and both qada and kaffarah are required.
D) The fast is valid only if he performed wudu afterwards.

Key Takeaway
Non-vaginal intimate contact only invalidates fasting if ejaculation occurs; without it, the fast is valid.

Eating Meat Between Teeth: Size Threshold
States the ruling for eating meat between teeth during fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi threshold for when eating meat stuck between teeth invalidates fasting, distinguishing between pieces the size of a chickpea or larger and smaller pieces.

Core Idea
Meat between teeth invalidates fasting only if it is the size of a chickpea or more, unless taken out and then eaten.

This lesson covers the ruling about meat stuck between teeth. The Hanafi school sets a clear threshold using a common food item, the chickpea. Understanding this measure helps determine when eating such residue requires qada. The key principle is that small residues difficult to avoid are excused, but if the piece is sizeable or deliberately taken out and eaten, the fast becomes invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the size threshold
The measure is a chickpea (حمصة). If the meat is that size or larger, eating it invalidates and requires qada.
2
Distinguish small pieces
If smaller than a chickpea, it does not invalidate, unless the person takes it out with his hand and then deliberately eats it, in which case it does invalidate.
3
Understand the principle
The commentary explains that small residues are difficult to avoid, so they are excused. Taking it out makes it avoidable, so eating it invalidates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وطئ ميتة، أو بهيمة، أو غير فرج أو قبل، أو لمس، إن أنزل قضى، وإلا فلا. وإن أكل لحماً بين أسنانه مثل حمصة قضى فقط، وفي أقل منها لا إلا إذا أخرجه وأخذه بيده، ثم أكل، ولو بدأ بأكل سمسمة فسد إلا إذا مضغ، وقيء كثير عاد، أو أعيد يفسد، لا القليل في الحالتين، وعند محمد له يفسد بإعادة القليل لا عود الكثير. [1] قوله: إن أنزل قضى؛ لأن هذه الصور ليس فيها الجماع حقيقة، فإنه عبارة عن إيلاج ذكر في فرج مشتهى، فإن أنزل وجد معناه فيفسد، وإلا فلا. [2] قوله: وفي أقل منها لا؛ الأصل فيه أن المانع من الإفطار هو ما لا يسهل الاحتراز عنه، وذلك هو القليل بين الأسنان والمتلاشي من السمسمة في الريق، وما ليس كذلك فهو مفسد. [3] قوله: اتفاقاً؛ أي ليس باحترازي، فإن المقصود وإن أكله بعد إخراجه مفسد أخذه باليد أو بالعود أو بغير ذلك. [4] قوله: يتلاشى؛ أي يفنى ويفقد باختلاطه بالريق. [5] قوله: يعتبر الصنع، بالضم: أي الفعل، فإن صورة الفطر وهو الابتلاع إنما تتحقق إذا تحقق فعله، ولأبي يوسف: أنه خارج، فبعوده يوجد الدخول من خارج.
And if he has intercourse with a corpse, or an animal, or other than the vagina (i.e., between the thighs), or kisses, or touches, if he ejaculates he makes qada, otherwise not. And if he eats meat between his teeth equivalent to a chickpea, he only makes qada, and for less than that, no qada unless he takes it out and takes it in his hand, then eats. And if he begins to eat a sesame seed it invalidates unless he chews it (so it disintegrates). And vomit that returns or is re-swallowed: if much, it invalidates; if little, it does not invalidate in both cases. According to Muhammad, re-swallowing little invalidates, but return of much does not. [Commentary: ...]
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage contains several rulings about what invalidates fasting, followed by a commentary (sharh) that explains each point. The matn (base text) states: (1) If a fasting person has intercourse with a corpse, an animal, or in a non-vaginal location (e.g., between thighs), or kisses or touches with desire, then if ejaculation occurs, qada is required; otherwise no qada. This is because actual intercourse (insertion into a desired vagina) is missing in reality, but if ejaculation occurs, the meaning (effect) of intercourse is present. (2) Eating meat stuck between teeth: if the piece is the size of a chickpea (hummusa) or more, qada is required; if smaller, no qada unless he takes it out with his hand (or similar) and then eats it. The principle is that what is difficult to avoid (small bits) does not invalidate; what is easy to avoid (larger or taken out) invalidates. (3) Starting to eat a sesame seed: if he puts it in his mouth and eats it as is, it invalidates; but if he chews it first, it disintegrates and mixes with saliva, thus not invalidating. (4) Vomit: if a mouthful of vomit returns to the stomach involuntarily, or if he deliberately re-swallows it, it invalidates (if much). Little vomit does not invalidate in either case. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Muhammad holds that re-swallowing little vomit invalidates (because it is an act of swallowing), while the return of much vomit does not invalidate (because it was not an act of swallowing). The commentary clarifies that 'al-san' (action) is the criterion for Muhammad, whereas Abu Yusuf focuses on the substance entering from outside.

Key Terms
قضى (qada): To make up the fast (qada) after it has been invalidated; here used for cases requiring compensation.
حمصة (hummusa): A chickpea; used as a measure for the threshold of what invalidates when eating food residue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A fasting person eats a piece of meat from between his teeth that is smaller than a chickpea. Does he need to make up the fast?
A) Yes, because any eating invalidates.
B) No, because it is small and difficult to avoid.
C) Yes, if he chewed it first.
D) No, only if he took it out first.

Key Takeaway
Eating meat from teeth only invalidates if it is the size of a chickpea or more, or if taken out and eaten.

Eating a Sesame Seed: Chewing vs. Swallowing
States the ruling for eating a sesame seed during fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a fasting person eats a sesame seed, distinguishing between swallowing it whole and chewing it first.

Core Idea
Swallowing a whole sesame seed invalidates fasting; chewing it so it disintegrates does not.

This lesson deals with a specific example: a sesame seed. The ruling illustrates a general principle about solids that disintegrate when chewed. If a fasting person puts a sesame seed in his mouth and swallows it whole, the fast becomes invalid because he has intentionally swallowed a solid. However, if he chews it so that it mixes with saliva and loses its form, it is considered like food residue that is difficult to avoid, so the fast remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The case is starting to eat a sesame seed (سمسمة).
2
Apply the condition
If he swallows it without chewing, it invalidates. If he chews it until it disintegrates, it does not.
3
Understand the principle
The commentary [4] explains: the substance disintegrates and mixes with saliva, becoming like food residue that is difficult to avoid.

Source Text

[1] قوله: إن أنزل قضى؛ لأن هذه الصور ليس فيها الجماع حقيقة، فإنه عبارة عن إيلاج ذكر في فرج مشتهى، فإن أنزل وجد معناه فيفسد، وإلا فلا. [2] قوله: وفي أقل منها لا؛ الأصل فيه أن المانع من الإفطار هو ما لا يسهل الاحتراز عنه، وذلك هو القليل بين الأسنان والمتلاشي من السمسمة في الريق، وما ليس كذلك فهو مفسد. Vomit: Much vs. Little and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
States the ruling for vomit during fasting with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding re-swallowing little vomit, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Much vomit invalidates fasting; little does not. But Muhammad holds that re-swallowing little vomit invalidates due to the act of swallowing.

The final ruling in the passage concerns vomit. The general rule is that a mouthful (much) of vomit invalidates the fast, whether it returns involuntarily or is re-swallowed deliberately. Little vomit does not invalidate in either case. However, Imam Muhammad differs on the detail: he says re-swallowing little vomit invalidates because the act of swallowing is intentional, while the return of much vomit does not invalidate because it is involuntary. This intra-Hanafi dispute illustrates how different considerations (substance vs. act) lead to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The matn states that much vomit (mouthful) invalidates regardless of whether it returns or is re-swallowed. Little vomit does not invalidate in either case.
2
Introduce Muhammad's view
Imam Muhammad disagrees: he says re-swallowing little vomit invalidates because the act of swallowing (صنع) is present. But the return (عود) of much vomit does not invalidate because it is not his act.
3
Compare with Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf (whose view is the matn) holds that little vomit does not invalidate even if re-swallowed, because the quantity is small and considered excusable. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Yusuf.

Invalidation of Fasting: Contact, Eating Residue, and Vomiting
Rulings on nullification by contact, eating residue, and vomiting with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for when contact, eating stuck food, and vomiting break the fast, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on vomiting.

Core Idea
A fast is invalidated only by deliberate entry of a significant substance; small unavoidable remnants are excused.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya covers several scenarios that may break a fast: sexual contact without intercourse, eating leftover food between teeth, chewing a sesame seed, and vomiting. The scholars discuss the underlying principles and the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether quantity or act determines invalidation for vomiting.

Guided Path
1
Identify each scenario and its ruling
List the scenarios: contact (kissing/touching), meat between teeth, sesame seed, vomiting, and unavoidable substances. For each, state whether it invalidates the fast and under what conditions.
Refer to the source translation for precise conditions.
2
Determine the underlying principle
For each ruling, identify the rationale: ejaculation vs no ejaculation for contact, size threshold for meat, disintegration for sesame seed, and avoidability for dust/smoke/water.
The text uses 'because it is unavoidable' as a key principle.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on vomiting
Compare Abu Yusuf's criterion (quantity: mouthful) with Muhammad's criterion (act: re-swallowing). Note the two cases of disagreement: re-swallowing small amount and return of large amount.
Both agree on re-swallowing a large amount (invalidates) and returning a small amount (does not invalidate).
4
Apply the tarjih to determine the mufta bihi
Identify which view is relied upon for each sub-issue: for re-swallowing a small amount, Abu Yusuf's view (no qada') is preferred; for returning a large amount, Muhammad's view (no qada') is preferred.
Consult the tarjih authorities: al-Khulasa and Qadi Khan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وطئ ميتة ، أو بهيمة، أو غير (فرج) : وهو التفخيذ، (أو قبل، أو لَمِس، إن أنزل قضى، وإلا فلا. وإن أكل لحماً بين أسنانِه مثلَ حِمّصة قضى فقط ، وفي أقل منها لا إلا إذا أخرجه وأخذَهُ بيده ، ثُمَّ أكل، التقييد بالأخذ باليد وقع اتفاقاً". (ولو بدأ بأكل سمسمةٍ فَسَدَ إلا إذا مضغ، فإنه يتلاشى في فمه بالمضغ . (وقي كثير عاد ، أو أعِيدَ يُفْسِدُ ، لا القليل في الحالين ، وعند محمد له يفسد[٤] بإعادة القليل لا عود الكثير) : أي إذا عاد القيء، فالمعتبر عند أبي يوسف له[0] الكثرة : أي ملء الفم، وعند محمد الله يعتبر الصنع : أي الإعادة. وفي صب الماء في الإذن؛ لأنه ليس مما فيه صلاح البدن بخلاف الدهن. وفي دخول الغبار والدخان والذُّباب ؛ لأنه مما لا يستطاع الاحتراز عنه ؛ ولذا لو أدخله عمداً يفسد ، ولهذا يفسد بوصول المطرِ والثَّلج في حلقه لإمكان الاحتراز عنه. [1]قوله : إن أنزل قضى ؛ لأنَّ هذه الصور ليس فيها الجماع حقيقة، فإنه عبارة عن إيلاج ذكر في فرج ،مشتهى ، فإن أنزل وجد معناه فيفسد، وإلا فلا. [٢) قوله : وفي أقل منها لا ؛ الأصلُ فيه أنَّ المانع من الإفطار هو ما لا يسهل الاحترار . عنه، وذلك هو القليل بين الأسنان والمتلاشي من السمسمة في الريق، وما ليس كذلك فهو مفسد. [٣] قوله : اتفاقاً ؛ أي ليس باحترازي، فإن المقصود وإن أكله بعد إخراجه مفسد أخذه باليد أو بالعواد أو بغير ذلك. (٤ ) قوله : يتلاشى ؛ أي يفنى ويفقد باختلاطه بالريق. 01 ] قوله : يعتبر الصنع ، بالضم : أي الفعل، فإن صورة الفطر وهو الابتلاع إنما تتحقق إذا تحقق فعله، ولأبي يوسف : أنه خارج ، فبعودِهِ يوجد الدخول من خارج

ففي إعادة الكثير يفسد اتفاقاً). وفي عود القليل لا يفسد اتفاقاً. (۳) وفي إعادة القليل لا يفسد عند أبي يوسف (۲) خلافاً لمحمد . وفي عود الكثير يفسد عند أبي يوسف الله لا عند محمد - فيوجد المفطر، وإن لم يكن بصنعِهِ إلا أن القليل وهو ما دون الفم معفو.
And if he has intercourse with a dead woman, or an animal, or other than the front passage (i.e., the thighs), or kisses, or touches: if he ejaculates, he makes up (the fast); otherwise not. And if he eats meat between his teeth the size of a chickpea, he only makes up; if less than that, no, unless he removes it and takes it in his hand then eats. The restriction of taking by hand is agreed upon. And if he begins by eating a sesame seed, it invalidates (the fast) unless he chews it, for it disintegrates in his mouth by chewing. And a large amount of vomit that returns or is re-swallowed invalidates, not a small amount in both cases. According to Muhammad, it invalidates by re-swallowing a small amount, not by return of a large amount. That is, if vomit returns, the consideration according to Abu Yusuf is quantity: that is, mouthful; according to Muhammad, consideration is the act: i.e., re-ingestion. And pouring water into the ear (does not break fast) because it is not something that benefits the body, unlike oil. And entry of dust, smoke, and fly (does not break fast) because it is unavoidable; thus if he deliberately introduces it, it invalidates. Hence, it invalidates by arrival of rain and snow in his throat, as avoidance is possible. [1] He said: 'If he ejaculates, he makes up' because these forms are not actual intercourse, which is insertion of penis into a desired vagina; if he ejaculates, its meaning is found, so it invalidates; otherwise not. [2] He said: 'And if less than that, no' – the principle is that what prevents breaking fast is that which is difficult to avoid, which is the small amount between teeth and the disintegrated sesame seed in saliva; otherwise it is invalidating. [3] He said: 'Agreed upon' meaning it is not restrictive; the point is that if he eats it after removing it, it invalidates whether he takes it by hand, toothpick, or otherwise. (4) He said: 'disintegrates' meaning it perishes and mixes with saliva. [0] He said: 'Considers the act' meaning the action, for the image of breaking fast - which is swallowing - only occurs if his action is realized. According to Abu Yusuf, it is (already) outside, so by its return, entry from outside occurs. So, regarding re-swallowing a large amount, it invalidates by agreement. Regarding return of a small amount, it does not invalidate by agreement. Regarding re-swallowing a small amount, it does not invalidate according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad. Regarding return of a large amount, it invalidates according to Abu Yusuf, not according to Muhammad. So the invalidator occurs, even if not by his action, except that a small amount (less than a mouthful) is excused.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' addresses various scenarios that may nullify a fast. The type is a ruling with proof, as the commentator provides rationale. The text first covers contact without insertion: intercourse with a dead woman, animal, or other than the vagina (thighs), as well as kissing and touching. If ejaculation occurs, the fast is broken and must be made up (qada'); otherwise not, because the essence of intercourse is missing. Next, eating meat stuck between teeth: if the piece is as large as a chickpea, it invalidates; if smaller, it does not unless the person removes it and then eats it deliberately. The exemption for small pieces is based on the principle that unavoidable remnants are excused. Chewing a sesame seed disintegrates it, so it does not invalidate. Regarding vomiting: a mouthful that returns or is re-swallowed invalidates; a small amount is excused. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf considers quantity (mouthful) as the criterion, while Muhammad considers the act of re-swallowing. The text states that re-swallowing a large amount invalidates by agreement, and returning a small amount does not invalidate by agreement. The difference arises in re-swallowing a small amount (Abu Yusuf: no invalidation; Muhammad: invalidation) and returning a large amount (Abu Yusuf: invalidation; Muhammad: no invalidation). The relied-upon positions vary by sub-issue, as noted in footnotes. The passage also clarifies that unavoidable substances like dust, smoke, and flies do not break the fast unless intentionally introduced.

Key Terms
مثل الحمصة (mithl al-himmasa): Size of a chickpea; used as a threshold for meat stuck between teeth — if piece is this size, it invalidates the fast.
الكثرة (al-kathra): Quantity; specifically mouthful of vomit as criterion according to Abu Yusuf.
الصنع (al-sun'): The act of re-swallowing; criterion according to Imam Muhammad for vomiting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fasting person accidentally swallows a small piece of meat (less than a chickpea) that was stuck between his teeth, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is invalid and qada' is required.
B) The fast remains valid because small remnants are excused.
C) The fast is invalid only if he intentionally removes and eats it.
D) The fast remains valid only if the meat was not removed first.

Key Takeaway
Small unavoidable remnants are excused; vomiting's invalidation depends on quantity (Abu Yusuf) or act (Muhammad), with preferred views differing by sub-issue.

6 min
Disliked Acts for the Fasting Person: Tasting, Chewing, and Kissing
Rulings on disliked acts: tasting, chewing, and kissing with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tasting, chewing, and kissing while fasting, and apply the condition of security from desire to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
Tasting and kissing are only disliked if there is risk of desire; necessity permits tasting.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between actions that are simply disliked (makruh tanzih) and those that invalidate the fast. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies three common scenarios: tasting food, chewing, and kissing. The ruling hinges on the presence of necessity or security from desire—a nuanced approach reflecting the school's attention to both the letter and spirit of the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Acts
List the three acts mentioned: tasting (al-dhawq), chewing (maddgh), and kissing (al-qubla). Note the default ruling: makruh tanzih.
2
Note the Exceptions
Tasting and chewing become permissible with necessity (e.g., feeding a child). Kissing is permissible if the person feels safe from desire (amn).
3
Analyze the Dalil
The commentator cites the Prophet's kissing while fasting as evidence for permissibility, and the prohibition as evidence for karaha when there is fear. This combination yields a nuanced ruling.
4
Apply the Condition
The critical factor is the person's confidence in controlling desire. If safe, permissible; if not, disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره له : الذوق ، ومضغ شيء إلا طعام صبي ضرورةً، والقبلة" إن لم يأمن ، لا لمن أمن
[1] قوله : وكره ; أي يكره تنزيهاً للصائم ذوق شيء ومضغه بلا عذر ، فإن كانت هناك ضرورة يجوز كما في إطعام الصبي، وكذا إذا كان سيدها أو زوجها سيء الخلق فيجوز لها الذوق.
(۲) قوله : والقبلة ، بالضم : أي يُكره له تقبيل المرأة، وكذا المس بشهوة إن لم يأمن من الإنزال، والوقوع في الجماع فإن أَمِنَ فلا بأس به، وقد ورد : «أنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يقبلُ وهو صائم ، وورد عنه النّهى أيضاً ()، وهو محمول على صورة التخوف كما فصلته في «التعليق الممجّد على موطأ الإمام محمد».
And it is disliked for him: tasting, and chewing something except the food of a child out of necessity, and kissing if he does not feel safe (from desire), but not for one who feels safe. [1] He said: 'And it is disliked' meaning it is tanzihan disliked for the fasting person to taste something or chew without an excuse. If there is a necessity, it is permissible, such as feeding a child; similarly, if her master or husband is ill-tempered, it is permissible for her to taste. (2) He said: 'And kissing' meaning it is disliked for him to kiss a woman, and similarly touching with desire if he does not feel safe from ejaculation and falling into intercourse. If he feels safe, there is no problem. It has been reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss while fasting, and prohibition has also been reported from him; this is interpreted as the case of fear (of desire) as I have detailed in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' al-Imam Muhammad.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage addresses acts that are makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful) for the fasting person. The text explicitly mentions tasting food, chewing anything (except when necessity arises, such as chewing food for a child), and kissing. The ruling is based on the principle of avoiding actions that might lead to breaking the fast or invoke desire. Tasting and chewing without excuse are disliked because they approach the act of eating, though they do not invalidate the fast. Kissing is disliked if the person fears he might be overcome by desire and proceed to intercourse; if he is confident he can control himself, it is not disliked. The commentator cites the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) kissing while fasting, which indicates permissibility, but also the hadith of prohibition, which is understood as applying to those who fear desire. Thus, the ruling is nuanced based on the individual's state.

Key Terms
الذوق (al-dhawq): Tasting food or drink with the tongue without swallowing; disliked for the fasting person without excuse.
القبلة (al-qubla): Kissing; disliked for the fasting person if it arouses desire and he fears falling into intercourse.
ضرورة (darura): Necessity that permits tasting, such as feeding a child or checking food for a ill-tempered husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a fasting person kisses his spouse while feeling safe from falling into desire?
A) It is haram and invalidates the fast.
B) It is makruh tanzih but does not invalidate the fast.
C) It is permissible and there is no problem.
D) It is wajib to avoid it even if safe.

Key Takeaway
The default ruling for tasting, chewing without necessity, and kissing when one fears desire is makruh tanzih; necessity or security from desire makes them permissible.

Ruling for Elderly, Pregnant, Nursing, and Sick Persons Unable to Fast
Ruling on concession for those unable to fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each category of person unable to fast (elderly, pregnant, nursing, sick) and apply the conditions for fidyah and qada.

Core Idea
Inability to fast permits breaking the fast, but the elderly must pay fidyah, and all categories must make up if ability returns.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the rulings for those who have legitimate excuses to break the fast in Ramadan. It covers four specific categories and clarifies the conditions under which breaking is allowed, the obligation of fidyah for the elderly, and the requirement to make up missed fasts if the inability later ceases. The proof is drawn from the Quranic verse on fidyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the four types of persons mentioned: elderly (shaykh fani), pregnant woman, nursing woman, and sick person. Note that each has a specific condition for being allowed to break the fast.
The elderly are those who are decrepit and physically unable to fast; the others require fear of harm.
2
State the ruling for each category
For all categories: breaking the fast is permissible (mubah). For the elderly: fidyah (feeding a poor person per day) is wajib. For all: if the inability ceases later, qada (making up the fasts) becomes wajib, and the fidyah for the elderly becomes void.
The fidyah is like fitrana: half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of barley.
3
Understand the condition of fear
For pregnant, nursing, and sick persons, the permission depends on a genuine fear of harm to themselves or the child/fetus. The fear must be reasonable (e.g., fear of increased illness, slow recovery, or harm to the baby). If no fear, fasting remains obligatory.
The text mentions specific examples: increase of illness, slowing of health, corruption of a limb, eye pain, wound, headache, etc.
4
Analyze the proof from Quran
The author cites Quran 2:184: 'And upon those who are able to fast (but with difficulty) a ransom of feeding a poor person.' The phrase 'yutiqoonahu' is interpreted as those who cannot bear it (inability). This verse establishes the concession of fidyah for those who cannot fast.
The Hanafi interpretation takes 'yutiqoonahu' to mean 'they can bear it with difficulty' – indicating that the verse applies to those for whom fasting is extremely hard, not merely possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشيخ فَانٍ عَجِزَ عن الصومِ يُفطرُ ويُطعم لكل يوم مسكيناً كالفطرة، ويقضي إن قدر، وحامل، أو مرضع خافت على نفسها أو ولدها ، أو مريض خاف زيادة مرضه. قوله : ويطعم ؛ لقوله : ( وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مسكين ) : أي لا يطيقونه.  قوله : كالفطرة : أي كمقدار الفطرة، وهو بالكسر : اسم لصدقة الفطر، يعني نصف صاع من برٍ أو صاع من شعير ونحو ذلك على ما مر ذكره في بابه. قوله : ويقضي ؛ يعني إذا قدرَ الشَّيخُ الفاني على الصوم يجب عليه القضاء، ويبطل حكم الفداء ؛ لأنَّ شرط خلفيَّةِ استمرار العجز، وإذ ليس فليس. قوله : أو مرضع ؛ اسم فاعل من الإرضاع، هي المرأة التي من شأنها الإرضاع، وإن لم تباشره، والمرضعة هي التي تباشره حال إلقائه الثدي في فم الصبي. كذا في «النهر». قوله : خافت ؛ أي خافتِ الحامل أو المرضعُ بالصوم على نفسها أو ولدها الرضيع أو الجنين بحدوث مرض أو ازدياده. قوله : زيادة مرضه ؛ أو إبطاءُ الصَّحَّةِ أو فساد العضو أو وجع العين، أو جراحة، أو صداعاً أو نحو ذلك ، فإن لم يخف منه فالصوم عليه واجب.
An elderly person who is unable to fast breaks fast and feeds a poor person for each day like the fidyah, and he must make up (the fasts) if he becomes able. And a pregnant woman or a nursing woman who fears for herself or her child (by fasting), or a sick person who fears an increase in his illness (may break fast). His statement: 'and he feeds' due to His saying: 'And upon those who are able to fast (but with difficulty) a ransom of feeding a poor person' (Quran 2:184) meaning those who cannot bear it. His statement: 'like the fidyah': meaning like the amount of the fidyah, which is the name for the charity of fitr, i.e., half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of barley or the like, as mentioned in its chapter. His statement: 'and he makes up' means if the elderly person becomes able to fast, he must make up, and the ruling of the fidyah is nullified because the condition for the substitute is the continuation of inability, and if not, then it is not. His statement: 'or a nursing woman': an active participle from breastfeeding; she is a woman who is characterized by breastfeeding, even if she is not actually doing it at the moment. The 'murdiah' is the one who actually breastfeeds when placing the nipple in the infant's mouth. Thus in 'al-Nahr'. His statement: 'feared': meaning the pregnant or nursing woman feared by fasting for herself or her nursing child or fetus due to the occurrence of illness or its increase. His statement: 'increase of his illness': or slowing of health, or corruption of a limb, or eye pain, or a wound, or headache, or the like. If he does not fear that, then fasting is obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on those who have a legitimate excuse to break the fast in Ramadan. It covers four categories: (1) An elderly person (shaykh fani) who is physically unable to fast. Such a person may break the fast and must pay fidyah (feed a poor person for each missed day) like the amount of fitrana (half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of barley). If he later regains the ability to fast, he must make up the missed days, and the fidyah becomes void because the substitute depends on the continuation of the inability. (2) A pregnant woman who fears harm to herself or her fetus. (3) A nursing woman who fears for herself or her nursing child. (4) A sick person who fears an increase in his illness or delayed recovery. All these may break their fast. However, if they do not have a genuine fear, then fasting remains obligatory upon them. The proof is the Quranic verse (2:184) which permits breaking fast for those who cannot bear it, with the alternative of feeding a poor person. The commentary clarifies linguistic points: 'murdih' vs. 'murdiah' and the condition that the fear must be based on reasonable grounds.

Key Terms
شيخ فان (shaykh faanin): An elderly person who is decrepit and approaching death, unable to fast.
فدية (fidyah): A ransom or compensation for missed fasts, consisting of feeding a poor person.
مرضع (murdhi'): A woman characterized by breastfeeding, even if not currently nursing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an elderly person who could not fast later becomes able to fast, what must he do?
A) He only needs to pay the fidyah and no further action.
B) He must make up the missed fasts and the fidyah becomes void.
C) He may choose to repeat the fasts or pay more fidyah.
D) He must continue paying fidyah and also fast.

Key Takeaway
The concession to break fast due to inability is conditional upon genuine fear or incapacity, and if the inability is temporary, qada becomes obligatory, voiding the fidyah.

Fidyah vs. Qada: Hanafi Rulings for Those Unable to Fast
Ruling with proofs for exemptions and compensation obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the obligations of fidyah and qada for those unable to fast, and identify the exceptions for pregnant, nursing, sick, and traveling persons.

Core Idea
The elderly give fidyah; others make up missed fasts with no fidyah.

Fasting during Ramadan is obligatory, but the Shari'ah provides exemptions for those who cannot bear it. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah outlines the rulings for five categories: the elderly, the pregnant woman, the nursing mother, the sick, and the traveler. You will learn when fidyah is due, when only qada is required, and how a nursing mother's case is resolved within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the five types of people mentioned: elderly person (shaykh fanin), pregnant woman, nursing woman (murdi'a), sick person, and traveler.
The elderly is one who is physically incapable of fasting permanently or nearly so.
2
Determine the ruling for each category
For the elderly: break fast and give fidyah (feed a poor person per day). For all others: break fast and only make up missed days (qada) later.
The fidyah is like the fitrah amount: half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of barley.
3
Apply the proofs (dalil)
The Quranic verse 2:184 supports fidyah for those who can barely endure fasting. Verse 2:185 and the hadith ('Allah has removed fasting from the traveler, pregnant, and nursing') support qada for the others.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some scholars restrict the permission to break fast for nursing to a hired wet nurse (murdi'a) excluding the mother. The majority (muhaqqiqun) include the mother because nursing is wajib upon her diyanatan when needed.
The relied-upon position is that the mother is included.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi is that the nursing mother may break her fast when she fears for her child, and she owes only qada, no fidyah.
This is the position of zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشيخ فانٍ عَجِزَ عن الصومِ يُفطرُ ويُطعم لكل يوم مسكيناً كالفطرة؛ لقوله تعالى: (وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِينٍ) أي لا يطيقونه. كالفطرة: أي كمقدار الفطرة، وهو نصف صاع من برٍ أو صاع من شعير. ويقضي إن قدر؛ يعني إذا قدر الشيخ الفاني على الصوم يجب عليه القضاء، ويبطل حكم الفداء؛ لأن شرط الخلفية استمرار العجز. وحامل، أو مرضع خافت على نفسها أو ولدها، أو مريض خاف زيادة مرضه. والمسافر، أفطروا وقضوا بلا فدية عليهم؛ لقوله تعالى: (وَمَن كَانَ مَرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَىٰ سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ)، وحديث: «إن الله وضع عن المسافر والحامل والمرضع الصوم». وقيل: حل الإفطار مختص بمرضعة دون الأم إلا إذا تعينت. ورد بأنه خلاف ظاهر الرواية؛ لأن الإرضاع واجب على الأم ديانة.
And an old man who is incapable of fasting breaks his fast and feeds for each day a poor person like the fitrah; due to His statement: 'And upon those who can afford it, a ransom: feeding a poor person.' Meaning those who cannot bear it. 'Like the fitrah': meaning like the amount of fitrah, which is half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of barley. And he makes up if he becomes able; meaning if the old man becomes able to fast, making up becomes obligatory upon him and the ruling of ransom is nullified because the condition of substitution is the continuation of incapacity. And a pregnant woman, or a nursing woman who fears for herself or her child, or a sick person who fears an increase in his illness. And the traveler, they break fast and make up without ransom upon them; due to His statement: 'And whoever is sick or on a journey, then a number of other days,' and the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has removed fasting from the traveler, the pregnant, and the nursing mother.' And it was said: The permissibility of breaking fast is specific to a hired wet nurse, not the mother, except if she is the only one. And it was rejected as contrary to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) because nursing is religiously obligatory upon the mother.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi rulings for those who are unable to fast due to valid excuses. It begins with the elderly person who is physically incapable of fasting. Such a person is permitted to break the fast and must give fidyah (ransom) for each missed day, equivalent to the amount of sadaqat al-fitr (half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of barley). This is based on the Quranic verse (al-Baqarah 2:184) which allows a ransom for those who can only bear fasting with difficulty. However, if the elderly person later regains the strength to fast, the fidyah is cancelled and he must make up the missed days (qada). The condition for the fidyah to substitute for fasting is the continuation of incapacity. The passage then addresses the pregnant woman, the nursing mother, and the sick person. If any of them fear harm to themselves (e.g., worsening of illness, danger to the fetus or infant) by fasting, they are allowed to break the fast and only owe qada — no fidyah is required. Similarly, the traveler is permitted to break the fast during Ramadan and must make up the missed days later, without any fidyah, as indicated by the same verse and supported by a hadith recorded by the compilers of the Sunan. A point of ihtilaf (dispute) within the Hanafi school is mentioned regarding the nursing woman. Some scholars (reported in al-Dhakhira) restrict the permission to break the fast to a hired wet nurse (murdi'a) because she is contractually obligated to nurse, whereas the mother is not obligated (since the husband can hire another nurse). However, this view is rejected by the muhaqqiqun (verifiers) who consider it contrary to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah). They argue that nursing is a religious duty upon the mother, especially if the husband is unable to hire a wet nurse. Therefore, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the mother also has the same permission to break the fast when she fears for the child.

Key Terms
فانٍ (fanin): An elderly person who is decrepit and nearing death due to old age.
فدية (fidyah): A ransom compensating for missed fast, feeding one poor person per day.
مرضع (murdi'a): A woman who nurses a child, either mother or wet nurse.
قضاء (qada): Making up missed fasts after Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if an elderly person who was paying fidyah later becomes able to fast?
A) He must continue giving fidyah and does not need to make up the fasts.
B) He must make up the missed fasts (qada) and the fidyah is cancelled.
C) He must both give fidyah and make up the fasts.
D) He is permanently excused and needs to do nothing.

Key Takeaway
The elderly give fidyah when permanently unable; others (pregnant, nursing, sick, traveler) break and only make up missed fasts; the nursing mother is included in the exemption.

The Ruling for Breastfeeding Women Fasting in Ramadan: Qada Only, No Fidyah
Explaining the ruling for breastfeeding women regarding breaking the fast and qada only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for breastfeeding women who fear for their child and identify the proofs and the rejected weak opinion.

Core Idea
Breastfeeding women break fast with only qada, no fidyah, based on Quran and hadith.

This lesson examines the ruling for breastfeeding women (both mother and wet nurse) who fear for their child if they fast in Ramadan. The Hanafi school, based on the Quran and hadith, permits them to break the fast and requires only make-up days (qada) without any expiation (fidyah). We will also address a weak opinion that restricts this permission to hired wet nurses and see how the commentators refute it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the ruling for a breastfeeding woman who fears for her child: she may break the fast and must only make up the days later (qada), with no fidyah.
2
Locate the textual proofs
Find the Quranic verse (2:185) and the hadith from the four Sunan that are cited as evidence for this ruling.
The verse shows that the sick and traveler qada only; the hadith explicitly includes the pregnant and breastfeeding woman.
3
Unpack the weak opinion (qil)
Understand the alternative view that only a hired wet nurse may break the fast, not the mother, unless she is specifically needed.
This opinion is mentioned in al-Dhakhira and is based on the idea that the mother can hire a wet nurse.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine how commentators like Fath al-Qadir and al-Bahr refute this opinion, arguing it contradicts the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah).
They hold that breastfeeding is religiously obligatory (diyaanatan) for both mother and wet nurse when the child is at risk, so the permission is general.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position
Confirm that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that both mother and wet nurse may break the fast when fearing for the child, with only qada required.
This is the position of the six core books (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسافر، أفطروا وقضوا بلا فدية عليهم والمسافر ، أفطروا وقضوا بلا فدية" عليهم). وقيل (١) : حل الإفطار مختص بمرضعة [1]قوله : قضوا ؛ أي بعد رمضان عند أمن الضرر وزوال السفر. [٢] قوله : بلا فدية ؛ الأصل فيه قوله : وَمَن كَانَ مَرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَبَامٍ أُخَرَ ) (۲) : أي إذا أفطرَ يلزم عليه الصّومُ بقدر ما فاته، ولا أثر للفدية فيه، والحامل والمرضع أعطي لها حكم المريض، فيلزم عليهما القضاء فقط، ويشهد له حديث: «إنَّ اللهَ وضعَ عن المسافرِ والحامل والمرضع الصوم»، أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة. [٣]قوله : قيل ... الخ ؛ حاصلُ هذا القول وهو مذكور في «الذخيرة» وغيرها : إِنَّ الإفطار إنّما يحلُّ بعلةِ الخوف على الولد لمرضعة، وكذا بالإجارة؛ لوجوب الإرضاع عليها، أما الأم فلا يجب عليها ذلك ؛ لإمكان أن يستأجر الزوج مرضعة فلا يحل لها الإفطار إلا إذا تعينت للإرضاع بأن كان الأب معسراً ، أو كان الولد لا يأخذ ثدي غيرها ونحو ذلك. ورده في «فتح القدير»، و«البحر») وغيرهما : بأنه خلاف ظاهر الرواية، أجَرت نفسها للإرضاع ، ولا يحلُّ للوالدة إذ لا يجب عليها الإرضاع. أقول : لو كان حل الإفطار بناء على وجوب الإرضاع، فعقد الإجارة لو كان قبل رمضان يحل لها الإفطار، لكن لو لم يكن قبل رمضان ، بل تُؤجر نفسها [لدا] في رمضان ينبغي أن لا يحل لها الإفطار إذ لا يجب عليها الإجارة إلا إذا دعت الضرورة إليها. [E]" أما الوالدة، فلا يحل لها الإفطار إلا إذا تعيّنت)، فحينئذ يجب عليها الإرضاع، فيحل لها الإفطار. وخلاف قولهم : خافت على ولدها بإضافة الولد إلى المرضع، بل الصحيح أنَّ الظَّئر والأمّ يَحِلُّ لكليهما الإفطار مطلقاً عند الخوف، والإرضاعُ عليهما واجب ديانةً مطلقاً وقضاء أيضاً فيما إذا كان الأب معسراً أو لم تتيسر مرضعة غيرها. [1]قوله : أجرت ؛ أي عقدت الإجارة على إرضاع ولد غيرها. [٢]قوله : بناء ؛ أي مبنياً على وجوب الإرضاع كما يفهم مما قيل. [۳]قوله : ينبغي... الخ؛ قد ردَّهُ كلَّ مَن نظر في كلامه بأن عقد الإجارة مباح في كل زمان، فإذا عقدت في رمضانَ بناءً على إباحتِها وجب عليها الإرضاع بناءً عليها، فيحل لها الإفطار. وبالجملة ؛ وجوب الإجارة في رمضانَ لا يضرُّ عدمُهُ في وجوب الإرضاع، وحلّ الفطرة إنما هو مبني على وجوب الإرضاع لا على وجوب الإجارة، فما حكم عليه بأنه ينبغي ليس مما ينبغي. [٤] قوله : يجب ؛ فإنها لو لم ترضع حينئذ أدى إلى هلاك الولد. (١) أي تعينت للإرضاع ؛ ؛ لفقد الظئر، أو لعدم قدرة الزوج على استئجارها، أو لعدم أخذ الولد ثدي غيرها. ينظر : «الإيضاح»(ق٣٦/أ).
And the traveler, they break the fast and make up [the days] without any fidyah upon them. And it was said: The permissibility of breaking the fast is specific to a wet nurse. [1] His saying: 'they make up' i.e., after Ramadan when safety from harm and the end of travel. [2] His saying: 'without fidyah'; the basis for it is His saying: {And whoever is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days} (2:185): i.e., if he breaks the fast, fasting is incumbent upon him for what he missed, and there is no effect of fidyah in it. And a pregnant woman and a breastfeeding woman are given the ruling of the sick, so only qada is incumbent upon them. And this is supported by the hadith: 'Verily Allah has lifted from the traveler, the pregnant, and the breastfeeding woman the fast,' narrated by the authors of the four Sunan. [3] His saying: 'it was said...' etc.; the gist of this saying, which is mentioned in 'al-Dhakhira' and others, is that breaking the fast is only permitted due to the fear for the child for a wet nurse, and likewise due to a hiring contract (ijara), because of the obligation of breastfeeding upon her. As for the mother, it is not obligatory upon her, because it is possible for the husband to hire a wet nurse, so breaking the fast is not permissible for her unless she is specifically required for breastfeeding, such as if the father is poor, or the child does not accept the breast of another, and so on. And it was refuted in 'Fath al-Qadir' and 'al-Bahr' and others that it is contrary to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah). She hired herself out for breastfeeding, and it is not permissible for the mother because breastfeeding is not obligatory upon her. I say: If the permissibility of breaking the fast is based on the obligation of breastfeeding, then if the hiring contract was before Ramadan, she may break the fast, but if it was not before Ramadan, rather she hires herself out for it in Ramadan, it seems that she should not be permitted to break the fast because the hiring is not obligatory upon her unless necessity calls for it. As for the mother, she is not permitted to break the fast unless she is specifically needed, and then breastfeeding becomes obligatory upon her, so breaking the fast becomes permissible for her. And contrary to their saying: 'she feared for her child' by attaching the child to the wet nurse, rather the correct view is that the wet nurse and the mother are both permitted to break the fast absolutely when there is fear, and breastfeeding is obligatory upon both religiously (diyaanatan) absolutely, and also legally (qada’an) when the father is poor or no other wet nurse is available. [1] His saying: 'she hired herself out' i.e., she contracted a hiring for breastfeeding a child of another. [2] His saying: 'based' i.e., based on the obligation of breastfeeding as understood from what was said. [3] His saying: 'it seems...' etc.; everyone who examined his words refuted it by saying that the hiring contract is permissible in every time, so if she contracts it in Ramadan based on its permissibility, breastfeeding becomes obligatory upon her based on it, so breaking the fast becomes permissible for her. In summary, the non-obligation of the hiring in Ramadan does not harm the obligation of breastfeeding, and the permissibility of breaking the fast is only based on the obligation of breastfeeding, not on the obligation of hiring. So what he ruled that 'it seems' is not what seems correct. [4] His saying: 'obligatory' because if she does not breastfeed then it leads to the destruction of the child. (1) i.e., she is specifically needed for breastfeeding due to the lack of a wet nurse, or the husband's inability to hire one, or the child not accepting the breast of another. Cf. 'al-Idah' (36/A).
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling for breastfeeding women regarding fasting in Ramadan. The author quotes the base text stating that travelers and those who break the fast must only make up the days (qada) without any fidyah. He then provides the Quranic proof from Surah al-Baqarah 2:185 and the hadith that Allah has lifted the fast from the traveler, the pregnant, and the breastfeeding woman. The main point of discussion is a weak opinion (qil) that the permission to break the fast applies only to a hired wet nurse (muriḍa) and not to the mother (walida), based on whether breastfeeding is obligatory. The commentator refutes this, citing the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) that both the mother and the wet nurse are permitted to break the fast absolutely when they fear for the child. He explains that the obligation of breastfeeding (diyaanatan) applies to both, and the permissibility of breaking the fast is based on this obligation, not on the existence of a hiring contract. The commentary on page 519 further clarifies that a hiring contract in Ramadan is permissible and does create an obligation to breastfeed, thus permitting breaking the fast. The author concludes that the correct view is the one from the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah), and the opposing opinion is weak.

Key Terms
مرضعة (muriḍa): A wet nurse hired to breastfeed a child.
والدة (wālida): The biological mother of the child.
فدية (fidya): An expiation (usually feeding a poor person for each missed fast) required for those who cannot fast due to permanent incapacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah), what is the ruling for a mother who fears for her child's life if she fasts in Ramadan?
A) She must fast and cannot break her fast under any circumstance.
B) She may break her fast and must pay fidyah for each missed day.
C) She may break her fast and must only make up the days (qada) later.
D) She may break her fast only if she is a hired wet nurse, not if she is the mother.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a breastfeeding woman who fears for her child may break her fast and must only make up the days later; no fidyah is required.

Breaking the Fast for Nursing Mothers and Wet Nurses in Ramadan
Ruling with proof and refutation on wet nurse's fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which it is permissible for a nursing woman to break her fast in Ramadan and apply the Hanafi ruling that permissibility is based on the obligation to breastfeed when necessary.

Core Idea
Breaking the fast is permissible only when breastfeeding becomes obligatory due to fear of harm to the child.

This passage addresses a nuanced fiqh question: when may a breastfeeding woman break her fast in Ramadan? The commentator distinguishes between a mother and a hired wet nurse, clarifying that permissibility is not automatic but depends on the obligation to breastfeed. He also corrects a flawed inference about the timing of the hire contract, affirming that the contract itself, once made, creates the obligation and thus permits breaking the fast. The correct view is that both mother and wet nurse may break fast whenever there is genuine fear for the child's well-being.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of women
The passage discusses two types of nursing women: the biological mother (al-wālida) and the hired wet nurse (al-ẓa’ir). Note that their starting points differ—the mother is not initially obligated to breastfeed, while the wet nurse becomes obligated via the hire contract.
The mother’s obligation arises only when she is specifically needed (ta‘ayyun).
2
Understand the basis of permissibility
The commentator states: ‘the permissibility of breaking the fast is based on the obligation of breastfeeding.’ This is the key principle. Whenever breastfeeding is wajib, breaking the fast becomes mubah (permissible).
Not the opposite—obligation to breastfeed is the cause, not the mere act of breastfeeding.
3
Analyze the temporal issue for the wet nurse
An initial (flawed) view suggested that if the hire contract was made during Ramadan, breaking fast should not be permissible because the hire itself was not obligatory. The commentator refutes this: the contract is valid at any time, and once contracted, breastfeeding becomes obligatory, so breaking is permissible.
The flaw was confusing the non-obligation of entering the contract with the obligation arising from it.
4
Examine the mother's situation
The mother is not initially obligated to breastfeed. She may break her fast only when she becomes specifically designated (ta‘ayyun)—i.e., when no other wet nurse is available or the child refuses others. Then breastfeeding becomes obligatory, and breaking is allowed.
This is a stricter condition than for the wet nurse, who has already undertaken the obligation.
5
Conclude with the correct ruling
The correct view is that both mother and wet nurse may break their fast absolutely whenever there is fear of harm to the child, and breastfeeding is a religious obligation (wajib diyānatan) on both when the father is unable to provide another wet nurse.
The earlier restriction (attributing the child specifically to the wet nurse) is rejected.

Source Text

She hired herself out for breastfeeding, and it is not permissible for the mother because breastfeeding is not obligatory upon her. But if it was not before Ramadan, rather she hires herself out in Ramadan, it should not be permissible for her to break the fast since the hire is not obligatory upon her unless necessity calls for it. And contrary to their statement 'she fears for her child' by attributing the child to the wet nurse, rather the correct view is that both the wet nurse and the mother are permitted to break the fast absolutely when there is fear, and breastfeeding is obligatory upon them both as a religious duty absolutely, and also as a make-up (qada) when the father is insolvent or no other wet nurse is available. [1] His saying: 'hired' means she contracted a hire to breastfeed someone else's child. [2] His saying: 'based' means built upon the obligation of breastfeeding as understood from what was said. So if she contracted it in Ramadan based on its permissibility, breastfeeding becomes obligatory upon her based on it, so it is permissible for her to break the fast. Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation on the permissibility of breaking the fast for a woman breastfeeding a child in Ramadan. The commentator first addresses a scenario where a woman hires herself as a wet nurse. If the hire contract was made before Ramadan, she may break her fast because she is obligated to breastfeed. But if she hires herself in Ramadan, the commentator initially suggests that breaking fast may not be permissible since the hire itself is not obligatory. However, this view is refuted: the hire contract is valid in any time, and once contracted, breastfeeding becomes obligatory; thus breaking fast is permissible. For the mother, it is not permissible to break fast just because she is a mother; rather, it becomes permissible only if she is specifically needed to breastfeed (e.g., no other wet nurse available) and she fears harm to the child. In that case, breastfeeding becomes obligatory and breaking fast is allowed. The correct position is that both the natural mother and the wet nurse may break fast whenever there is fear of harm to the child, and breastfeeding is a religious obligation when the father is unable to provide another wet nurse or is insolvent.

Key Terms
الظائر (al-za'ir): A wet nurse who is not the biological mother, hired to breastfeed a child.
الإجارة (al-ijara): A contract of hire for services (here, breastfeeding).
التعين (al-ta‘ayyun): Specific designation; the mother becomes the only feasible source of breastfeeding, making it obligatory upon her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when may a biological mother break her fast in Ramadan while breastfeeding?
A) Whenever she feels tired from breastfeeding.
B) Only when she is specifically designated to breastfeed (ta‘ayyun) and fears harm to the child.
C) Only if she hired herself as a wet nurse.
D) It is never permissible for a mother to break her fast.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of breaking the fast for a nursing woman is contingent on the obligation to breastfeed, which for the mother arises only when she is uniquely needed, and for the wet nurse arises through her hire contract, both driven by necessity to protect the child.
