Traveler's Fasting and Fidya Upon Death Before Qada'
Ruling on traveler fasting and fidya upon death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when fasting is preferred for a traveler and determine whether fidya is due if a person dies before making up missed fasts.

Core Idea
A traveler should fast if no hardship; fidya is due only if death occurs after qada' obligation is established.

This lesson addresses two related rulings: the preference for a traveler to fast in Ramadan when it causes no harm, and the compensation (fidya) for missed fasts upon death. It clarifies that 'harm' means significant hardship, not mere inconvenience, and that fidya is contingent on the ability to perform qada' before death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Separate the passage into: (a) the ruling on traveler fasting (mustahabb if no harm, makruh if hardship) and (b) the ruling on fidya upon death (obligatory only if the person had a chance to make up fasts but died after recovering).
The fidya ruling extends to all excused persons (sick, pregnant, nursing).
2
Understand 'harm' in Hanafi fiqh
The commentator explains that harm (darar) in this context means severe hardship (mashaqqa), not just any discomfort. The hadith 'ليس من البر الصوم في السفر' is interpreted as applying only when hardship exists.
This is a key Hanafi usul point: interpreting a general prohibition as restricted by the presence of harm.
3
Trace the proof for fasting preference
The author cites the practice of the Prophet and Companions fasting during the Conquest of Mecca as evidence that fasting in travel can be better. This overrides the apparent prohibition of the hadith when no hardship occurs.
The Hanafi method: specific practice (fi'l) can qualify the general command.
4
Analyze the fidya condition
Fidya becomes wajib only if the deceased had the ability (qudra) to perform qada' but died before doing so. If death occurs during the excuse period (before opportunity for qada'), no fidya is due because the obligation of qada' never became binding.
This is a subtle point: the obligation of fidya is subsidiary to the obligation of qada'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصوم مسافر لا يضرُّهُ أحبّ، ولا قضاء إن مات في سفره، أو مرضيه ، وإن صح، أو أقام ، ثم مات ، فَدَى عنه وليه [ ١ ] قوله : لا يضره ؛ صفةٌ للمسافر ، وقيد به لأنه لو كان الصوم يصره فهو في حكم المريض ونحوه، فالأفضلُ الفطر. والمراد بالضرر المشقة، لا ضرر البدن بخصوصه، وهو محمل حديث: «ليس من البرِّ الصَّومُ في السفر ) ، أخرجه مسلم والنسائي وغيرهما، فقد ثبت: «أَنَّ رسولَ اللهِ رأى رجلاً تحت ظل شجرةٍ يرش عليه الماء ، فقال : ما بال صاحبكم؟، فقالوا : إنه صائم»(٢)، فقال ذلك القول زجراً عن صوم المسافر ، وعدم قبول الرخصة مع وجودِ المشقة. [٢) قوله : أحب ; فإنَّ الرُّخصة في الصوم للمسافر للترفيه والتيسير، وقد يكون ذلك في الصوم مع الجماعة، لا سيما عند عدم المضرة، ويشهد له : «أَنَّ النَّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وكثيراً أصحابه صاموا في رمضانَ في سفرِ غزوة فتح مكة»، رواه البخاري، ولو كان الأفضلُ التُركُ مطلقاً لما وقع ذلك. (۳) قوله : ولا قضاء ... الخ ; هذا الحكم وإن ذكرَهُ أكثرُهُم في المسافرِ والمريض لكنه ليس بمختص بهما ، بل هو شامل للحامل والمرضع أيضاً. وبالجملة ; المعذور بالعذرِ المبيحُ للفطر إذا مات في أيام عذرِهِ ولم يدرك عدة من أيام أُخر لا تجب عليه الفدية عوض قضائه ; لأنَّ وجوب الفدية مبني على وجوب القضاء، ووجوبه مبني على تحقق القدرة عليه ، كذا حققه في «البحر»، وغيره. [٤]قوله : فدى عنه ; لقول ابن عمر : «مَن مات وعليه صيام فليطعم عنه»
And fasting of a traveler whom it does not harm is more beloved, and there is no qada if he dies in his travel or his illness, and if he becomes healthy or stays (in residence), then dies, his guardian should feed (fidya) on his behalf. [1] His saying: 'whom it does not harm' is a description of the traveler, and it is restricted by this because if fasting harms him, he is like the sick and the like, so breaking the fast is better. And what is meant by harm is hardship, not specifically bodily harm, and this is the interpretation of the hadith: 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling' reported by Muslim and others. It is established that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) saw a man under the shade of a tree with water being poured on him, and said: 'What is with your companion?' They said: 'He is fasting.' He said that statement to discourage the traveler from fasting and not accepting the concession when hardship exists. [2] His saying: 'more beloved' because the concession for fasting for the traveler is for ease and facility, and it may be that fasting with the congregation is better, especially when there is no harm, and it is witnessed by the fact that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and many of his Companions fasted in Ramadan during the journey of the Conquest of Mecca, reported by Bukhari. If breaking the fast were absolutely better, that would not have occurred. [3] His saying: 'and there is no qada...' etc. This ruling, although most scholars mention it regarding the traveler and the sick, it is not specific to them, but rather includes the pregnant woman and the nursing mother as well. In summary: a person excused by an excuse that permits breaking the fast, if he dies during his days of excuse and does not reach a number of other days, he is not obligated to pay fidya in compensation for qada because the obligation of fidya is based on the obligation of qada, and its obligation is based on the actual ability to do it. This is verified in al-Bahr and others. [4] His saying: 'should feed on his behalf' due to the saying of Ibn Umar: 'Whoever dies with fasting upon him, let food be given on his behalf.'
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on whether a traveler should fast in Ramadan and what happens if a person with missed fasts dies before making them up. The base text states that it is more beloved (mustahabb) for a traveler to fast if it does not cause him harm. The commentator explains that 'harm' means significant hardship (mashaqqa), not mere discomfort. If fasting causes hardship, then breaking the fast is better; otherwise, fasting is preferable because it is in accordance with the practice of the Prophet and many Companions during journeys. The second part deals with the consequence of death before making up missed fasts. If a traveler or sick person dies during their period of concession (i.e., before they even had a chance to make up the fasts), no qada or fidya is owed because the obligation to make up the fasts never took effect. However, if they recover or become resident and then die, fidya (feeding a poor person for each missed day) must be paid by their guardian. This ruling extends to all excused persons, including pregnant and nursing women. The proof cited is the statement of Ibn Umar that one should feed on behalf of the deceased who had fasting obligations.

Key Terms
الفدية (al-fidya): Compensation for missed obligatory fasts, usually feeding a poor person per day, when one dies before making them up.
القضاء (al-qada): Making up a missed obligatory fast on a later day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a traveler's fasting considered more beloved (ahab) according to the Hanafi school?
A) When he is on a long journey.
B) When it does not cause him harm or hardship.
C) When he is alone.
D) When it is the last ten days of Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi ruling: fasting in travel is preferred when no harm, and fidya upon death is only due if the person had the ability to perform qada' before death.

Fidyah for Missed Fasts: Calculation, Bequest, and Third of Estate
Rulings on fidyah for missed fasts with calculation method, bequest condition, and payment from third of estate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the fidyah for missed fasts when a person dies, and state the conditions for its payment from the estate, including the requirement of a bequest.

Core Idea
Fidyah is wajib, but the guardian must pay it only if the deceased bequeathed it from the third.

This lesson focuses on the Hanafi rulings on fidyah (compensation) for missed obligatory fasts when a person dies before making them up. The text from 'Umdat al-Riʿāyah explains the calculation of fidyah, the crucial condition of a bequest (īṣāʾ) for the guardian's obligation, and the rule that payment is valid from one-third of the estate. Understanding these rules ensures that the deceased's obligations are fulfilled correctly and that the guardian's actions are legally sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fidyah for missed obligatory fasts (ṣawm) when a person dies before making them up. The Hanafi school holds that fidyah is wājib upon the deceased, but the guardian is obligated to execute it only if the deceased bequeathed it.
Fidyah is a compensation, not a substitute for fasting itself; it is due for each missed day.
2
Understand the calculation method
The fidyah is calculated based on the number of days the person lived in health or residence after Ramadan, up to the number of missed days. If he lived fewer such days, fidyah is for the days he lived; if he lived enough or more, fidyah is for all missed days. The text provides an example: 10 missed days, 5 healthy days after Ramadan → fidyah for 5 days.
This ensures proportionality: the person had opportunity to make up some fasts, so those days are not compensated by fidyah.
3
Recognize the condition of bequest
The guardian is not obligated to pay fidyah unless the deceased explicitly bequeathed it (īṣāʾ). If the deceased did not bequeath, the guardian's payment is voluntary (tabarruʿ) and hoped to be accepted by Allah. The text emphasizes 'wa-shuriṭa la-hā al-īṣāʾ' (bequest is required for it).
This distinguishes Hanafi fiqh: the original obligation is on the deceased; the guardian acts as a deputy only when instructed.
4
Determine payment from the third of the estate
Fidyah, being a bequest, must be paid from the one-third (al-thuluth) of the estate after funeral expenses and debts. The right of people (debts) takes precedence over the right of Allah (fidyah). If the bequest exceeds one-third, it is only valid with the heirs' permission.
This connects to inheritance law; the third rule applies to all bequests.
5
Analyze the evidence and application
The proof is a hadith (though with weak chain) stating 'For each day, (feed) a poor person' (makānu kulli yawm miskīn). Despite its weakness, the Hanafi school uses it as support, combined with istiḥsān. The text also compares with prayer: fidyah for each missed prayer is the same as for a fast (feeding a poor person per unit).
The authority weight is ẓāhir al-riwāyah, and the correct opinion (ṣaḥīḥ) is equality between prayer and fast fidyah.

Source Text

Book of Fasting / Section on Causes of Invalidation. (It is owed) according to what he missed if he lived after it for its extent, otherwise according to both. And bequest (al-īṣāʾ) is required for it (the fidyah), and it is valid from the third (of the estate). And the fidyah of each prayer is like the fast of a day; that is the correct (opinion). [1][2] (Commentary on 'according to what he missed if he lived after it for its extent, otherwise according to both'): That is, according to the extent of health and residence. For if ten days were missed and he resided after Ramadan for five days then died, or he became healthy after Ramadan for five days then died, then upon him is the fidyah of five days. And bequest (al-īṣāʾ) is required for it, and it is valid from the third... and the fidyah of each prayer is like the fast of a day; that is the correct (opinion). [0] (The hadith:) 'For each day, (feeding of) a poor person' – narrated by al-Bayhaqī and al-Dāraquṭnī, and reported by al-Tirmidhī and Ibn Mājah with a weak chain raised to the Prophet. [1] His saying: 'after it' – i.e., after health and residence, or after Ramadan. [2] His saying: 'its extent' – i.e., according to the days whose fasting was missed. [3] His saying: 'and it is required' – in the passive voice, its pronoun referring to the fidyah, and the purpose is the obligation of fidyah. That is, the bequest is required for the obligation of fidyah upon the guardian, meaning that he (the deceased) should bequeath it before his death. For the original obligation is upon him, and its performance is upon the guardian as a deputy. So it is not obligatory upon the guardian except when instructed by the deceased. If he did not bequeath it and the guardian performs it from his own self, then it is a donation from him, hoped to be accepted by Allah's bounty and mercy. [4] His saying: 'and it is valid from the third' – i.e., from one-third of his wealth after his preparation (for burial), shrouding, and fulfilling the debts of people; because the right of the servant takes precedence over the right of Allah in its payment due to his need and Allah's self-sufficiency. And it is established in its place that the execution of bequests is only from the third. If it exceeds the third, it is not executed except with the permission of the heir. If he has no heir, it is executed from all the wealth. Its detail is in the 'Book of Bequests'. [0] His saying: 'like the fast of a day' – said in 'al-Baḥr': The gist is that whatever is a bodily act of worship, the executor feeds on his behalf after his death for every obligatory (act) like the fidyah (for fasting). For financial acts like zakāh, he pays the obligatory amount. For composite acts like hajj, he hires a man (to perform it) from the deceased's wealth.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Riʿāyah is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqāyah, expanding on rulings related to fidyah for missed fasts. The matn states three key rulings: First, the calculation of fidyah for missed fasts when a person dies before making them up. If a person had health or residence after Ramadan for a number of days equal to the missed fasts, the fidyah is calculated according to the days he lived in health/residence. If he did not live that long, the fidyah is calculated according to both the missed days and the days of health/residence. The commentator clarifies with an example: if ten days were missed and he lived five healthy days after Ramadan, fidyah is for five days. Second, a bequest (īṣāʾ) from the deceased is a condition for the guardian to be obligated to pay the fidyah. If the deceased did not bequeath, the guardian's payment is voluntary but hoped for acceptance. Third, the fidyah is paid from one-third of the estate, after burial expenses and debts, because the right of people takes precedence over the right of Allah. The passage also includes a ruling that the fidyah for a missed prayer is the same as for a day's fast – feeding a poor person for each missed unit. The evidence quoted is a hadith (though weak) about feeding a poor person for each day. The commentary further distinguishes types of acts: bodily (like fasting) require feeding on behalf; financial (like zakāh) require paying the amount; composite (like hajj) require hiring someone from the estate.

Key Terms
فدية (fidyah): Compensation for a missed obligatory fast, typically feeding a poor person for each day missed.
إيصاء (īṣāʾ): A bequest or instruction from the deceased to the guardian to pay fidyah on his behalf.
الثلث (al-thuluth): One-third of the deceased's estate, from which bequests (including fidyah) can be paid after debts and funeral expenses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The guardian must have sufficient wealth.
B) The deceased must have bequeathed it before death.
C) The missed fasts must exceed ten days.
D) The guardian must be a relative.

Key Takeaway
Fidyah for missed fasts is wajib on the deceased, but the guardian must pay it only if the deceased bequeathed it, and it is valid from the third of the estate after debts and funeral.

Making Up Missed Ramadan Fasts: Method and Fidyah
Ruling on making up missed fasts and dispute on fidyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making up missed Ramadan fasts consecutively or separately and explain why no fidyah is due when the next Ramadan arrives.

Core Idea
Making up missed fasts is flexible; no fidyah even if delayed to a second Ramadan.

When a person misses Ramadan fasts, they must make them up later. But how? This passage clarifies that the Hanafi school permits making them up either all in a row or on separate days. A bigger question arises when the next Ramadan arrives before one has finished making up: should one pay fidyah as a penalty? The Hanafi school says no, and we'll see why, especially in contrast with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on the method of qada
The matn says 'yaqḍī Ramaḍān waṣlan wa faṣlan' – one may make up the missed fasts consecutively or separately. Identify this as a permissive ruling (jawāz).
Consecutive means fasting day after day; separated means with breaks in between.
2
Find the dalīl for permissibility
The commentator cites Quran 2:184 'fa-ʿiddatun min ayyāmin ukhar' (a number of other days) – the word 'ukhar' (other) does not specify continuity, hence both methods are allowed. Also a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ was asked and replied 'If he wishes, he may do them consecutively; if he wishes, he may do them separately.'
The hadith is reported by al-Dāraquṭnī and al-Bayhaqī.
3
Identify the scenario of a second Ramadan
The matn then says: 'fa-in jāʾa Ramaḍān ākhar ṣāmahu, thumma qaḍā al-awwal bi-lā fidyah' – if another Ramadan comes, one fasts it, then makes up the first without fidyah. This is the second ruling.
The current Ramadan takes precedence over making up the previous one.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
The commentator notes that al-Shafi'i holds fidyah is obligatory (wājib) for each delayed day. This is the core dispute.
The Shafi'i view relies on a hadith from Abū Hurayrah reported as marfūʿ.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: the hadith is not sound (lā yathbut); imposing fidyah is against qiyās because delaying qaḍā is inherently permissible, like delaying a missed prayer. Therefore, the Hanafi position stands.
The commentator also mentions that even Muḥammad ibn Muqātil first held fidyah but later retracted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقضي رمضان وصلاً وفصلاً ، فإن جاءَ رمضان آخر صامه ، ثُمَّ قضى الأول بلا فدية ، وعند الشافعي تجب الفدية . [1]قوله : فدية ؛ هو قول محمد بن مقاتل له أولاً ثم رجع عنه. كذا في «السراج». [2] قوله : وصلاً وفصلاً ؛ أي يجوز أن يقضي تتابعاً أو مع تفارق، لظاهر قوله ال: ﴿ فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَامٍ أُخَرَ ﴾ (البقرة: 184)، وقد سُئل رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عنه، فقال: «إن شاء تابعه، وإن شاء فرقه» (أخرجه الدارقطني والبيهقي). [3]قوله: تجب الفدية ؛ هو المروي عن أبي هريرة له عند الدارقطني وغيره، وروي مرفوعاً بسند لا يثبت، ونحن نقول : وجوب الفدية مما لا معنى له، فإن تأخير القضاء جائز ليس له حد كتأخير قضاء صلوة إلى وقت آخر، ووجوب الفدية على خلاف القياس ، فلا يحكم به ما لم يثبت ذلك بسنة مرفوعة صحيحة.
And he makes up Ramadan consecutively or separately. Then if another Ramadan comes, he fasts it, then makes up the first (missed) without fidyah. And according to al-Shafi'i, fidyah is obligatory. [1] His saying: 'fidyah' – this is the opinion of Muhammad ibn Muqātil first, then he retracted from it. It is mentioned in 'al-Sirāj'. [2] His saying: 'consecutively or separately' – i.e., it is permissible to make them up continuously or with intervals, due to the apparent meaning of His saying: 'then a number from other days' (Quran 2:184), and the Messenger of Allah ﷺ was asked about it, so he said: 'If he wishes, he may do them consecutively; if he wishes, he may do them separately.' (Narrated by al-Dāraquṭnī and al-Bayhaqī). [3] His saying: 'fidyah is obligatory' – this is narrated from Abū Hurayrah at al-Dāraquṭnī and others, and it is reported as marfūʿ with a chain that is not established. And we say: the obligation of fidyah has no meaning, because delaying the make-up is permissible without a limit, like delaying the make-up of a prayer to another time. And the obligation of fidyah is contrary to analogy, so it is not ruled by it unless it is established by a sound marfūʿ sunnah.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on making up missed Ramadan fasts. The matn states that one may make them up either consecutively (waṣlan) or separately (faṣlan). The commentator explains that both are permissible, citing the Quranic verse 'then a number from other days' which does not specify continuity, and a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ was asked and replied that one may choose. The second part addresses a scenario: if another Ramadan arrives before one has made up the previous year's missed fasts, one should fast the current Ramadan first, then make up the previous year's missed fasts later, and no fidyah is required (contra al-Shafi'i). The commentator refutes the Shafi'i view by noting that delaying the make-up is inherently permissible without a fixed limit, similar to delayed prayers, so fidyah cannot be imposed unless proven by a sound hadith — which is lacking. He also notes that the opinion of fidyah was held by Muhammad ibn Muqātil but later retracted. This is a clear inter-madhhab dispute.

Key Terms
وصلاً وفصلاً (waṣlan wa faṣlan): Making up missed fasts either consecutively (waṣlan) or with intervals (faṣlan).
فدية (fidyah): Financial compensation for missed fasts, here contested for delay beyond one Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Fast the current Ramadan and then make up the missed fasts without fidyah.
C) Only fast the missed fasts after the current Ramadan without fidyah.
D) Pay fidyah and not fast the missed fasts.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, making up missed fasts is flexible in timing and carries no fidyah even if delayed to a second Ramadan.

Guardian Cannot Perform Bodily Acts of Worship for Deceased
Ruling with proof: prohibition of proxy fasting and praying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on proxy fasting and praying for the deceased and identify the evidence (qawl sahabi) that supports it.

Core Idea
No one can fast or pray on behalf of another — deputyship is invalid in purely bodily acts.

This short passage from a Hanafi commentary establishes a clear boundary: the acts of fasting and prayer are so personal that no one else can perform them on behalf of a deceased person. The author anchors this ruling in a companion's statement and a general legal principle. Let's unpack the evidence and reasoning behind this prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What specific act is being ruled upon? The guardian's attempt to fast or pray on behalf of the deceased. The ruling is that it is not valid (la yajuz).
The act is proxy performance of a bodily worship.
2
Locate the evidence
What proof is cited? The saying of Ibn Abbas: 'No one fasts on behalf of another, and no one prays on behalf of another' (narrated by al-Nasa'i). Also a similar saying from Ibn Umar.
This is qawl sahabi, a companion's statement, considered a proof in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this saying prove the ruling? The explicit negation with general wording ('لا') indicates prohibition. The phrase 'عن أحد' means 'on behalf of anyone', making it general.
In Hanafi usul, the imperative form indicates obligation; here the negation indicates prohibition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Is this ruling agreed upon? No, the Shafi'i school allows proxy fasting and praying for the deceased under certain circumstances (e.g., if the deceased bequeathed it).
This difference stems from differing assessments of the evidence.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Why do Hanafis adopt this position? The explicit companion statement, combined with the general principle that niyaba does not apply to purely bodily acts, supports the ruling.
The principle is that bodily acts require personal intention and effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصوم ولا يُصلّى عنه وليه [٤] قوله : وليه ؛ لأنَّ النِّيابةَ لا تجري في العباداتِ البدنية المحضة، ويشهده قولُ ابن عباس : «لا يصوم أحد عن أحد، ولا يصلي أحد عن أحد» (أخرجه النسائي في «سننه الكبرى»، ومثله عن ابن عمر) .
And his guardian does not fast or pray on his behalf. [4] His saying: 'his guardian' – because deputyship (niyāba) does not apply to purely bodily acts of worship. And this is witnessed by the saying of Ibn ʿAbbās: 'No one fasts on behalf of another, and no one prays on behalf of another' (narrated by al-Nasāʾī in his 'Sunan al-Kubrā'), and similar from Ibn ʿUmar.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage establishes a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh: deputyship (niyāba) is not valid for purely bodily acts of worship (ʿibādāt badaniyya maḥḍa). This means that a guardian or any other person cannot perform fasting or prayer on behalf of a deceased person. The evidence cited is the statement of Ibn ʿAbbās (a companion) that no one fasts or prays for another, which is narrated by al-Nasāʾī with a sound chain (as noted in the commentary). Similarly, Ibn ʿUmar held the same view. This distinguishes bodily acts from financial acts (like zakāt) or composite acts (like hajj), where deputyship is allowed. Therefore, if a person dies with missed fasts or prayers, the guardian does not perform them; instead, fidyah is paid (as discussed in previous lessons) if the deceased bequeathed it. This ruling is consistent across the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
نيابة (niyāba): Deputyship or proxy – the act of one person performing an obligation on behalf of another.
عبادات بدنية محضة (ʿibādāt badaniyya maḥḍa): Purely bodily acts of worship that involve physical actions only, like fasting and prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Proxy fasting and praying for the deceased are invalid in Hanafi fiqh; the obligation is not transferable.

Qada of Ramadan Fasts: Flexibility and Refutation of Fidyah
Expands ruling on qada of Ramadan fasts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consecutive vs. separate qada, explain why no fidyah is due for delay, and refute the Shafi'i position using the Hanafi proof.

Core Idea
Qada of Ramadan fasts is flexible; no fidyah for delay; proxy fasting is invalid.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the commentator expands on the matn of al-Wiqaya regarding making up missed Ramadan fasts. The Hanafi position is clear: qada can be done consecutively or separately, and delaying it past the next Ramadan incurs no fidyah. This lesson will walk you through the evidence and the refutation of the Shafi'i view that fidyah becomes obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is qada (making up) of missed Ramadan fasts. The ruling is that it is wajib, but the manner is flexible: it may be performed consecutively (waslan) or separately (faslan).
The flexibility is derived from the general Quranic command 'several other days' and a hadith.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof for flexibility is two-fold: (1) Quran 2:184 'fa-'iddatun min ayyamin ukhar' (a number of other days), which does not specify sequence; (2) a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'If he wishes, he may do it consecutively, and if he wishes, he may separate them' (al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi).
The hadith is cited as evidence that both methods are permissible.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Quranic verse uses a general expression ('a number of other days') without any condition of contiguity. The hadith explicitly permits separation. Thus, the Hanafi rule is that qada is not time-bound in terms of sequence; it can be delayed indefinitely.
The absence of a deadline means that delaying qada does not entail any penalty like fidyah.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that if qada is delayed past the next Ramadan, fidyah becomes obligatory for each day. Their evidence includes a report from Abu Hurayra and a marfu' hadith (though the commentator notes it is weak).
The opposing school's proof is based on a non-mutawatir transmission.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) The marfu' hadith is not authentic (la yathbut); (2) Imposing fidyah is against qiyas (analogical reasoning) because qada of prayers is also flexible without fidyah, and fasts are analogous. Therefore, without a sound proof, fidyah is not imposed.
The commentator also notes that Muhammad ibn Muqatil initially held the fidyah view but retracted, reinforcing the standard Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقضي رمضان وصلاً وفصلاً، فإن جاء رمضان آخر صامه، ثم قضى الأول بلا فدية، ولا يصوم ولا يُصلّى عنه وليه. وعند البعض فدية صلاة يوم واحد كفدية صوم يوم.
(ويقضي رمضان وصلاً وفصلاً، فإن جاء رمضان آخر صامه، ثم قضى الأول بلا فدية)، وعند الشافعي تجب الفدية، (ولا يصوم ولا يُصلّى عنه وليه).
[1] قوله: فدية؛ هو قول محمد بن مقاتل له أولاً ثم رجع عنه. كذا في «السراج».
[2] قوله: وصلاً وفصلاً؛ أي يجوز أن يقضي تتابعاً أو مع تفارق، لظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ﴾، وقد سئل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عنه، فقال: «إن شاء تابعه، وإن شاء فرقه»، أخرجه الدارقطني والبيهقي.
[3] قوله: تجب الفدية؛ هو المروي عن أبي هريرة عند الدارقطني وغيره، وروي مرفوعاً بسند لا يثبت، ونحن نقول: وجوب الفدية مما لا معنى له، فإن تأخير القضاء جائز ليس له حد كتأخير قضاء صلاة إلى وقت آخر، ووجوب الفدية على خلاف القياس، فلا يحكم به ما لم يثبت ذلك بسنة مرفوعة صحيحة.
[4] قوله: وليه؛ لأن النيابة لا تجري في العبادات البدنية المحضة، ويشهده قول ابن عباس: «لا يصوم أحد عن أحد، ولا يصلي أحد عن أحد» أخرجه النسائي في «سننه الكبرى»، ومثله عن ابن عمر أخرجه عبد الرزاق.
And he makes up Ramadan consecutively and separately. If the next Ramadan comes, he fasts it, then makes up the first without fidyah. And his guardian does not fast or pray on his behalf. And according to some, the fidyah of one day's prayer is like the fidyah of one day's fast. (He makes up Ramadan consecutively and separately, if the next Ramadan comes, he fasts it, then makes up the first without fidyah), and according to al-Shafi'i, fidyah is obligatory, (and his guardian does not fast or pray on his behalf). [1] He said: fidyah; this is the saying of Muhammad ibn Muqatil initially then he retracted. [2] He said: consecutively and separately; meaning it is permissible to make up consecutively or with intervals, due to the apparent meaning of His statement: 'then a number of other days', and the Messenger of Allah was asked about it, and he said: 'If he wishes, he may do it consecutively, and if he wishes, he may separate them', reported by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi. [3] He said: fidyah is obligatory; this is reported from Abu Hurayra by al-Daraqutni and others, and narrated as marfu' with a chain that is not established. And we say: obligatory fidyah has no meaning, because delaying the qada is permissible without a time limit, like delaying the qada of a prayer to another time, and obligatory fidyah is against qiyas, so it is not judged unless established by a sound marfu' sunnah. [4] He said: his guardian; because deputyship does not apply in purely bodily acts of worship, and this is witnessed by the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'No one fasts on behalf of another, and no one prays on behalf of another', reported by al-Nasa'i in his Sunan al-Kubra, and similar from Ibn 'Umar reported by Abd al-Razzaq.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqaya, expanding on the ruling of qada (making up) missed Ramadan fasts. The matn states that qada can be performed consecutively (waslan) or separately (faslan), reflecting the Hanafi position of flexibility based on the general Quranic command 'several other days' and a hadith allowing either method. If the next Ramadan arrives before qada is completed, one fasts the current Ramadan first, then makes up the previous one without any fidyah (compensatory payment). This directly opposes the Shafi'i view that fidyah becomes due if qada is delayed past the next Ramadan. The commentator refutes the Shafi'i stance by noting that the hadith marfu' about fidyah is weak (ghayr thabit), and that delaying qada is inherently permissible without a fixed deadline, analogous to delaying makeup prayers. Thus, imposing fidyah contradicts qiyas (analogical reasoning) and requires a sound proof. The second part clarifies that a guardian (wali) cannot fast or pray on behalf of a deceased person, because purely bodily acts of worship (ibadat badaniyya mahda) cannot be delegated. This is supported by Ibn Abbas's statement and the practice of Ibn Umar. The Hanafi school therefore interprets the hadith 'whoever dies with fasts due, his guardian should fast on his behalf' as referring to feeding (it'am) instead, as mentioned in al-Binaya.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed obligatory act of worship, here specifically fasting.
فدية (fidyah): Compensatory payment (feeding a poor person) for a missed or invalidated fast.
ولي (wali): Guardian or relative responsible for the deceased person's affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person has missed Ramadan fasts and the next Ramadan arrives before they are made up, what should they do?
A) Fast the current Ramadan, then make up the previous fasts with fidyah for each day.
B) Fast the current Ramadan, then make up the previous fasts without fidyah.
C) Skip the current Ramadan and make up the previous fasts first.
D) Pay fidyah and not fast the current Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
Qada of Ramadan fasts may be done separately or together; no fidyah is due for delay; proxy fasting is invalid.

The Obligation to Complete a Voluntary Fast and Qada upon Breaking
Ruling on the binding nature of voluntary fasts and qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a started voluntary fast becomes wajib to complete and requires qada if broken, and identify the Quranic and hadith evidence supporting this.

Core Idea
A voluntary fast becomes wajib to complete once started; if broken, qada is owed.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi principle: that a voluntary act of worship, once initiated, becomes binding for completion. We will explore the textual evidence from the Quran and Sunnah, and see how the Hanafi school defends this ruling against an opposing view attributed to Imam Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is starting a voluntary (nafl) fast. The ruling: it becomes binding (wajib) to complete, and if broken, qada (make-up) is required. Note the distinction: the initial initiation is not obligatory, but the completion becomes so.
The commentator clarifies 'luzum bi al-baqa' not 'bi al-ibtida'.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites two Quranic verses: 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (47:33) and 'Complete the standing up to the night' (22:29 paraphrase?), and the hadith of Aisha and Hafsa where the Prophet ﷺ said 'اقضيا يوماً مكانه' (Make up a day in its place).
The hadith is reported by multiple chains.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'اقضيا' indicates obligation (wujub) of qada. The Quranic verses imply that once a worship is started, it must be completed and not invalidated.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik reportedly said in the Muwatta': 'I have not heard that any of them ordered anyone to fast on behalf of another', implying qada might not be required. The Hanafis respond by interpreting the hadith about fasting for the deceased as referring to feeding (it'am), not actual fasting.
This shows how the school handles apparent contradictory evidence.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi because the hadith of Aisha and Hafsa is explicit and direct, while the opposing view is based on absence of a report. The commentator affirms the Hanafi position as the relied-upon one.
Cross-madhhab status: mukhtalaf fih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزم صوم نفل شرع فيه أداءً وقضاءً: أي يجب عليه إتمامه، فإن أفسد فعليه القضاء.
وقال مالك في «موطئه»: لم أسمع أن أحداً منهم أمر أحداً أن يصوم عن أحد، ولا أحد. وبناءً على هذا حمل أصحابنا حديث: «من مات وعليه صيام صام عنه وليه» أخرجه البخاري على الإطعام القائم مقام الصوم. كذا في «البناية».
[1] قوله: نفل؛ المراد به ما ليس بفرض ولا واجب، فيشمل السنة.
[2] قوله: يجب عليه؛ إشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد بلزوم الأداء لزومه ابتداء بل بقاء.
[3] قوله: إتمامه؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ولا تبطلوا أعمالكم﴾، وقوله: ﴿أتموا القيام إلى الليل﴾.
[4] قوله: أفسد؛ بعذر أو بغير عذر، وكذا إذا فسد بعروض حيض. كذا في «النهاية».
[0] قوله: فعليه القضاء؛ لما روي أن عائشة وحفصة رضي الله عنهما كانتا صائمتين تطوعاً فأهدي لهما طعام فأفطرتا، فقال لهما رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «اقضيا يوماً مكانه» أخرجه مالك والنسائي والترمذي وغيرهم بطرق متعددة يشد بعضها بعضاً.
And a voluntary fast that one enters into is binding for performance and qada: meaning he must complete it, and if he breaks it, then qada is upon him. And Malik said in his Muwatta': 'I have not heard that any of them ordered anyone to fast on behalf of another, nor anyone.' And based on this, our companions interpreted the hadith: 'Whoever dies with fasts due, his guardian should fast on his behalf' reported by al-Bukhari, as referring to feeding (which stands in place of fasting). Thus in al-Binayah. [1] He said: nafl; intended is that which is not fard nor wajib, so it includes sunnah. [2] He said: binding upon him; indicating that the meaning is not the initial obligation of performance but rather the obligation of completion. [3] He said: completing it; due to His statement: 'And do not invalidate your deeds' and His statement: 'Complete the standing up to the night.' [4] He said: he breaks it; with an excuse or without an excuse, and likewise if it breaks due to the onset of menstruation. Thus in al-Nihayah. [0] He said: then qada is upon him; because it is reported that Aisha and Hafsah, may Allah be pleased with them, were fasting voluntarily, and food was gifted to them, so they broke their fast, and the Messenger of Allah said to them: 'Make up a day in its place', reported by Malik, al-Nasa'i, al-Tirmidhi and others through multiple chains that support each other.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Hanafi ruling on voluntary (nafl) fasts. The matn states that once a person begins a voluntary fast, it becomes binding (lazim) to complete it, and if broken, qada (making it up) is required. The commentator distinguishes: the initial act of starting a nafl fast is not obligatory, but the obligation of completion kicks in after starting (luzum bi al-baqa' not bi al-ibtida'). This is based on Quranic verses prohibiting the invalidation of deeds and commanding completion of acts of worship. Further, a hadith about Aisha and Hafsah confirms that qada is due even for voluntary fasts broken intentionally. The commentary also addresses an alternative view from Malik and how the Hanafis interpret the hadith about a guardian fasting for a deceased person: they hold that it refers to feeding (it'am) instead, consistent with the principle that no one can physically perform acts of worship for another. Additionally, the ruling applies whether the fast is broken with or without a valid excuse, including the onset of menstruation, which also necessitates qada.

Key Terms
نفل (nafl): Voluntary act of worship beyond what is obligatory; includes sunnah and nafl proper.
لزوم (luzum): Binding obligation; here it means the fast becomes obligatory to complete after starting.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed or broken fast; here for voluntary fasts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone starts a voluntary (nafl) fast and then breaks it without a valid excuse, what is required?
A) Nothing; voluntary fasts can be broken anytime.
B) They must complete the fast that day.
C) They must make up that day with another fast (qada).
D) They must give charity as expiation.

Key Takeaway
A voluntary fast, once begun, becomes wajib to complete; breaking it requires qada, based on the hadith of Aisha and Hafsa.

Supererogatory Fast Becomes Binding Once Started
Ruling and proof that nafl fast becomes binding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafl fast, explain its basis in Quran and hadith, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Maliki school.

Core Idea
Starting a nafl fast makes it wajib to complete; breaking it requires qada.

The Hanafi school holds a unique position on supererogatory acts: once initiated, they become obligatory to complete. This lesson examines the ruling for nafl fast, its evidence from the Qur'an and hadith, and the disagreement with Imam Malik who does not require qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states that a nafl fast, once started, is binding (lāzim) both in performance and in making up. The commentary clarifies that 'binding' means obligation to complete (wujūb al-itmām), not initial obligation.
The ruling is that if one breaks a nafl fast, qada is required.
2
Locate the primary dalīl
Two Quranic verses are cited: 'And do not invalidate your deeds' (47:33) and 'Complete the fast to the night' (2:187). The imperative 'atimmū' (complete) indicates obligation, and the prohibition 'lā tubṭilū' shows that invalidating deeds is prohibited.
These verses are general in scope and apply to all obligatory and supererogatory acts.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith of ʿĀʾishah and Ḥafṣah (may Allah be pleased with them) is quoted: they were fasting voluntarily, broke their fast when given food, and the Prophet ﷺ ordered them to 'make up a day in its place'. This is transmitted by Mālik, al-Nasāʾī, al-Tirmidhī and others, strengthening its authenticity.
The hadith directly proves that qada is required for a broken nafl fast.
4
Note the ikhtilāf with Mālik
Imam Mālik in his Muwaṭṭaʾ states he has not heard of any Companion ordering qada for a broken nafl fast. He interprets the hadith of fasting on behalf of a deceased as referring to feeding the poor. The Hanafīs respond that the hadith of ʿĀʾishah and Ḥafṣah is authentic and directly commands qada.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) is that according to Mālik, no qada is needed; in the Hanafī school, it is required.
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ
The Hanafī reliance on the authentic hadith, supported by the general Quranic command to complete the fast and not invalidate deeds, establishes the position that qada is required. This is the muftā bihī (operative ruling) in the school.
The authority weight is ẓāhir al-riwāyah, from the six core books of Imām Muḥammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم/باب موجب الإفساد
ويلزم صوم نفل شرع فيه أداءً، وقضاءً : أي يجب عليه إتمامه، فإن أفسد فعليه القضاء. وقال مالك في «موطئه»: «لم أسمع أنَّ أحداً منهم أمر أحداً أن يصوم عن أحد، ولا أحد)، وبناءً على هذا حمل أصحابنا حديث: «مَن مات وعليه صيام صام عنه وليه ) ، أخرجه البخاري على الإطعام القائم مقام الصوم. كذا في «البناية». قوله : نفل ; المراد به ما ليس بفرض ولا واجب، فيشمل السنة. قوله : يجب عليه ; إشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد بلزوم الأداء لزومه ابتداء بل بقاء. قوله : إتمامه ; لقوله تعالى : ولا تبطلوا أَعْمَلَكُم ، وقوله : أتموا القِيَامَ إِلى اللَّيْلِ. قوله : أفسد ; بعذر أو بغير عذر، وكذا إذا فسد بعروض حيض. كذا في «النهاية». قوله : فعليه القضاء ; لما روي أنَّ عائشةَ وحفصة رضين الله عنهما كانتا صائمتين تطوعاً فأهدي لهما طعامٌ فأفطرتا ، فقال لهما رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «اقضيا يوماً مكانه ، أخرجه مالك والنسائي والترمذي وغيرهم بطرق متعددة يشد بعضها بعضاً.
Book of Fasting / Chapter on Causes of Invalidating. And a supererogatory fast in which one has started is binding, both in performance and in making up: i.e., completing it is obligatory upon him, and if he invalidates it, then qada is upon him. And Malik said in his Muwatta': 'I have not heard that any of them ordered anyone to fast on behalf of another, nor one...' and based on this, our companions interpreted the hadith: 'Whoever dies with a fast due, his close relative should fast for him' narrated by Bukhari as referring to feeding which stands in place of fasting. As in al-Binaya. His saying: 'nafl' means what is not fard or wajib, so it includes sunnah. His saying: 'obligatory upon him' indicates that what is meant by binding in performance is not initial obligation but remaining obligation. His saying: 'complete it' due to His saying: 'And do not invalidate your deeds' and His saying: 'Complete the fast to the night.' His saying: 'invalidates' either with an excuse or without, and likewise if it is invalidated by the onset of menstruation. As in al-Nihaya. His saying: 'then qada is upon him' due to what was narrated that Aisha and Hafsa were fasting voluntarily and food was gifted to them, so they broke their fast, then the Messenger of Allah said to them: 'Make up a day in its place' narrated by Malik, Nasa'i, Tirmidhi and others via multiple chains that strengthen each other.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on supererogatory fasts. The matn states that starting a nafl fast makes it binding to complete, and if broken, qada is required. The commentary explains that 'binding' means during the fast, not initially. The evidence is from Quran (don't invalidate deeds) and the hadith of Aisha and Hafsa. The Hanafis also address Malik's view that no one can fast for another, but they interpret the hadith of fasting for the deceased as referring to feeding. The commentary clarifies that 'nafl' includes all non-obligatory fasts, including sunnah. Invalidating occurs with or without excuse, including menstruation. The hadith is quoted as proof for qada. This is the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah).

Key Terms
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory act of worship, not fard or wajib initially.
قضاء (qada): Making up for a broken or missed fast by fasting another day.
إتمام (itmam): Completion of the fast from start to end.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone starts a nafl fast and then breaks it without an excuse, what must they do?
A) Nothing, because it was voluntary.
B) Make up the day with qada.
C) Feed sixty poor persons.
D) Seek forgiveness but no qada.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a supererogatory fast becomes binding upon initiation, and breaking it obligates qada (making up the day).
